December 14, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Abisag Ayala, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rubab Z. Razvi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 29, 2009, decision to deny his untimely motion to reopen. On January 7, 2009, the final administrative order in the respondent’s removal proceeding was entered by the Immigration Judge. On April 16, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. On April 29, 2009, the Immigration Judge denied his motion because the respondent failed to timely file and failed to adequately support his application for cancellation of removal with proper documentation.
 
“¿Aº party may file only one motion to reopen deportation or exclusion proceedings (whether before the Board of the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent did not file his motion to reopen within 90 days of the final administrative decision, nor did he file it on or before September 30, 1996. As such, we agree with the Immigration Judge that his motion to reopen the final administrative order is time-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time limitations for motions to reopen during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002). The respondent has argued that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
We note that the respondent failed to meet any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and did not show that he had been prejudiced in his prior proceedings. The steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, include, inter alia, that the motion reflect 1) the agreement with counsel; (2) that prior counsel has been notified and afforded an opportunity to respond; and 3) whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Despite the respondent statement that he has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, the record does not support his position. The respondent has not explained the agreement he had with counsel, given counsel notice of the ineffective assistance and an opportunity to respond, nor has the respondent filed any complaint with the appropriate authorities or explained why he failed to do so. See Respondent’s Attachments to Brief on Appeal. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
*2 Moreover, an alien must establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought before a motion to reopen will be granted. See, e.g., INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent did not submit the required documentation to support his prior motion to reopen. Furthermore, the respondent has not filed such documentation on appeal. In the absence of such, the Board cannot consider the respondent’s request for reopening to apply for cancellation of removal as he had not demonstrated statutory eligibility for this relief. Matter of K-L-, 20 I&N Dec. 654, 659 (BIA 1993). Therefore, the respondent has also not demonstrated prejudice from the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (must show the outcome might have been affected had the negligent representation not occurred).
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: VICTOR ANTONIO ESTRADA-GARCIA A.K.A. VICTOR GARCIA
File: AXXX XX8 158 - Memphis, TN
December 14, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on July 22, 2009. The appeal was accordingly due on or before August 21, 2009. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on November 19, 2009. We accordingly find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal the respondent has filed a Motion to File Late Appeal. Respondent states that his former counsel neglected to file his appeal on his behalf. However, he provides no evidence in support of this claim and does not comply with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the motion to accept the late filed appeal is denied. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15. The record before us indicates that the respondent may have waived appeal. In view of our disposition of this case, we need not determine whether the respondent made a knowing and intelligent waiver in this case.
 
We note that because we are dismissing the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) & (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) & C of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) & (B) and (c)(7)(A) & C; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
David B. Holmes

2009 WL 5252791 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO MAURICIO RODRIGUEZ-BRITO A.K.A. CARLOS RODRIGUEZ A.K.A. ARTHUR RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX2 326 - New York, NY
December 11, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
S. Bernard Schwarz, Esquire
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
On February 18, 2005, with the respondent represented by Victor A. Schiano, the Board affirmed a decision of an Immigration Judge finding the respondent, who is from Ecuador, undeserving of relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The Board found, after considering the respondent’s “extensive criminal history that ranged from 1985 through 1992 that resulted in sentences to extensive periods of confinement”, that his equities did not outweigh the negative factors in the respondent’s case. The Board declined to reconsider this decision, on June 27, 2005, with the respondent represented by Donald F. Madeo. The Second Circuit Court of Appeals upheld the Board’s February 18, 2005, decision in an unpublished order dated April 26, 2007. Rodriguez-Brito v. Gonzales, 223 Fed.Appx. 55, 2007 WL 1233594 (2d Cir. April 26, 2007)(unpublished). The respondent was represented by H. Raymond Fasano, who is associated with Madeo.
 
On September 25, 2007, represented by David W. Guy, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, claiming that he had received ineffective assistance of counsel from Dina Fleissig, an attorney associated with Schiano, who appeared with the respondent at the removal hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on October 4, 2007. The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on April 21, 2009. The Board found, citing Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974), that the respondent was required to file his motion with the Board and the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction to adjudicate the motion, inasmuch as the Board issued the final administrative decision on the merits in this case on February 18, 2005.
 
The respondent, now represented by S. Bernard Schwarz, filed an untimely motion to reopen with the Board, on May 14, 2009. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion brings a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Schiano, Fleissig, and Guy.
 
The respondent provides an inadequate reason for the lateness of the motion. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131 (2d Cir. 2008) (“no matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence in pursuing his claim”, citing Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 171 (2d Cir. 2006)); Wang v. Board of Immigration Appeals, 508 F.3d 710 (2d Cir. 2007)(diligence must be shown by respondent wishing to pursue an untimely motion). The respondent brings claims of ineffective assistance of counsel against Schiano and Fleissig, who represented him before the Immigration Judge at the removal hearing, and on appeal to the Board on February 18, 2005. The respondent also complains that attorney Guy provided ineffective assistance of counsel, by improperly filing the motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on September 25, 2007.
 
*2 The motion neglects to mention, however, that at least between March 21, 2005 (when a motion to reconsider was filed with the Board) and July 19, 2007 (when the Second Circuit denied a petition for rehearing), the respondent was represented by the law firm of Madeo & Fasano, and its attorneys, H. Raymond Fasano and Donald F. Madeo. The pending motion fails to explain why any claim of ineffective assistance of counsel could not have been presented by these attorneys during this time period of over 2 years. No claim is made that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel from these attorneys.
 
Thus, the respondent fails to show diligence that would justify tolling the motions deadline for such a long period of time. Reopening under the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a) is reserved for exceptional situations, which have not been demonstrated here. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The motion to reopen will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

APPEAL

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude (sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation (sustained)
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (not sustained)
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 25, 2008, decision. In that decision, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1996), abandoned. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
At the October 12, 2005, master calendar hearing, the Immigration Judge reset the hearing for October 30, 2006, so that the respondent could submit his application for relief (Tr. at 4-8). On October 18, 2006, the respondent’s counsel filed a Motion to Withdraw Representation as Attorney, which was granted by the Immigration Judge. For reasons not set forth in the record, the hearing was again reset for July 25, 2008. At the July 25, 2008, hearing, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance and deemed his application for relief abandoned because it had not been filed. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 25, 2008, decision. On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance. Specifically, the respondent argues that his prior counsel failed to inform him of his obligation to file his application for relief.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is considered to have abandoned his claim because he failed to timely file his application for relief from removal (I.J. at 3-4). An Immigration Judge has broad discretion to conduct removal proceedings and to set time limits and extensions for filing applications. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). In this case, the respondent’s counsel was advised by the Immigration Judge that the application for relief was required to be filed at the next hearing (Tr. at 6). Moreover, as shown above, proceedings in this matter had been delayed to allow the respondent ample time to prepare for his merits hearing. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 350 (BIA 1982) (holding that an Immigration Judge has a duty to determine removability in an expeditious manner and to avoid dilatory tactics designed to delay removal proceedings). To the extent that the respondent alleges that his former counsel engaged in ineffective assistance, we note that the respondent has not attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See Voluntary Departure: Effect of a Motion To Reopen or Reconsider or a Petition for Review, 73 Fed. Reg. 76,927, 937-38 (Dec. 18, 2008) (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c)(3)(iii), (e)(1)).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 73 Fed. Reg. At 76938 (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i)).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS AGUIRRE TORRES
File: AXXX XX9 634 - Atlanta, GA
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria M. Odom, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley Fortson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a 37-year-old native and citizen of Mexico, appealed the Immigration Judge’s September 19, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent’s attorney’s motion to withdraw was granted on February 26, 2007. On March 20, 2007, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable, as charged, and that he had abandoned any applications for relief. Through a new attorney, he filed a motion to reopen on June 13, 2007, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal was remanded by the Board on May 22, 2008, to the Immigration Court so that the record file could be located. The record was located and returned to the Board on August 19, 2009. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was admitted to the United States on or about December 1, 1990, as a lawful permanent resident. He married a citizen of Mexico on February 24, 2002, in Mexico. They have five United States citizen children ages 19, 16, 11, 10 and 6 years old. He was convicted in Cobb County, Georgia, on or about June 3, 1997, on a plea of guilty of carrying a concealed weapon. He was fined and given a 12 month suspended sentence. The Notice to Appear was issued on April 11, 2005, at the border after he had made a trip to Mexico. His request for a change of venue from San Antonio, Texas, to Atlanta, Georgia, was granted on March 8, 2006. The respondent contended in his motion to reopen that his original counsel was ineffective by not pursuing his application under 240A(a) of the Act, and that the record should be reopened and remanded. The Immigration Judge found no ineffective assistance of counsel had been proven, and that the motion should be denied.
 
The respondent has not complied substantially with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He submitted a copy of the agreement for representation, which was limited to the initial appearance, and he has informed prior counsel that he intended to make a claim of ineffective assistance to the Board. Former counsel has responded. However, the respondent did not file any complaint with the relevant authorities. He explains in his brief that the situation, in his new counsel’s opinion, does not warrant a bar complaint. If the respondent does not believe a bar complaint is warranted, he also cannot claim that the actions of his former counsel rise to the level of ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 Moreover, ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding is a denial of due process only “¿iºf the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from presenting his case.” Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491, 499-500 (9th Cir. 1986) (quoting Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). See also Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent must show that the conduct of former counsel was so egregious that it rendered his hearing unfair. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998). The respondent stated in his affidavit that he was unable to pay former counsel the amount counsel wanted to continue his representation and file the application. He implies that the amount demanded was excessive, but he does not believe it merits the attention of the state bar. He also states that counsel apparently did not inform the Immigration Court of his new address, because the judge’s order was sent to his old address in North Carolina. Counsel’s motion to withdraw informs the court of the respondent’s new address in Georgia. He does not state that the use of his former address by the Immigration Court caused him any prejudice. We note that the respondent admits his former attorney advised him to attend his hearing scheduled for March 23, 2007, and to request additional time to find a new attorney. He did not appear at that time, even though he could not yet have received the Immigration Judge’s order of removal. We find that there has been no showing that the respondent was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s performance. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005). In these circumstances, we find the respondent’s due process rights have not been violated.
 
The respondent complains that the Immigration Judge did not consider him for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), but he has not made a prima facia case of eligibility. Primarily, he has not filed an affidavit indicating that he is able and willing to post bond and to leave the United States, if so ordered. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(3); 1240.26(c)(iv).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BO WANG
File: AXXX XX3 577 - Los Angeles, CA
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas J. Mayer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lily C. Hsu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of the People’s Republic of China. She was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on December 6, 2007, after failing to appear for a hearing. She filed a motion to reopen on February 25, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied it on April 21, 2008, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that an “exceptional circumstance” prevented her from appearing at the hearing. She indicates that the Immigration Judge misconstrued her motion as contending that she sought reopening based on a failure of notice. The record contains the respondent’s statement. Therein, she admits that she received the Notice to Appear that informed her that her hearing was scheduled for February 7, 2008. She further indicates that she hired a Mr. Dong from New Arrival Immigration Services. According to the respondent, Mr. Dong failed to inform her that the Immigration Court had changed her hearing from February 7, 2008, to December 6, 2007. The respondent further states that Mr. Dong later informed her of the order of removal that was entered in absentia, and he advised the respondent to find a medical doctor who would falsely attest that the respondent failed to attend her hearing on account of illness. The respondent further argues that Mr. Dong’s overall representation was unprofessional, and he is not authorized to practice law. She asks the Board to reopen these proceedings because she was the victim of a negligent representative.
 
An alien’s failure to appear for a hearing caused by ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute an “exceptional circumstance” warranting reopening of a proceedings. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003). The Ninth Circuit also has found ineffective assistance of counsel to exist if it is established that an alien suffered prejudice from the negligent advice of a non-attorney purporting to be a licensed attorney. See Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
In the instant case, the respondent does not indicate that Mr. Dong or any representative from New Arrival Immigration Services purported to be an attorney. The business card for New Arrival Immigration Services also does not state that the organization employs licensed attorneys to represent its clients. The respondent has therefore not established that she had detrimentally relied on the negligent advice of a person who had falsely purported to be an attorney. In addition, the respondent has not established that her failure to appear for her hearing was caused by the negligence of Mr. Dong or a representative of New Arrival Immigration Services. The respondent argues that she should have been informed of her rescheduled hearing by either Mr. Dong or New Arrival Immigration Services. However, the hearing notice was mailed to her by the Immigration Court at the address contained on the Notice to Appear. She also has not alleged in either the motion or on appeal that she did not receive the Notice of Hearing. It was therefore incumbent on her to appear even if she had not received an instruction from her representative to appear for a hearing on December 6, 2007.
 
*2 The respondent has not established that her failure to appear for her hearing was caused by the advice or lack thereof of her representative. We therefore do not find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings based on “exceptional circumstances” having prevented the respondent from appearing for her hearing.
 
We note that the respondent has not argued on appeal with particularity that she did not receive the Notice of Hearing. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish that the hearing notice provided herein was inadequate. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008) (discussing the factors for rebutting the presumption of delivery of regularly sent mail). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: LORENZO ENRIQUE GARCIA A.K.A. LORENZO GARCIA
File: AXXX XX0 570 - Eloy, AZ
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dominique J. Honea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Convicted of a controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATIONS: Removability; reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 11, 2009. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), as an alien present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. Based upon a Verification of Removal (Exh. 7), the Immigration Judge made a factual finding that the respondent departed the United States on May 22, 2004, after being ordered removed (I.J. at 4), and this factual finding is not clearly erroneous. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). Applying a de novo standard of review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has failed to meet his burden of proving his lawful re-entry to the United States. See Lopez-Rodriguez v. Mukasey, 536 F.3d 1012, 1015 (9th Cir. 2008) (advising that once DHS establishes identity and alienage, the burden shifts to the respondent to prove time, place, and manner of entry).
 
Based upon evidence of the respondent’s 2003 conviction for possession or use of drug paraphernalia (Exh. 4-B), we further affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is inadmissible under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation. See Matter of Martinez-Espinoza, 25 I&N Dec. 118 (BIA 2009).
 
The respondent focuses his arguments on appeal on his request for reopening based upon a claim that prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance by filing a deficient motion to reopen his prior removal proceeding following the March 9, 2002, issuance of an in absentia order of removal. The Immigration Judge denied that motion on April 2, 2002, and the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of that decision on July 2, 2003.
 
*2 The respondent’s request for reopening fails. First, the respondent’s 2004 removal from the United States pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s 2002 in absentia order of removal acted to strip us and the Immigration Judge of authority to reopen prior removal proceedings, whether on the respondent’s motion or sua sponte. See Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008) (declining to follow Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir. 2007)). Second, the respondent has not attempted to comply with the procedure set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 5252680 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLBIN ELVIR-SANCHEZ¿FN2º
File: AXXX XX1 106¿FN1º - San Antonio, TX
December 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Oma N. Nkele, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jane H. Thomson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s March 24, 2009, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind his January 3, 2006, in absentia order of removal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard, and review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The record shows that the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on January 3, 2006. On March 13, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, finding that the respondent has failed to establish that he lacked proper notice of his January 3, 2006, hearing, and to meet the requirements of a motion to reopen. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to satisfy his burden of establishing any basis for granting his motion to reopen.
 
We note that the respondent first argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion because the Notice to Appear (NTA) was deficient, and because the Immigration Court in San Antonio, Texas, did not have jurisdiction to issue an in absentia order of removal in his case since he reported an address in Florida. These arguments are unavailing.
 
The record shows that the NTA was issued by a Supervisory Border Patrol Agent, who is specifically designated by the regulations as one authorized to issue an NTA. 8 C.F.R. § 239.1(a)(10); see Ali v. Gonzales, 435 F.3d 544, 546-47 (5th Cir.2006). The record also shows that the NTA was filed with the San Antonio, Texas, Immigration Court. Thus, jurisdiction is vested, and venue properly lies, with the San Antonio Immigration Court. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.20(a), 1003.14(a), 1239.1(a). Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(g) does not include any language obligating the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) to move for a change of venue on his behalf because he provided a Florida address upon being released on his own recognizance. This regulation simply requires the DHS to advise the Immigration Court of the respondent’s custody status, and the respondent’s fixed address at which the respondent may be served with notices or other documents pertinent to his proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(g). Moreover, the respondent was released on his own recognizance, and thus, the instructions on the Form I-830, which indicates that a motion to change venue may be filed by the DHS if the respondent is “transferred in custody to another location,” is inapplicable to him. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief, Tab 10. Accordingly, we conclude that the NTA was not deficient, and the San Antonio Immigration Court properly had jurisdiction over his case.
 
*2 An in absentia order may be rescinded if the respondent demonstrates that he or she did not receive the hearing notice in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(a)(1), (2). Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. A written notice of the hearing is not required, however, if the respondent, after being informed of his address obligation, failed to provide the address at which he or she can be contacted regarding the removal proceedings. See sections 239(a)(2)(B) of the Act.
 
The record shows that on November 24, 2004, the NTA was served in person to the respondent, and provided the respondent’s address as “210 Pelhamm Road Apt. 120, Fort Walton, FL 32547.” The NTA, under the heading “¿fºailure to appear,” notified the respondent of his obligation to provide a full mailing address at which he may be reached, and to inform the Immigration Court of any changes in his address or telephone number by using a Form EOIR-33. Not having received a Form EOIR-33, the respondent’s January 3, 2006, hearing notice was properly sent by mail to the address provided on the NTA. The record, however, indicates that this hearing notice was returned to the Immigration Court.
 
The respondent, in his affidavit in support of his motion to reopen, did not dispute that he was personally served his NTA, but instead alleges, in general terms, that he had problems communicating with the border patrol agent who gave him the NTA.¿FN3º The respondent, however, did not claim on his affidavit that he was not told or was unaware of his obligation to provide an address at which he could be reached, as he was specifically advised on the NTA, or that his hearing notice was not mailed to the address provided on the NTA. The respondent’s affidavit is spare, and does not even include any statement attesting to the full and correct address he was residing at the time the hearing notice was sent.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent did not receive “actual notice” of his January 3, 2006, hearing, as the record shows that the hearing notice was in fact returned to the Immigration Court, stamped, “rtn to sender/attempted not known.” The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit in Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354 (5th Cir. 2009), has held that a failure to receive actual notice of a removal hearing does not always warrant rescission of an in absentia removal order, particularly when the failure to receive actual notice was due to the aliens’ neglect of their obligation to keep the Immigration Court apprised of their current address. In the instant case, the respondent provided no evidence establishing that the address he provided, which was used on the NTA was, in fact, his true and correct mailing address where he could receive mail, and that he had not moved from that address. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, supra, at 361. On the record before the Immigration Judge, we conclude that the respondent has not met his burden of proving that his proceedings should be reopened, and his in absentia order of removal rescinded, based on lack of notice.¿FN4º
 
*3 The respondent’s assertions relating to “exceptional circumstances” are difficult to understand, and we are not persuaded that such circumstances exist that would warrant reopening his removal proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 17-19, 23). There was no evidence submitted showing that ineffective assistance of counsel, or other exceptional circumstances, caused his failure to appear for his January 3, 2006, hearing, and that the 180-day deadline for filing a motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order should be equitably tolled. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). There is likewise no evidence provided showing that exceptional circumstances beyond the respondent’s control prevented him from timely filing a motion to reopen to apply for new relief from removal within 90 days of the date of entry of the final administrative order of removal, nor has he submitted the requisite applications and established his prima facie eligibility for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), (3); Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) (setting forth requirements for filing a motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of removal proceedings); see also generally Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 356-57 (BIA 1998).
 
The respondent’s argument that he is entitled to sua sponte reopening of his proceedings because he was denied due process is unavailing. As previously discussed, the record does not demonstrate that the respondent did not receive the statutorily required notice of his January 3, 2006, hearing. There is no indication that the Immigration Judge failed to consider the evidence he submitted with his motion to reopen. Contrary to the respondent’s assertion, the Immigration Judge was not required to wait for the DHS’s response before adjudicating the motion, and the Immigration Judge’s decision to rule on the motion before receiving the DHS’s response does not establish that the Immigration Judge was biased or that he lack neutrality. The respondent, moreover, has not shown that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s actions. A lack of a response from the DHS does not compel the Immigration Judge to grant the respondent’s motion, nor is it equivalent to a “jointly filed motion” which would have allowed consideration of his otherwise untimely motion to reopen. We invoke our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings sparingly, and only for truly exceptional situations. The respondent has not demonstrated that the application of such an extraordinary remedy is warranted in this case. Matter G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*4 In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

2

The respondent has alleged on appeal that his true name is “Olvin Daniel Elvir-Sanchez.”

3

The respondent claims, in particular, that the border patrol agent told him to spell his name “Olbin,” rather than the true spelling of his name “Olvin.” At the outset, we note that the respondent’s allegation that he was compelled to misspell his own name because of a border patrol officer’s insistence that the letter “v” is pronounced like a “b” in Spanish, is undermined by the fact that the NTA listed his family name as “Elvir.” Finally, we are not persuaded that the respondent has shown that the alleged erroneous substitution of one letter in his name has any material significance to determining the propriety of the service and issuance of the NTA, the hearing notice, and to the outcome of his motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal.

Moreover, statements by counsel in a motion, Notice of Appeal, or appellate brief, are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980).

U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: SEGUNDO MARCELO ANGAMARCA LLIVICURA

December 4, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew L. Friedman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kori LaPoint
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated February 21, 2008. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on May 8, 2007, pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5) (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent’s appeal will be sustained, and the record of proceeding will be remanded for further proceedings. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600 (BIA 1978) (“¿Rºemand is effective for the stated purpose and for consideration of any and all matters which the ¿ immigration judgeº deems appropriate in the exercise of his administrative discretion or which are brought to his attention in compliance with the appropriate regulations.”). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2009); Matter of Lodge, 19 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 1987) (“Oral argument is heard at the discretion of the Board and will not be granted unless the party appealing has specified the reasons for the appeal in some meaningful fashion.”).
 
The facts as presented for the Immigration Judge’s consideration in the respondent’s motion to reopen and the Department of Homeland Security’s opposition (“DHS”) and as relevant to this appeal are as follows: The DHS personally served the respondent with the Notice to Appear on August 18, 2006, while the respondent was in DHS custody in San Diego, California (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1 (May 8, 2007)). The Notice to Appear was filed in San Diego Immigration Court on September 1, 2006 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1 (May 8, 2007)). The respondent was released from the DHS’s custody in San Diego following the posting of a $15,000 surety bond on September 27, 2006 (Exh. 3 (May 8, 2007)). At that time the respondent reported his address following release to the DHS as being in Corona, New York (Exh. 3 (May 8, 2007)).
 
The initial master calendar hearing in these proceedings was originally scheduled for October 3, 2006 (Exh. 2 (May 8, 2007)). However, on October 4, 2006, the respondent’s master calendar hearing was rescheduled for February 6, 2007 (Exh. 4 (May 8, 2007)). On January 9, 2007, the date of the respondent’s first master calendar hearing was rescheduled once again for May 8, 2007 (Exhs. 9 and 10 (May 8, 2007)). The respondent failed to appear on May 8, 2007, for the scheduled master calendar hearing and, accordingly, was ordered removed in absentia on that date (I.J. at 1). The respondent stated in the affidavit submitted in support of the motion to reopen that he received a copy of the in absentia order from the Immigration Court on May 10, 2007 (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿respondent’s affidavitº).
 
*2 Meanwhile, the respondent retained private counsel to file a motion to change the venue for these removal proceedings from San Diego, California to New York, New York (I.J. at 2-3; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿ respondent’s affidavitº; Exhs. 5, 6, 7, 8, 11, 12, 13 and 14 (May 8, 2007)). In all, the private counsel filed four motions for change of venue on the respondent’s behalf, each of which (1) was properly rejected by the Immigration Court staff for failure to comply with the San Diego Immigration Court’s Local Operating Procedures (Exhs. 5, 6, 7 and 8 (May 8, 2007)), (2) was denied by the Immigration Judge for failure to comply with the San Diego Immigration Court’s Local Operating Procedures (Exh. 12 (May 8, 2007)), or, in the case of the final untimely motion for change of venue filed on April 30, 2007 (Exh. 13 (May 8, 2007)), (3) had prompted the Immigration Judge to provide advanced, written notice to the respondent’s privately retained counsel (but not to the respondent personally) that “¿yºou are given to understand that it may be that the motion will not be granted,” and that the respondent “should therefore be prepared to appear in person on hearing day and be ready to proceed (I.J. at 3; Exh. 14 (May 8, 2007)).”
 
Subsequent to the entry of the May 8, 2007, in absentia removal order, the respondent received a correspondence from the DHS, Notice to Deliver Alien (Form 1-340), dated January 4, 2008, directing him to appear for removal in San Diego, California on February 5, 2008 (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1,2008) at Exh. A ¿respondent’s affidavitº and Exh. J ¿Notice to Deliver Alien º). The DHS’s correspondence induced the respondent to retain present counsel of record who filed the underlying motion to reopen on the respondent’s behalf on February 1, 2008 (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿ respondent’s affidavitº). On February 7, 2008, the DHS filed a memorandum in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen, to which the respondent replied on February 15, 2008 (I.J. at 3).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is predicated on a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 2-3; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008)). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In our view, the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the actions of the respondent’s former counsel in repeatedly filing procedurally deficient motions for change of venue and, most of all, in advising the respondent without any evident justification in fact or law not to appear for the May 8, 2007, master calendar hearing constituted ineffective assistance and, therefore, “exceptional circumstances” (theoretically) warranting rescission of the in absentia removal order (I.J. at 3). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (e)(1) of the Act (defining “exceptional circumstances”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996) (“¿Wºe note that one must look to the “totality of circumstances” to resolve this issue of exceptional circumstances.”); see also Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938 (9th Cir. 2003); Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008) (“We now join our sister circuits in concluding that, under BIA precedent applicable to the pre-1996 version of the INA as well as its current iteration, a lawyer’s inaccurate advice to his client concerning an immigration hearing date can constitute ‘exceptional circumstances’ excusing the alien’s failure to appear at a deportation hearing ¿º and meriting the reopening of an in absentia deportation order ¿º.” (citing Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798, 801-02 (5th Cir. 2007); Lo v. Ashcroft, supra; Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54, 59 n. 4 (2d Cir. 2005)); Matter of Grijalva, supra (“An order of deportation issued following a hearing conducted in absentia may be rescinded under section 242B(c)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(Supp. V 1993), where an alien properly establishes that his failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel which amounts to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of section 242B(f)(2) of the Act.”).
 
*3 The Immigration Judge nevertheless denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on the grounds that it was untimely and, in considering the applicability of equitable tolling to the respondent’s motion to reopen, determined that the doctrine does not apply in this case because the respondent failed to submit “conclusive proof” that the respondent retained the first private attorney subsequent to the entry of the in absentia order of removal on May 8, 2007, for the purpose of preparing and filing a motion to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia order of removal (I.J. at 4; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿respondent’s affidavitº).
 
The respondent does not dispute that the motion to reopen filed on February 1, 2008, is untimely. Thus, the sole issue is whether the doctrine of equitable tolling should be invoked in order to toll the 180-day filing deadline for motions to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia removal order based on “exceptional circumstances” arising from the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003). Although this is a close case, we conclude that it should.
 
In concluding that the doctrine of equitable tolling should not be invoked, the Immigration Judge credited a letter from the respondent’s former counsel submitted by the DHS in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen on February 7, 2008 (I.J. at 4). While the respondent claimed to have paid the former attorney a separate fee of $300.00 to file the motion to reopen shortly after he received the in absentia order in the mail, the former attorney denied this assertion in the letter dated February 6, 2008 (I.J. at 4). Specifically, the letter from the former attorney acknowledged that the respondent arrived at his law office on May 15, 2007, after, according to the respondent’s affidavit, the respondent had received the in absentia removal order on May 10, 2007, in the mail, but the respondent’s former attorney claimed that a payment of $200.00 that he had extracted from the respondent during the May 15, 2007, office visit was applied toward the remaining balance of the $1,200.00 fee that he had charged the respondent to file the (ineffective) motion(s) to change venue in the San Diego Immigration Court (I.J. at 4).
 
For his part, the respondent claims that the former attorney assured him that the entry of the in absentia order was an “error” when he visited his office after receiving the in absentia order in the mail (Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿respondent’s affidavitº). The respondent further claims that the former attorney failed to apprise him of the four procedurally defective motions to change venue that he had filed on the respondent’s behalf and that he only became aware of these defective motions after he retained present counsel of record and was able to obtain a copy of his file from the former attorney on January 29, 2008 (Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1,2008) at Exh. A ¿ respondent’s affidavitº). The respondent’s former attorney failed to acknowledge, much less address, these serious allegations of on-going ineffective assistance of counsel extending beyond the entry of the in absentia removal order in the letter submitted by the DHS on February 7, 2008 (I.J. at 3; The DHS’s Opposition to the Respondent’s M.T.R. at Exh. B).
 
*4 We conclude that the respondent has provided sufficient evidence in support of the motion to reopen the record of proceeding to warrant reopening and rescission of the in absentia removal order dated May 8, 2007. And we disagree with the weight the Immigration Judge accorded the letter from the respondent’s former attorney dated February 6, 2008, proffered by the DHS in opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen, which is the only evidence in the record of proceeding invoked by the Immigration Judge in support of the conclusion that the equitable tolling doctrine does not apply under these facts (I.J. at 4). See Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
First, the assertion made by the respondent’s former attorney that he was not aware of the entry of the in absentia order dated May 8, 2007, until June 25, 2007, is highly dubious (I.J. at 3; The DHS’s Opposition to the Respondent’s M.T.R. at Exh. B). The fourth and final motion for change of venue the former attorney filed on the respondent’s behalf on April 30, 2007, reflects that he was aware of the hearing scheduled for May 8, 2007 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 13 (May 8, 2007)). Furthermore, the Immigration Judge wrote to the respondent’s former attorney on May 1, 2007, in response to that motion that “¿yºou are given to understand that it may be that the motion will not be granted,” and that the respondent “should therefore be prepared to appear in person on hearing day and be ready to proceed (I.J. at 3; Exh. 14 (May 8, 2007)).” Accordingly, we can see no reasonable explanation in the record of proceeding for the former attorney’s claimed ignorance of the entry of the in absentia removal order on May 8, 2007 (I.J. at 3; The DHS’s Opposition to the Respondent’s M.T.R. at Exh. B).
 
Second, the letter from the former attorney is silent on the most serious allegation of professional misconduct levied against him in this case, that is, that he instructed the respondent not to appear at the May 8, 2007, master calendar hearing after receiving the Immigration Judge’s May 1, 2008, correspondence directing the respondent to appear (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s M.T.R. (Feb. 1, 2008) at Exh. A ¿respondent’s affidavitº); Exh. 14 (May 8, 2007)). Third, it is noteworthy that the respondent’s former attorney freely admits to accepting $200.00 from the respondent on May 15, 2007, for the provision of services that he never effectively provided after filing four procedurally defective motions for change of venue (I.J. at 4; The DHS’s Opposition to the Respondent’s M.T.R. at Exh. B).
 
We conclude that the respondent’s actions before and after the entry of the in absentia removal order exhibited a reasonable level of diligence under the circumstances; he promptly retained counsel after his release from the DHS’s custody; the action for which he retained counsel, obtaining a change of venue, was the correct initial step in fulfilling his appearance obligations in these proceedings and, after this necessary initial step failed to be carried out by the respondent’s former attorney, there is no convincing explanation that we can see on this record for the former attorney’s professed ignorance of the entry of the in absentia removal order on May 8, 2007, or the former attorney’s failure to take any step(s) to apprise and assist his client, the respondent, after the in absentia order’s entry. We therefore conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen should be granted. Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
*5 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia order of removal dated May 8, 2007, is granted. The record of proceeding is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Board Member Filppu respectfully concurs in the result. The Immigration Judge wrongly demanded “conclusive proof” that the respondent contracted with prior counsel for the filing of a motion to rescind the in absentia order. Given the totality of the evidence, the respondent met the “high standard” set by Congress for a showing of exceptional circumstances. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33, 34 (BIA 1998); section 240(c)(4)(A) and (B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 5252741 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIA ANA RESENDEZ-GONZALES JULIA ANA GONZALES-RESENDEZ JUDITH ARIANA GONZALES-RESENDEZ
Files: AXXX XX7 005 - Houston, TX
AXXX XX7 001
AXXX XX7 002
December 3, 2009
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Francisco J. Alvillar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents have appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 17, 2008, denying their motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondents removed in absentia for their failure to appear for the hearing on August 9, 1994. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board may review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of immigration judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). The Board should defer to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493, 496 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500, 501 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal the respondents contend that the government did not demonstrate proper notice or deportability by “clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence,” as required by former section 242B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b.
 
As the respondents’ deportation proceedings are governed by former section 242B of the Act, we apply the case of Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995) (Board held that a hearing notice which is sent by certified mail to the alien’s last known address is sufficient to establish by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that the alien received written notice of the deportation hearing within the meaning of section 242B(c)(1) of the Act). The record reflects that on May 27, 1994, the Houston Immigration Court sent a separate notice of hearing by certified mail to each of the respondents at their last known address of 1550 Blalock, Apt. #161, Houston, TX 77080. Therefore, it is clear that the respondents received notice pursuant to Matter of Grijalva, supra. We held further that there “is no requirement that the certified mail return receipt be signed by the alien or a responsible person at his address to effect service.” Id. at 34. In this case, in the record of proceedings there is a signed return receipt card for each notice of hearing sent to the respondents. A notice of hearing dated May 27, 1994, was sent to respondent, Julia Ana Resendez-Gonzalez (AXXX XXX XXX), at her last known address. The receipt for certified mail is attached to the copy of the hearing notice in the record and indicates the postmark date of “05/27/94.” The corresponding return receipt card signed by “Julia A. Gonzalez” indicates the date of delivery as “5/31/94.” The record of proceedings contains the same documentation for the other two respondents. The return receipt card for the hearing notice sent to respondent Judith Ariana Gonzales-Resendez (AXXX XXX XXX) is signed by “Julia A. Gonzalez” and indicates the date of delivery as “5/31/94,” and the return receipt card for the hearing notice sent to respondent Julia Ana Gonzales-Resendez (AXXX XXX XXX) is signed by Jorge Gonzalez and indicates the date of delivery as “6/1/94.” Similarly, the article numbers on the receipts for certified mail match the article numbers on the signed return receipt cards, which are attached to the corresponding notices of hearing in the record of proceedings. The record clearly reflects that the return receipt cards specifically pertain to the notices of hearing, as they were all sent by the Office of the Immigration Judge in Houston, Texas, and they are all postmarked May 27, 1994, the same date as the hearing notices.¿FN1º
 
*2 The respondents argue that the notices of hearing were sent directly to the respondents, and that their former attorney, Juan Reyes, Esquire, was not provided with copies of the notices of hearing. However, the record does not contain a Notice of Entry or Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28), which would indicate that the respondents were represented by an attorney or representative before the Immigration Court. Therefore, the Houston Immigration Court was only required to send notices of hearing to the respondents. The lead respondent and her spouse, Jorge Gonzales, state in their affidavits that they did not understand the certified letters so they sent them to Mr. Reyes. They further indicate that in 1998, their attorney informed them that they had an order of deportation against them. We note that the respondents have not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel for their failure to appear at the hearing. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In light of the documentary evidence in the record that the notices of hearing were sent by certified mail through the U.S. Postal Service to the respondents’ last known address and there is proof of delivery of the notices of hearing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondents received proper notice of the hearing and they have failed to overcome the strong presumption of effective service.
 
The respondents also assert that the government did not establish by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that the respondents were deportable because they had been issued border crossing cards and were crossing the United States/Mexico border legally during 1994. They contend that the existence of the border crossing cards at the time of the allegedly illegal entry demonstrates that the government could not have met their burden of proof. However, the Orders to Show Cause reflect that the respondents were charged with entering the United States without inspection in 1986. In the in absentia orders dated August 9, 1994, the Immigration Judge found that during the hearing, “service of the Order to Show Cause upon the respondent was confirmed, and deportability on the identified charge(s) established by ‘clear, convincing, and unequivocal’ evidence which has been received to the record of proceedings.” An Immigration Judge may receive into evidence any oral or written statement which is material and relevant to any issue in the case made by the alien or any other person during any investigation or examination. Such evidence may be admitted as long as it is probative and its use is fundamentally fair so as not to violate due process standards. Form I-213 is a record which is routinely prepared by the Department of Homeland (DHS) officers as a summary of information obtained at the time of the initial processing of an individual suspected of being an alien unlawfully present in the United States. Espinoza v. INS, 45 F.3d 308 (9th Cir. 1995). Courts have recognized the inherent trustworthiness attributable to “public records or reports” such as Form I-213. Id.; see also Matter of Gomez-Gomez, 23 I&N Dec. 522 (BIA 2002). Form I-213 is frequently offered into evidence at deportation and removal hearings, and we have noted its admissibility. Id. In his decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge found it unnecessary to address the respondents’ contention that they entered the United States with border crossing cards, noting that this argument was not presented to the trial court and the presiding Immigration Judge was unaware of the existence of border crossing cards.
 
*3 The respondents have submitted additional evidence on appeal. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). The record supports the finding that the respondents had notice of their hearing. Accordingly, the respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

All the return receipt cards for the notices of hearing have the pre-printed ‘Office of the Immigration Judge’ in Houston, Texas, as the return address. In contrast, the Orders to Show Cause were sent by the former Immigration and Naturalization Service, and the government’s return address would appear on the return receipt cards for the Orders to Show Cause.

2009 WL 5252750 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ISRAEL ABDIAS ROMERO BONILLA
File: AXXX XX3 919 - Portland, OR
December 2, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaesa W. McLin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah C. Schreck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on April 2, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his application for Temporary Protected Status (“TPS”). On July 2, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent contends in his motion that he received ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented him at his hearing and on appeal to the Board. He states that his attorney did not properly prepare him for the hearing and did not ask questions that would have established his presence in the United States as of February 13, 2001, which was required for the respondent to be granted TPS.
 
With certain exceptions, motions to reopen must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Under the controlling law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, a respondent seeking to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the procedural requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent must also show that counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence and must demonstrate that actual prejudice was suffered due to the attorney’s performance. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 794-95 (9th Cir. 2005). Equitable tolling is available to a respondent who is prevented from filing the motion within the required time period due to deception, fraud, or error if the respondent exercises due diligence in discovering such circumstances. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
First, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. Because the final order of removal in this case was issued on April 2, 2009, the motion to reopen was required to be filed no later than July 1, 2009. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act. The motion, which was filed on July 2, 2009, was 1 day late. Additionally, the respondent does not allege that equitable tolling should apply and does not claim that there was any deception, fraud, or error that prevented him from filing the motion in a timely fashion.
 
Furthermore, even assuming that the motion were timely filed and assuming that the respondent substantially complied with the technical requirements set out in Lozada, the respondent has failed to show how he was prejudiced by his attorney’s performance. As the DHS correctly argues, the respondent was denied TPS because he provided inconsistent testimony and documentary evidence as to his date and manner of entry. While he claimed in his TPS application to have entered in January 2001, he conceded that his mother’s application for TPS indicated his entry was not until April 2001 or later (Tr. at 26). Also, he claimed at different times to have entered the United States in Phoenix, Arizona and Houston, Texas, neither of which is near the border. The affidavits of family and friends, which were written many years after the respondent’s claimed date of entry, were determined to be insufficient to establish his presence in the United States as of the required date, given the other evidence.
 
*2 The respondent has failed to explain in his motion what additional evidence was available at the time of the hearing that could have established his eligibility for TPS. In fact, the respondent has still not established his eligibility for TPS. Accordingly, the respondent has not shown any prejudice from his former attorney’s representation.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 5252750 (BIA)

2009 WL 4899008 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KINGSLEY NEDU ONYEMORDI
File: AXXX XX2 910 - Dallas, TX
November 27, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, has filed a timely motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) to reconsider our March 27, 2009, final decision dismissing the appeal taken from the Immigration Judge’s decision. The Immigration Judge denied a continuance to pursue adjustment of status based upon the second petition (Form I-130) filed by the respondent’s United States citizen wife, and ordered the respondent removed. In our final decision, we agreed, inter alia, that the Immigration Judge did not err in concluding the respondent failed to establish eligibility for adjustment of status in light of the revocation of an earlier petition because of marriage fraud pursuant to section 204(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§ 1154(c). We also denied a motion to reopen filed while the appeal was pending. That motion requested a remand to apply for cancellation of removal. As the instant motion proffers purportedly new evidence, it will also be treated as a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
The instant motion argues that, during proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent was the victim of the ineffective assistance of counsel who failed to advise the respondent that he was eligible to apply for cancellation of removal. In support, the motion proffers a complaint against Attorney Mark McBroom, the counsel who represented the respondent before the Immigration Judge.
 
However, the complaint against the attorney is on a Form EOIR-44 and should have been filed with the Office of General Counsel, Executive Office of Immigration Review, rather than with this Board. The complaint does not include specific allegations of ineffective assistance. Also, no complaint has been filed with the appropriate state bar authority and no letter has been sent to the Attorney providing an opportunity to respondent to the allegations.
 
The motion does not comply with the procedural requirements to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim that would warrant a tolling of the time limit on motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Furthermore, the motion is number barred. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*2 Also, there is no explanation as to why the ineffective assistance of prior counsel argument was not made while the appeal was pending, when the respondent was represented by a different attorney. The motion does not cite to any errors of law or fact in our prior decision that would warrant reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 4899008 (BIA)

2009 WL 4899009 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SELVIN LIZANDRO YAGUAS-BARRIOS A.K.A. SELVIN LIZANDRO YAGUAS A.K.A. JACOB FRANK VELA
File: AXXX XX2 651 - San Antonio, TX
November 27, 2009
 
REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Prince Uche Nwakanma, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen after our September 29, 2009, decision dismissing his appeal. He wishes to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and urges that his prior attorney was ineffective for failing to file such an application at his hearing below. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
The respondent has failed to support his motion to reopen with an application for the relief he seeks, or to otherwise demonstrate that he is prima facie eligible for a section 212(h) waiver. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (motions to reopen must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief “and all supporting documentation”); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 319 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988). Moreover, the record reflects that the respondent was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1994 and that he was thereafter convicted of an aggravated felony, for which he was found removable. As such, he is barred from receiving a 212(h) waiver. Matter of Yeung, 21 I&N Dec. 610 (BIA 1996). Thus, it would not appear that any failure to submit a waiver application below could be considered ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 4899009 (BIA)
End of Document
