2009 WL 4899054 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEMPSEY J. LUCIEN A.K.A. LUCIEN DEMPSEY
File: AXXX XX5 262 - Boston, MA
November 27, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL and MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William E. Graves, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gwendylan Tregerman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Remand, termination
 
The respondent filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s August 26, 2009, finding the respondent removable, as charged, based on his record of conviction (Exh. 2), and denied the respondent’s request to terminate proceedings. While the respondent’s appeal was pending before the Board, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and remand this matter to the Immigration Court for further proceedings based on new, material, and previously unavailable evidence. The respondent’s request is premised on the claim that the conviction which forms the basis for the grounds of removal, has been vacated. Specifically, the respondent has presented evidence that on October 5, 2009, the First Justice of the Brockton District Court, Brockton, Massachusetts, granted the respondent’s Motion to Withdraw Guilty Plea and Order a New Trial¿FN1º on the basis of ineffective assistance of former counsel at his criminal trial. We observe the respondent’s motion was properly served on the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), and whereas the DHS has expressed no opposition to remanding the record for further proceedings, it has also indicated that on remand it proposes to lodge additional charges against the respondent.
 
The Board in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), reversed on other grounds, Pickering v. Gonzales, 465 F.3d 263 (6th Cir.2006), held that if a court vacates a conviction based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings, there is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. However, an alien remains convicted of a removable offense when the predicate conviction is vacated simply for rehabilitative purposes or to aid the alien in avoiding adverse immigration consequences and not because of any procedural or substantive defect in the original conviction. Id. at 624; see also Saleh v. Gonzales, 495 F.3d 17, 25 (2d Cir. 2007) (joining and citing to the decisions of the various circuits in support of the Board’s decision in Pickering that if a court vacates an alien’s criminal conviction solely on the basis of immigration hardships or rehabilitation, rather than on the basis of a substantive or procedural defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, the conviction is not eliminated for immigration purposes and will continue to serve as a valid factual predicate for a charge of removability despite its vacatur); see also Matter of Chavez-Martinez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007) (alien’s conviction cannot be vacated solely for immigration purposes).
 
*2 In the matter before us, however, it is clear the criminal court judge’s ruling granting the respondent’s Motion to Withdraw Guilty Plea and Order a New Trial, was based on his finding that the plea entered by the respondent at his criminal trial was not “knowingly, voluntarily, and intelligently made,” where it was the product of ineffective assistance of counsel. Therefore, the court’s action was premised upon what it perceived to be a constitutional infirmity in the underlying proceedings, rather than some form of post-conviction relief. See Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31, 34-35 (1st Cir. 2006) (a vacated conviction is no longer a “conviction” within the meaning of the immigration laws only “if a court with jurisdiction vacates ¿the º conviction based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings.”); Herrera-Inirio v. INS, 208 F.3d 299 (1st Cir. 2000).
 
The question here is whether there is now an conviction for a crime involving moral turpitude, or any conviction, rendering the respondent not only removable but also precluding his eligibility for relief, or whether the proceedings should be terminated. Consequently, in view of the foregoing and the lack of opposition by the DHS, the respondent’s motion will be granted, the record will be remanded to Immigration Court for further proceedings, in order to enable the Immigration Judge to consider the new evidence offered by the respondent relating to his removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i), as well as considering any new additional charges that may be lodged by DHS,¿FN2º and to determine whether the respondent can now establish his eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the proceedings should be terminated.
 
Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD

The record reflects the respondent had been convicted on September 8, 2006, in the Brockton District Court, Brockton, Massachusetts, for the offense of Attempt to Commit a Crime, to wit: Robbery, in violation of M.G.L. Chapter 274, section 6 (Exh. 2).

2

As indicated above, in its non-opposition to the respondent’s motion, the DHS announced its intention to lodge additional charges against the respondent upon remand to the Immigration Court.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARBARA ROBINSON
File: AXXX XX2 823 - Buffalo, NY
November 24, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reconsider, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
On August 5, 2003, an Immigration Judge issued a final removal order against the respondent, who is from Jamaica. The Immigration Judge granted voluntary departure, found that no other relief from removal was requested, and noted that appeal to the Board was waived. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied on February 26, 2009.
 
The Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on September 2, 2009. The Board found no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely and numerically barred, as it was not submitted within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s August 5, 2003, decision and was the respondent’s second motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The Board also noted that it does not have the authority to apply an “exceptional circumstances” or other general equitable exception to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(1).
 
The Board also found that, to the extent that the respondent argued that her failure to depart the United States under her voluntary departure order was a result of ineffective assistance of counsel, we agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reopen failed to comply with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board recognized that the respondent had submitted additional evidence on appeal regarding her claim of ineffective assistance; however, the Board found, we do not consider evidence first offered on appeal, and the respondent failed to establish that the information contained in the documents she sought to offer on appeal could not have been produced before the Immigration Judge.
 
The Board declined to reconsider its September 2, 2009, decision on September 22, 2009. The Board found no error in its refusal to grant the respondent a second extension to file a brief on appeal. The Board also treated the respondent’s filing as an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen, as the respondent submitted evidence with her motion. Moreover, the Board found, the respondent did not present an exceptional situation which would warrant sua sponte reopening.
 
The respondent on October 8, 2009, filed a “Motion To Re-Consider Sua Sponte”. To the extent that the respondent seeks reconsideration of the Board’s September 22, 2009, decision, the regulations state that a party “may not seek reconsideration of a decision denying a previous motion to reconsider.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). To the extent that the respondent presents a motion to reopen, the motion is untimely, and numerically barred. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). As the Board stated in its most recent decision, we could only reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a). This action is taken only in “exceptional situations”, Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), which have not been presented here. The pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The pending motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Case Reopened by IN RE: OLGA MERCEDES SOLANO-VARGAS, BIA, October 12, 2010
2009 WL 4639867 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLGA MERCEDES SOLANO-VARGAS
File: AXXX XX7 390 - Seattle, WA
November 18, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Diana E. Moller, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marci L. Ellsworth
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 8, 2008. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to suppress but denied her motion to terminate. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to terminate. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent and her sisters were stopped by a deputy sheriff for traffic violations. They claim that the traffic stop was racially-motivated. An immigration officer was called to the scene and the respondent and her sisters were apprehended. During these events, the respondent, her sisters, and their mother, who arrived after her daughters’ apprehension, made admissions of alienage. The respondent, her three sisters, and their mother were all placed in proceedings.
 
At a master calendar hearing held on August 28, 2006, the respondent appeared with counsel, pled to the Notice to Appear, and conceded removability. Subsequently, pursuant to a motion filed by the respondent, her case was consolidated with proceedings for her mother and sisters.¿FN1º The respondent’s new counsel filed a motion to suppress. However, the new counsel did not request that the respondent’s concession be withdrawn or raise any issues in that regard before the Immigration Judge. New counsel did not explain why the concession should not be binding on the respondent, except for claiming that the concession was made in bond proceedings and was tainted evidence.
 
The Immigration Judge granted the motion to suppress the evidence obtained during the traffic stop.¿FN2º However, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate based on her independent admissions of alienage, through counsel, at a master calendar hearing in removal proceedings.
 
We review de novo questions of law, discretion, and judgment. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, only to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
On appeal, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to suppress the concession of removability made by her prior counsel at an August 28, 2006, hearing (Tr. at 3).¿FN3º The respondent contends that the concession cannot be considered because it was tainted evidence and was made during a bond hearing. We disagree. The respondent conceded removability before her new counsel filed the motion to suppress the Form I-213. Furthermore, the concession was obtained during a master calendar hearing in removal proceedings, not a bond proceeding as argued by the respondent on appeal. See Tr. at 1 (stating that the respondent was in removal proceedings); Tr. at 5 (the respondent’s prior attorney requesting that the master calendar hearing be continued); Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings dated August 16, 2006 (indicating that a master calendar hearing in removal proceedings was scheduled for August 28, 2006). Independently obtained evidence such as statements made by the respondent at a hearing may be relied upon in removal proceedings. Matter of Cervantes-Tortes, 21 I&N Dec. 351, 353 (BIA 1996).
 
*2 Lastly, to the extent that the respondent claims that she would not have been placed in proceedings but for the unlawful seizure, she has no legitimate expectation of avoiding deportation proceedings and remaining in illegal status indefinitely. See, e.g., Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973-74 (9th Cir. 2004). Based on the foregoing, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable as charged. Therefore, we affirm his denial of the respondent’s motion to terminate.
 
Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

2

The Department of Homeland Security has appealed the Immigration Judge’s grant of the motion to suppress only in the case of the respondent’s mother and sisters. That appeal will be decided in a separate decision by this Board.

3

We note that the respondent does not claim ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented her when the concession was made.

Oscar L. Amorow, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on April 29, 2009, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s October 4, 2007, decision. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider on May 28, 2009. The respondent has submitted documentation with her motion, and, cast as a motion to reopen, her motion is timely. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The Board considered the respondent’s prior motion to remand pursuant to Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The Attorney General’s decision in Matter of Compean has now been vacated. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). With respect to reconsideration, the motion is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent has not identified a material error of fact or law as of the time that our decision was rendered. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Although Matter of Compean has been vacated, we find that under the pre-Compean standards, the respondent still has not demonstrated prejudice with respect to the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of her request for a waiver under section 212(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(I), or our agreement thereof (I.J. at 5; Bd. Dec. at 2). Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Moreover, the respondent has not carried the “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if her motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). The respondent has submitted, inter alia, evidence that she has filed a Form I-601, Form I-485, and has an approved Form I-130. She has also submitted evidence that she has communicated with her prior attorney regarding her current ineffective assistance of counsel claim. However, as we found above, the respondent has failed to establish prejudice arising from her former attorney’s actions (Bd. Dec. at 2). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has provided no evidence to disturb the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent’s departure from the United States would not result in extreme hardship to her husband, for purposes of a section 212(i) waiver of inadmissibility (I.J. at 5; Bd. Dec. at 2). We arrived at this conclusion in our prior decision, and the respondent has provided no evidence of hardship with her current motion.
 
*2 Accordingly, we do not find a remand warranted based on the respondent’s arguments. Id; see also Afwani v. Mukasey, 526 F.3d 788 (4th Cir. 2008). Therefore, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

2009 WL 4639831 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALMA DEYANIRA GUILLEN
File: AXXX XX5 211 - Houston, TX
November 13, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sharon B. Gray, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 19, 2008, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings that were conducted in absentia on May 23, 2008. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Specifically, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not demonstrate that the in absentia order of removal qualifies for rescission under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (stating that the Board reviews questions of law de novo); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (recognizing that notice to the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (stating that whenever an alien is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to attorney of record). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings leading up to that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (mandating that the Board “will accept the determination of factual issues by an immigration judge … unless the determination is clearly erroneous”).
 
It is undisputed that notice of the respondent’s May 23, 2008, hearing that was scheduled to begin at 9:00 a.m. was properly served on the respondent’s former counsel of record (Exh. 3). However, the respondent on appeal claims she that appeared at 1:30 p.m., instead of 9:00 a.m., due to the erroneous advice of her former attorney (Resp. Br. at 5-6).
 
Although the respondent contends that she did, in fact, appear, albeit at 1:30 p.m., instead of 9:00 a.m., the respondent on appeal provides no details of the circumstances surrounding her late arrival, i.e., whether she spoke with Immigration Court personnel, whether the Immigration Judge was still on the bench, or what, if any efforts were made to contact the Immigration Judge. Cf. Matter of Alarcon-Chavez v. Gonzales, 403 F.3d 343 (5th Cir. 2005) (holding that an alien who arrives only 20 minutes late for his Immigration Court hearing, endeavors unsuccessfully to have the Immigration Judge conduct the hearing, and promptly files a motion to reopen has not failed to appear). Therefore, on this record, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated that her late appearance should be excused.
 
*2 Second, we reject the respondent’s apparent ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent provided no evidence other than her own affidavit to support her claim that she received erroneous advice from her former counsel. Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent failed to show she did not receive notice of her hearing, which was properly served on the respondent’s former attorney of record. See, e.g., Joshi v. Ashcroft, 389 F.3d 732, 735 (7th Cir. 2004) (stating that “a bare, uncorroborated, self-serving, denial of receipt, even if sworn, is weak evidence”). We also do not find that the respondent’s assertion that her former attorney failed to properly inform her of her scheduled hearing is alone sufficient to demonstrate patent error. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Nor, as the Immigration Judge noted, has the respondent either explicitly alleged ineffective assistance of counsel or sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
On this record, and in view of the 2008 final removal order, we decline to set aside the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, for the reasons explained by the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph Lopez-Wilson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paul R. Stultz
Deputy Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of April 9, 2008, in which his motion to remand was denied. The record will be remanded.
 
On January 4, 2008, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal to El Salvador, of which he is a native and citizen, but granted a period of voluntary departure until May 5, 2008. On February 21, 2008, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen, claiming that his former counsel never advised him of the opportunity to apply for asylum and withholding of removal.
 
In his decision denying the motion, the Immigration Judge cited the time and numerical restrictions on motions to reopen set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and he concluded that the respondent did not fall within the exception to those limitations contained in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4).
 
The initial period of voluntary departure was granted pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b), and among the prerequisites for such a grant is that the respondent waive appeal of all issues. The respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision but instead filed the motion with the Immigration Judge. However, the motion was technically barred neither on numerical or timeliness grounds. The motion, filed well within the 90-day period permitted for motions to reopen, was based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and the Immigration Judge did not address it merits.
 
Consequently, we will remand this case for the Immigration Judge to revisit the motion to address its merits and enter a new decision. The following order will consequently be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2009 WL 3818076 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WELINTON FELIZ BETANCES A.K.A. WELINTON FELIZ
File: AXXX XX7 368 - Baltimore, MD
November 6, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julia M. Toro, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dimple R. Shah
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 13, 2008, decision denying his request for a postponement to obtain a witness subpoena and denying his request for voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent first contends that the Immigration Judge erred in finding him statutorily ineligible for “pre-conclusion” voluntary departure under section 240B(a)(1) of the Act because the statute does not specifically require travel documentation.¿FN1º See Respondent’s Notice of Appeal. However, as noted by the Immigration Judge, in order to obtain “pre-conclusion” voluntary departure, an alien must be willing to waive appeal of all issues (I.J. at 8). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i) (2009). Here, the respondent indicated that he was appealing the Immigration Judge’s denial of his request for a postponement to seek a witness subpoena (I.J. at 8). As a result, the respondent was not eligible for “pre-conclusion” voluntary departure (I.J. at 8-9). Therefore, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision in this regard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d) (de novo review).
 
Next, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not allowing a postponement to obtain a subpoena for the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) officer that conducted his and his wife’s interview in regard to his application for adjustment of status. See Respondent’s Notice of Appeal. The respondent asserts that the DHS officer that conducted the interview forced his wife to sign a document attesting to the alleged marriage fraud under duress. See id. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request due to his failure to follow Local Operating Procedures Rules 2 and 5 in regard to the filing of both a written motion and a witness list prior to the hearing (I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge also concluded that the respondent failed to establish good cause for an adjournment to obtain a subpoena where he and his counsel were aware of the relevant DHS documentation as early as May 2007 and failed to seek the subpoena prior to the date of the hearing (I.J. at 2-3).
 
*2 Rule 5 of the Local Operating Procedures of the Baltimore Immigration Court, promulgated pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.40, provided as follows:¿FN2º
Except in cases of unforeseen rebuttal witnesses, if any party intends to present one or more witnesses, other than the respondent(s), the party intending to call such witness must file a witness list not less than ten (10) days before the merit hearing, identifying
 
(1) the subject matter of the testimony of each proposed witness, and
 
(2) the language of such witness and whether an interpreter will be needed.
 
The respondent did not file a witness list. Absent exceptional circumstances, an Immigration Judge acts within the scope of her discretion by excluding the testimony of witnesses who were not included in a pre-hearing witness list. See Positive Black Talk, Inc. v. Cash Money Records, Inc., 394 F.3d 357, 378 (5th Cir. 2004) (citing Singer v. City of Waco, 324 F.3d 813, 822 (5th Cir. 2003)). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s contention that he was calling the DHS officer as a rebuttal witness was insufficient inasmuch as he was aware of the witness and the disputed documentation as early as May 2007 (I.J. at 2-3).
 
The respondent has not established, or even argued, that the omission of a witness list was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, and therefore the decision not to file the list is binding on the respondent. See generally Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, decisions of counsel are binding on alien clients). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that the failure of the DHS officer to testify caused him substantial prejudice, as required to prove a due process violation. Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248, 258 (5th Cir. 1981). Specifically, the relevance of the testimony of the DHS officer to the respondent’s proceedings is unclear as it did not affect either his removability or his application for voluntary departure.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Robert Lieber, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert K. Bingham
Special Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final administrative order in this case was entered on August 19, 2002. On September 26, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied because it was filed six years after the final administrative order. It is therefore untimely. With certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent has failed to establish a basis to equitably toll the 90-day deadline. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129-135 (2d Cir. 2000)(aliens relying on equitable tolling must establish that their constitutional right to due process was violated by counsel’s conduct and that they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled). See also Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130-32 (2d Cir. 2008) (an alien is required to exercise due diligence both before and after he has or should have discovered ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
We first point out that the record does not show that either Sheri Paige or Mario DeMarco, the counsel against whom he substantively makes his allegations of ineffective assistance, represented him in these proceedings. It was Mark C. Salvucci, who was associated with Mr. DeDarco’s law offices, who appeared on the respondent’s behalf in the proceedings before the Immigration Judge and before the Board on appeal. Mr. DeMarco did not file a notice that he was representing the respondent nor did he appear at the hearing or submit any filings on his behalf.¿FN1º Further, although Mr. Salvucci is mentioned in passing in the letter to bar counsel (Exh. C), disciplinary complaint forms and specific allegations of ineffectiveness are argued in the motion only with respect to Ms. Paige and Mr. DeMarco (Exh. D). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The respondent has not demonstrated that he acted diligently in pursuing a claim of ineffective assistance against a counsel who represented him in these proceedings. See Iavorski v. United States INS, supra; Rashid v. Mukasey, supra.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

The Executive Office For Immigration Review recognizes appearances by individual representatives, not by firms. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.1. If more than one member of a firm wishes to enter an appearance, a separate notice of appearance is required.

IN RE: KHALED SADI

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; stay of removal
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 13, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s June 1, 2009, decision finding the respondent removable as charged and denying him various forms of relief from removal. On October 21, 2009, the respondent filed the instant timely motion for reconsideration of our October 13, 2009, decision and for a stay of removal pending our disposition of the motion. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent has failed to identify any material error of fact or law that would justify reversal of our prior decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s assertions that reconsideration is warranted are lacking in merit. First, the fact that the DHS did not charge the respondent with removability as an aggravated felon based on his 2005 larceny conviction has no bearing on whether that conviction constitutes an aggravated felony that renders the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(3). And we find no factual or legal error in our determination that the respondent’s larceny conviction is an aggravated felony that pretermits his application for cancellation of removal. Hence, we see no basis to reconsider our affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s denial of cancellation of removal.
 
The respondent also contends that the Immigration Judge failed to engage in fact-finding to determine whether his 2000 conviction for possession of narcotics renders him ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). (Motion to Reconsider at 2-3, 6). However, that argument could and should have been raised on direct appeal and, as such, does not warrant reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that a motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied). In any event, as we pointed out in our October 13, 2009, decision, the respondent did not even seek section 212(h) relief at his original hearing, notwithstanding his contention to the contrary (Motion to Reconsider at 7). To the extent that the respondent now claims for the first time that his former counsel was ineffective in failing to pursue a section 212(h) waiver, that allegation does not implicate an error of fact or law in our prior decision that would warrant reconsideration.¿FN1º
 
*2 We also deny reconsideration based on the respondent’s vague, newly raised claim that his 2000 narcotics conviction is not a controlled substance offense for immigration purposes and, as such, should not affect his eligibility for section 212(h) relief (Motion to Reconsider at 6). That argument could and should have been raised on direct appeal and, thus, provides no basis for reconsideration of our prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58.
 
We also see no basis to reconsider our prior decision pursuant to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit’s decision in Romero v. Ashcroft, 382 F.3d 930 (9th Cir. 2007). That case is non-binding in the Second Circuit, in which this case arises. In any event, the respondent has not clearly articulated why that case would warrant reconsideration of our prior decision (Motion to Reconsider at 4), and we see no reason why it would. To the extent that the respondent is now, for the first time, seeking to challenge the DHS’s decision to charge him under section 212 of the Act rather than section 237 of the Act in his removal proceedings, that argument could and should have been raised earlier in the proceedings and, as such, does not justify reconsideration of our prior decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58.
 
Finally, while we acknowledge that we never expressly addressed the respondent’s request for review of his appeal by three Board members in our October 13, 2009, decision, any error in that regard is harmless and does not warrant reconsideration of our prior decision. This case does not fall within any of the regulatory categories warranting review by a three-member panel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (e)(6). Our October 13, 2009, adjudication of this matter by a single Board Member was, therefore, appropriate.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We further note that, to the extent that the respondent is seeking reopening of his proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel, he has failed to offer any evidence that substantially complies with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988), or demonstrate that he suffered the requisite prejudice from counsel’s alleged inaction, such that reopening of these proceedings would be warranted. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009); see also Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 142 (2d Cir. 2007) (providing that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show substantial compliance with Lozada’s requirements and that he was prejudiced by the performance of counsel).

IN RE: GHASSAN BADIH MANSOUR, BENEFICIARY OF VISA PETITION FILED BY GLORIA JEAN ANDERSON, PETITIONER

IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Neda Zaman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lucinda A. Love
Associate Regional Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
 
*1 The petitioner, a United States citizen, appeals from the decision of the Field Office Director (“Director”) dated April 2, 2009, denying the visa petition which was filed on behalf of her husband, the beneficiary. The record will be remanded.
 
The Director found that the evidence submitted by the petitioner was insufficient to establish by “clear and convincing evidence” that the petitioner has a bona fide marriage with the beneficiary.¿FN1º This standard of proof is applicable because the beneficiary married after the commencement of removal proceedings against him on December 18, 2002.¿FN2º Consequently, the requirements of sections 204(g) and 245(e)(3) and 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii) apply.
 
In addition to evidence of the legal termination of their prior marriages, the record contains evidence of a joint life for the petitioner and the beneficiary, such as their monthly rental agreement for “316 W.40th Street, San Bernardino, CA 92407;” the petitioner’s voter’s registration card, showing a joint residence; a joint Bank of America check; evidence of joint insurance through Farmer’s Insurance; and a notice of delinquent motor vehicle renewal sent to their “316 W.40th Street” residence. Despite this evidence which was submitted in response to the July 30, 2008 and September 5, 2008, requests for further evidence, the Notice of Intent to Deny (“NOID”) only mentioned the lease agreement and the proof of car insurance. The Director’s final decision likewise only referenced the Bank of America joint account and the joint automobile registration. Neither the NOID nor the final decision in this case mentioned the same evidence or all of the record evidence in arriving at the conclusion that there was not “clear and convincing” proof of the marital bona fides.
 
In order to ensure a correct outcome to the instant visa petition, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Director to verify the completeness of the record and reevaluate her legal and factual findings by taking into account all of the record evidence. On remand, both parties may submit additional evidence, including that evidence which has been submitted for the first time on appeal. See Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Obaigbena, 19 I&N Dec. 533 (BIA 1988) (this Board will not generally accept or consider evidence offered for the first time on appeal).
 
*2 In light of our remand, we decline to address the petitioner’s claim that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from her prior attorney of record, Mr. Gregory Olive. We will note, however, that her only evidence in support of a due process claim consists of a letter from the California State Bar Association which states that they have no record of attorney Olive on their membership list. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-526 (9th Cir. 2000) (Lozada requirements generally reasonable, but full compliance is not required if the record independently shows that the “ineffective assistance” claim is legitimate and substantial). See also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).¿FN3º
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Field Office Director for further consideration of the visa petition consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD

*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on September 15, 2008 in a final administrative order. Over a year later, on September 24, 2009, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen. See C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that former counsel failed to notify him of the Board’s September 15, 2008, decision, and requests that the Board reopen, or reissue, its earlier decision so that he can preserve his right to appeal before the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit. However, the respondent has produced no evidence in support of his argument, and the respondent has provided nothing to indicate that he acted with due diligence in claiming that equitable tolling applies to his reopening deadline. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248, 252-53 (3d Cir. 2005). Moreover, the respondent has not met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); we note, specifically, that he has not, inter alia, demonstrated how he has been prejudiced by any alleged untimely Circuit Court appeal. Id. Incidentally, the Board’s September 15, 2008, decision was mailed to the respondent’s attorney’s address of record as provided on his EOIR-27, and the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional circumstance which would persuade us to reopen the proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The pending motion will be, therefore, denied.

2009 WL 3818031 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA GUADALUPE ROJAS-DE GALLARDO
File: AXXX XX3 142 - San Diego, CA
October 30, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eudene Eunique-Valle, Esquire
*1 On August 20, 2009, the respondent, who is from Mexico, submitted a timely “Motion for Reconsideration”, concerning the Board’s July 21, 2009, decision. Section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act, as an alien who, at any time, knowingly encouraged, induced, assisted, abetted or aided any other alien to enter or try to enter the United States in violation of law. The Immigration Judge also denied an application for special rule cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Act. The respondent was represented before the Immigration Judge by Robin Chandler Carr.
 
Current counsel filed a Notice of Appeal with the Board on April 18, 2008, stating only that “¿tºhe Immigration Judge erred in finding respondent deportable because she was merely a passenger in the vehicle and she did not do any affirmative act in furtherance of alien smuggling.” Counsel was given the opportunity to file a brief to the Board, but no brief was filed. The Board properly noted when it dismissed her appeal on July 21, 2009, that the respondent challenged only the Immigration Judge’s finding that she is removable under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act. In finding that the respondent’s removability as an alien smuggler had been established by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence, the Board stated that:
Specifically contradicting the respondent’s contention in the Notice of Appeal that she took no affirmative action in furtherance of the smuggling of two minors from Mexico is the Record of Sworn Statement of the respondent taken at San Ysidro Port of Entry by an Immigration and Customs Enforcement Border Inspector (Exh. 11). The respondent stated that she contacted her daughter in Mexico, and made arrangements to have her granddaughter flown to Tijuana, where the respondent met her at the airport. The respondent stated that she had contacted her sister-in-law to help her smuggle her granddaughter into the United States, and that her sister-in-law agreed to help by providing her own United States citizen daughter’s birth certificate to use at the border. The respondent further indicated that she knew it was illegal to bring individuals into the United States without valid documents. By her own admissions, the respondent was more than just a passenger in the vehicle stopped at the port of entry, but rather was an active participant in arranging, and carrying out the plan to have her granddaughter illegally enter the United States.
 
 
*2 The respondent’s motion claims that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from Robin Chandler Carr, who represented her before the Immigration Judge. The respondent asserts that Carr did not assist her to file evidence in support of a claim to special rule cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Act (Respondent’s Mot., at 2-3; Respondent’s Affidavit). The motion claims that the attorney did not submit documentary evidence that the respondent had been subject to battery or extreme cruelty by her lawful permanent resident husband, Id; section 240A(b)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act. The respondent argues in a “Motion To Stay Deportation” that Carr erred by not objecting to the introduction of the sworn statement (removal hearing Exh. 11).
 
As noted, however, on appeal to the Board, while represented by current counsel, the respondent did not challenge the Immigration Judge’s denial of special rule cancellation of removal, and made no claim that she had received ineffective assistance of counsel from prior counsel. 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c)(1)(a motion to reopen will not be granted unless “the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing”); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006) (indicating that “¿aº motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied”). Moreover, the pending motion is unaccompanied by any evidence that would support a claim to special rule cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Act. Therefore, there is no reason to reconsider or reopen the respondent’s case concerning such relief.
 
The motion also seeks to have the Board reconsider its decision agreeing with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Act (Respondent’s Mot., at 3-4). The Board will not reconsider its July 21, 2009, decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra (a motion to reconsider must allege a material factual or legal error, assert that the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion, or argue a change in law). The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge found that she had been subject to battery by her husband, and the Immigration Judge and Board should have determined that she therefore was under duress when she acted as an alien smuggler (Respondent’s Mot., at 3-4). Contrary to this argument, in determining that the respondent was ineligible for special rule cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(2)(A) of the Act, the Immigration Judge did not conclude that the respondent had been subject to battery by her husband, but “… there is significantly conflicting testimony as to whether or not respondent was abused or subject to extreme cruelty by her spouse” (I.J. at 15). In finding that the respondent’s removability as an alien smuggler had been established by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence, the Board fully considered the argument that the respondent took no affirmative act in furtherance of alien smuggling, and properly rejected this argument based on the evidence presented in the case. The pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s “Motion for Reconsideration” is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s grant of a stay on October 21, 2009, pending consideration of the motion, is vacated in light of the Board’s order denying the “Motion for Reconsideration”.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NOORIA GARDIZY
File: AXXX XX2 322 - San Francisco, CA
October 29, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank P. Sprouls, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Afghanistan. She has resided in the United States since the 1980s and was a lawful permanent resident for many years. On March 17, 2004, the Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal and ordered her removed based on two theft convictions. On January 24, 2005, the Board issued its final administrative decision in this proceeding.
 
On December 2, 2005, the Board denied a motion to reopen, which was based on ineffective assistance of counsel, as well as changed country conditions. On May 20, 2009, the Ninth Circuit denied in part and granted in part the petition for review with respect to the motion, and remanded the record for further proceedings related to the respondent’s arguments involving changed country conditions. Gardizy v. Holder, No. 05-76969, 2009 WL 1396834 (9th Cir. May 20, 2009).¿FN1º
 
On August 27, 2009, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. The respondent argues, inter alia, that she still has a well-founded fear of future persecution based on her gender. In support of her motion, she has attached additional background information on the treatment of women in Afghanistan.
 
The most recent motion, as well as the motion we must reconsider on remand, are clearly untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, the time and number limitations do not apply to a motion to reopen based on changed country conditions. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii)(time and number limits do not apply to motions to reopen “¿tºo apply or reapply for asylum or withholding of deportation based on changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality … if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing”).
 
The motion will be granted. The respondent has not demonstrated changed country conditions or circumstances since the time of her 2004 hearing before the Immigration Judge with respect to the treatment of women in Afghanistan. However, after residing in the United States for so many years, a large part of the time as a lawful permanent resident, it appears that the respondent has adopted Western styles and attitudes. Further, according to her cancellation of removal application, she was divorced in the United States, and has several United States citizen children. Such changes may result in much worse circumstances for the respondent if she returns to Afghanistan. Further, she may have a claim of a well-founded fear of persecution on account of the fact that she is a woman who has adopted Western customs (a political statement). Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted and the record remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision, including any available relief.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the Ninth Circuit expressed serious concerns about current counsel’s representation. On remand, the Immigration Judge may wish to address these issues directly with the respondent so that she understands the significance of the problems.

2009 WL 3713330 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERT HERBERT TELLERIA
File: AXXX XX3 191 - Orlando, FL
October 29, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Stoller, Esquire¿FN1º
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Manuel Ramirez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)º - Controlled substance trafficker
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) Waiver
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s May 19, 2008, reissued decision denying his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1994). The appeal will be sustained in part and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Bolivia and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, does not dispute that he is removable as charged. Accordingly, the only issue on appeal is whether the respondent merits section 212(c) relief in discretion. We review such discretionary judgments de novo, but the findings of fact underlying such judgments are reviewed for clear error. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i)-(ii) (2008).
 
In a decision issued on August 20, 2007, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for section 212(c) relief in the exercise of discretion and ordered him removed to Bolivia. The respondent appealed that decision to this Board, but the appeal was dismissed as untimely in November 2007. The respondent then filed a motion with the Immigration Judge requesting, among other things, that the judge reissue his earlier decision in order to preserve the respondent’s appeal rights, which had been compromised by his attorney’s neglect. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had received ineffective assistance of counsel, and therefore he granted the respondent’s motion for the limited purpose of reissuing his August 20, 2007, decision.¿FN2º The respondent now appeals from that reissued decision.
 
In determining whether the respondent merits a favorable exercise of discretion, we must balance the adverse factors evidencing his undesirability as a permanent resident with the social and humane considerations presented in his behalf to determine whether the granting of section 212(c) relief appears in the best interests of this country. Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581, 584 (BIA 1978). As the Immigration Judge determined, significant negative factors militate against a favorable exercise of discretion in this case. The respondent is removable because he sustained a 1990 federal felony conviction for conspiracy to distribute cocaine. 21 U.S.C. §§ 841(a) and 846. Furthermore, the respondent has sustained three separate convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol (“DUI”). Two of those convictions occurred in quick succession in 1995. The third such conviction occurred in 2006, shortly after these removal proceedings commenced, but was based on an offense committed in 2005.
 
*2 As the Immigration Judge determined, the respondent has also adduced very substantial equities in support of his claim. The respondent has been a lawful permanent resident of the United States since infancy, a period of approximately 40 years. Further, he has been married to a United States citizen for nearly 20 years and has 3 minor children, all of whom are United States citizens. The respondent’s United States citizen parents also reside in his home. In short, the respondent’s whole life is in this country, and his family will experience significant emotional and economic hardship if he is removed. The respondent has been gainfully employed in the computer industry in recent years, and he and his wife own 2 vehicles and are mortgagors on a 5-bedroom house, but the respondent’s wife-who is employed as a registered nurse-would not have sufficient income to afford this property in the absence of the respondent’s financial contributions. These equities are “unusual and outstanding” within the meaning of our precedents.
 
We consider this to be a very close case. The respondent’s equities are very strong, and we cannot lightly disregard the hardship his family will experience in the event of removal. Although the respondent’s drug crime is a serious adverse factor, he claims to have been a minor player in the drug conspiracy and to have been motivated to commit the offense out of economic desperation. (Tr. 16-17). Furthermore, the respondent claims that he was not driving drunk when he was arrested for DUI in 2005, and that he was only convicted of that crime because the arresting officers in Texas “were not very reasonable” and failed to administer a Breathalyzer or urine test and because his criminal defense attorney encouraged him to plead guilty despite his innocence. (Tr. 70-73).
 
The Immigration Judge was not obliged to accept the respondent’s mitigating account of his conduct. The problem we face on appeal, however, is that the Immigration Judge’s credibility assessment is internally contradictory. In his decision of August 20, 2007, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was “entitled to a positive finding of credibility.” (I.J. Dec. at 17). Yet after making that positive credibility finding, the Immigration Judge proceeded to express serious (and understandable) misgivings about the plausibility of many of the respondent’s claims. (I.J. Dec. at 17-18). Furthermore, in denying relief, the Immigration Judge appeared largely to discredit the respondent’s account of his activities in connection with the drug conspiracy and the 2005 DUI offense. (I.J. Dec. at 17-18, 20-21). We cannot reconcile this inconsistency. The respondent’s account is either credible or implausible; it cannot be both.
 
If the respondent is entitled to a positive credibility finding with respect to his accounts of the 1990 drug crime and 2005 DUI offense, then he may well have demonstrated to our satisfaction that he merits relief. However, if those accounts are not credible, then his continued presence in this country would likely not be in the best interests of the United States. We cannot make such credibility determinations on appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (iv). Accordingly, we will remand this matter to the Immigration Judge for entry of a new decision with respect to the respondent’s credibility. Until such a determination is made, we have no basis for appellate review. Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002).
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is sustained in part and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent the foregoing decision.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Notice of Entry of Appearance (Form EOIR-27) filed in this case is signed by David Stoller, Esquire, and therefore Mr. Stoller is the respondent’s attorney of record. However, the respondent’s appellate brief was filed over the signature of an attorney, apparently from Mr. Stoller’s law firm, who has not filed a Notice of Entry of Appearance in this matter. Because Mr. Stoller is the respondent’s attorney of record, the filing of a brief by a different attorney from his law firm is not permitted. Board of Immigration Appeals Practice Manual, §§ 2.3(d)-(f) and 3.3(b)(ii). Therefore the brief is subject to rejection. To prevent prejudice to the respondent we will accept the brief in the exercise of discretion, but we expect greater compliance with Board procedures in the future.

2

However, that issue is distinct from the question presented in the respondent’s appeal, i.e., whether he merits section 212(c) relief in discretion, and therefore the DHS needed to raise it by means of an appeal. The DHS has not filed a notice of appeal, and its appellate pleading was submitted long after expiration of the 30-day appeal period. Thus, the merits of the respondent’s motion to reopen are not properly before us. We express no further opinion with regard to that issue.

IN RE: SISILIA KAFOVALU PAKALANI A.K.A. SISILIA KAFOVALU SAKE A.K.A. SISILIA KAFOVALU FIFITANIUA
File: AXXX XX4 133 - Honolulu, HI
October 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel G. Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia A. Beattie
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of material fact
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a 30-year-old native and citizen of Tonga, appealed the Immigration Judge’s July 2, 2009, decision denying her motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a), based on an approved visa petition filed by her mother, and a waiver of inadmissibility. On April 13, 2009, the Immigration Judge found her removable, as charged, and granted her voluntary departure under section 240B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. Her application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(i), and accompanying waivers, was withdrawn and she was granted voluntary departure. The respondent did not appeal. Through a new attorney she filed a motion to reopen on June 10, 2009, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel at the hearing. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent entered the United States on or about February 18, 1997, as the unmarried child of a United States citizen, her mother. She was, in fact, already married on April 27, 1995. The respondent’s mother filed a new visa petition for the respondent as the married daughter of a United States citizen on or about September 13, 1998. This petition was approved and is now current. The Notice to Appear was issued on August 27, 2007. At the hearing it was revealed that the respondent had a child born in Tonga on March 18, 1999. The respondent now concedes she is not eligible for adjustment under section 245(i), because she was not in the United States on December 21, 2000. 8 U.S.C. § 245(i)(1)(C). The respondent admitted she had left the United States after her initial fraudulent entry. At the next hearing counsel withdrew the applications and accepted voluntary departure. The respondent contended in her motion to reopen that her counsel was ineffective by not pursuing an application under 245(a) and a waiver under 212(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(i), and that the record should be reopened and remanded. The Immigration Judge found no ineffective assistance of counsel had been proven, that she had not shown eligibility for relief upon reopening, and that the motion should be denied in discretion.
 
*2 The respondent has not complied substantially, or in part, with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). She stated in her affidavit that she agreed to the withdrawal of her applications in return for voluntary departure which would allow her to return to the United States. She has not demonstrated she informed prior counsel that she intended to make a claim of ineffective assistance to the Board. The respondent has not shown she filed any complaint with the relevant authorities. Moreover, ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding is a denial of due process only “¿iºf the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from presenting his case.” Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491, 499-500 (9th Cir. 1986) (quoting Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). See also Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent must show that the conduct of former counsel was so egregious that it rendered her hearing unfair. Matter of B-B-, 23 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998).
 
It appears that the deficiencies in the record are attributable to the actions of the respondent. She admittedly lied on her applications by omitting any reference to her youngest child. She did not admit she had left the United States. Her overall credibility is in question. We find that there has been no showing that the respondent was prejudiced by her prior attorney’s performance. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 371 F.3d 771 (11th Cir. 2004). The preservation of her ability to voluntarily depart was a tactical decision with potential benefit to the respondent. As she conceded removability under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), as an alien present in violation of the Act ¿now 237(a)(1)(B) of the Actº, and she is not eligible for adjustment of status, the failure to pursue a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(H), for her admission by fraud is moot. In these circumstances, we find the respondent’s due process rights have not been violated.
 
The respondent has asserted that she wishes a remand for consideration of her adjustment application and a waiver of inadmissibility. We uphold the finding that the respondent’s motion does not set forth a prima facie case of eligibility for relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992). The respondent’s last entry was in 2003, but she has not shown that she was inspected or paroled into the United States. See Form I-601 Respondent’s Trial Brief, Exh. 9. Further, she was in unlawful immigration status on the date of filing her adjustment application and failed to maintain continuously a lawful status since entry. See 245(c)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1245(c)(2). Therefore, she is not eligible for adjustment of status.
 
*3 It was not necessary for the Immigration Judge to address the request to withdraw voluntary departure because the respondent never perfected her request for voluntary departure by filing the bond set by the Immigration Judge and she filed a post-hearing motion to reopen. Therefore, her voluntary departure grant lapsed and the order of removal became effective. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3) and (4).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: NDEY ARAM NDOW JOBE ABDOULIE SOWE
Files: AXXX XX8 347 - Detroit, MI
AXXX XX2 797

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Caridad Pastor Cardinale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tara L. Harris
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated August 20, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondents’ motion to reopen their removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The respondents, natives and citizens of Gambia, filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) was personally served on the respondents and their counsel on March 12, 2008 (Exh. 3). The NOH contained notice of a July 9, 2008, Master hearing and a June 29, 2009, Individual hearing. The respondents failed to appear at their July 9, 2008, hearing, and were ordered removed in absentia on that date.
 
On July 21, 2008, the respondents, through counsel, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, in which they contend that they did not receive notice of their “June 29, 2008,” hearing.¿FN1º Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, page 2. Attached to the motion were the respondents’ NOH and the Immigration Judge’s July 9, 2008, in absentia order. On August 20, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondents and their counsel had been personally served with the NOH, and they had not explained their failure to attend their hearing. The Immigration Judge also noted that no affidavit accompanied the motion. The respondents timely appealed that decision.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he or she did not receive proper notice of the time and place of the hearing, or because he or she was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his or her own.
 
While on appeal, the respondents have retained new counsel and have filed a “motion to reopen” based upon ineffective assistance of former counsel. This is essentially a motion to remand. Specifically, the respondents allege that former counsel advised them not to appear at their July 9, 2008, hearing because he would be filing their applications by mail. This claim is supported by an affidavit from the lead respondent. Her affidavit also restates her desire to apply for asylum and withholding of removal from Gambia. The Department of Homeland Security states in its brief on appeal that not all the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) have been satisfied, and no prejudice from any alleged ineffective assistance of counsel has been shown. However, we find that there has been Lozada compliance in this case, and that prejudice has been shown, as the respondents’ non-appearance at their hearing deprived them of the opportunity to present their persecution claim.
 
*2 Given all these circumstances, we find that exceptional circumstances existed excusing the respondents’ failure to appear for the July 9, 2008, hearing. We will therefore sustain the respondents’ appeal, reopen the proceedings, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondents to apply for relief from removal. See generally Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407 (6th Cir. 2003) (alien’s attorney’s failure to notify alien of asylum hearing, and subsequent fraud in advising alien that case was still pending, resulting in alien being ordered removed in absentia, was type of exceptional circumstances that could warrant recision of order of removal if alien filed motion to reopen within 180 days of date of order of removal); Denko v. INS, 351 F.3d 717 (6th Cir. 2003) (ineffective assistance of counsel in a deportation proceeding will rise to the level of a due-process violation under the Fifth Amendment if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case).
 
Consequently, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

There was no hearing scheduled for June 29, 2008. This appears to be a typo, as the hearing was scheduled for June 29, 2009.

IN RE: KSENIA VLADIMIROVNA RANGEL, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY COREY FRANK RANGEL, PETITIONER
File: AXXX XX4 582 - Jacksonville, FL
October 23, 2009
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda Hopkins
Associate Regional Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
 
*1 In a decision dated December 4, 2008, the Field Office Director (“Director”) denied the visa petition filed by the United States citizen petitioner for the beneficiary as his wife. The petitioner appealed from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed. The request for oral argument is denied.
 
In a notice of intent to deny (“NOID”) dated October 17, 2008, the Director informed the petitioner of discrepancies that arose in the parties’ responses to questions at interviews conducted by the. Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) on January 5, 2007. By means of the NOID, the petitioner was given the opportunity to provide additional evidence to rebut the inconsistencies. The NOID allotted the petitioner 30 days within which to submit rebuttal evidence. In visa petition proceedings, the petitioner has the burden of establishing eligibility for the benefit sought. See Matter of Brantigan, 11 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 1966). The petitioner must prove the required elements by a preponderance of the evidence. See Matter of Pazandeh, 19 I&N Dec. 884 (BIA 1989). Where the bona fides of a marriage are challenged, the petitioner must present documentary or testimonial evidence to show that the marriage was not entered into for the primary purpose of evading the immigration laws. See Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975).
 
We find correct the Director’s determination in the December 2008 decision that the petitioner failed to respond to the October 2008 NOID and, consequently, failed to explain the cited interview discrepancies and failed to submit probative documentary evidence of a shared life so as to overcome the doubts raised by the discrepancies.
 
On appeal, the petitioner argues that he did not receive the NOID. However, the NOID reflects that it was sent to the petitioner at his last correct address as listed on the visa petition, 705 Water Oak Drive, Panama City Beach, Florida 32408. Although the petitioner contends that he changed his address, the record does not contain a change of address form or other proof as required to substantiate that he notified the DHS regarding the new address.¿FN1º Moreover, the NOID indicates that a copy was sent to the attorney who represented the petitioner before the DHS. Further, the petitioner acknowledges on appeal that the parties were provided actual notice regarding the NOID by the attorney through an October 2008 telephone call. To the extent the petitioner is claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We agree with the Director that a denial of the petition is justified under these circumstances, as the record does not demonstrate that the petitioner met his burden of establishing a bona fide marital relationship.
 
*2 The petitioner may, of course, file a new visa petition on the beneficiary’s behalf that is supported by competent evidence that the beneficiary is entitled to the status sought, that of his spouse, under the immigration laws.
 
Accordingly, we will dismiss the appeal.¿FN2º
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD

The Form I-130 visa petition includes instructions, which specify that, if a petitioner changes his or her address while a petition or application is pending, he or she is to submit a Form AR-11 Alien’s Change of Address Card to the appropriate U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services office or is to submit the change online at www.uscis.gov.

2

With the appeal, the petitioner has submitted new evidence. We observe that this Board will not accept evidence offered for the first time on appeal. See Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Obaigbena, 19 I&N Dec. 533 (BIA 1988).

IN RE: MIGUEL ERAZO-HERNANDEZ A.K.A. MIGUEL SANCHEZ-ERAZO
File: AXXX XX0 048 - Los Angeles, CA
