IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mike Razi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lee Crystal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on February 20, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 26, 2006. The Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged, ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). The Immigration Judge also determined that even if the respondent were eligible for adjustment of status, the respondent should be denied that relief as a matter of discretion, given such factors as the respondent’s extensive criminal history and his history of violating this country’s immigration laws.
 
On February 3, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings in which he claims he received deficient performance of counsel from his attorney during proceedings before the Board. Specifically, he complains that his attorney failed to send his appeal brief to the correct address and so it was not considered by the Board on appeal. He states that he learned about the attorney’s error during proceedings on his petition for review before the court of appeals. During those proceedings, the Government argued that the respondent failed to exhaust his administrative remedies in that he failed to file an appeal brief raising certain issues to the Board.
 
In addition to other evidence, the respondent has attached to his motion a copy of the appeal brief that was intended to be filed with the Board but that was sent to the wrong address. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion to reopen. The DHS argues that the respondent has not met the requirements for deficient performance of counsel claims as set out in Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
At the outset, we note that the Attorney General has recently vacated the decision in Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (providing that the Board and the Immigration Judges shall continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Given the totality of the circumstances as presented in the record, we will reopen proceedings sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 (a), vacate our decision of February 20, 2008, and again consider the respondent’s appeal in light of the arguments advanced in his appeal brief. Upon consideration of the appeal brief, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal for the following reasons.
 
*2 We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See Matter of SH-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. See Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
First, we conclude that there is no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s factual findings. Next, considering the issue of discretion de novo, we conclude that even assuming the respondent were eligible for a waiver under section 212(h) of the Act and for adjustment of status, he should be denied adjustment as a matter of discretion. As the Immigration Judge correctly found, the record establishes that the respondent has a long criminal history and a history of violating immigration law that includes two prior deportations and subsequent unlawful re-entries (Tr. at 39, 46-50; Exh. 3; I.J. at 2-3).¿FN1 º Although the respondent has fathered three children, he did not benefit at his hearing from any testimony from his children or the mother of his children in which they explained the respondent’s role in their lives (I.J. at 4). Additionally, while the respondent complains that the Immigration Judge failed to take into account the fact that the respondent has lived in the United States since he was 12 years old, it is a matter of record that the respondent failed to actually establish his age of entry during his hearing, and there was varying evidence presented on this issue (Tr. at 43-45, 54-55, 58-59, 65, 73; I.J. at 3-4).
 
Even considering the respondent’s time in the United States, his three United States citizen children, his lack of criminal convictions since 1999, and his recent work history, the respondent has simply not presented factors that outweigh his criminal history and history of immigration violations. The grant of adjustment of status is a matter of discretion and administrative grace, and the respondent has the burden to show that discretion should be exercised on his behalf. See Matter of Leung, 16 I&N Dec. 12 (BIA 1976); Matter of Arai, 13 I&N Dec. 494 (BIA 1970). The respondent has failed to meet this burden. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: Proceedings are reopened pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision of February 20, 2008, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision is dismissed.
 
*3 Frederick D. Hess

IN RE: GLENDA CANA GALIEGOS

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 19, 2008, decision, denying her motion to reopen, except to grant voluntary departure.¿FN1º The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear at the hearing on April 17, 2007. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2009). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to reopen, and in opening for the sole purpose of granting voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge issued a decision briefly concluding that the respondent had not established prima facie eligibility for any relief from removal and had not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to lodge an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge did not specifically address whether the motion was filed in a timely manner, how the respondent had failed to comply with Matter of Lozada and why the respondent’s contentions regarding her assertion that she was eligible for adjustment of status or cancellation of removal were incorrect. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (the Immigration Judge’s decision must fully explain the reasons for denial of a motion to reopen deportation proceedings). See also Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec, 462 (BIA 2002) (holding that because the Board’s fact finding ability on appeal is limited, it is important for Immigration Judges to include in clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and are in compliance with controlling law). In addition, the respondent did not seek reopening to apply for voluntary departure. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s grant order reopening to this relief was in error. Accordingly, the order granting voluntary departure is vacated. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings and to enter a new decision. We express no opinion on the merits of the motion to reopen.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent also submitted a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge 1 day before filing her appeal with this Board. The record contains no decision on this motion. However, upon filing her appeal with the Board the Immigration Judge was divested of jurisdiction over the motion to reconsider. See generally 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(b)(2), 1003.2(c)(4), 1003.23(b)(1).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  On Reconsideration IN RE: JOSEPH KOMENAN A.K.A. ABDOULIE SAMBA, BIA, May 24, 2010
2009 WL 3448155 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSEPH KOMENAN¿FN1º
File: AXXX XX2 684 - Atlanta, GA
October 13, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laine S. Posel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Renae M. Hansell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2008, decision denying his motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has requested that the Immigration Judge’s decision be affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard, and review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On July 11, 2008, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to the Ivory Coast, and his applications for relief from removal deemed abandoned when he failed to appear for his hearing. On August 8, 2008, the respondent, through attorney Shamsideen (Sam) O. Laguda (“Laguda”), filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, claiming that his prior counsel, attorney Michael Wallace (“Wallace”), misinformed him that the date of his hearing was July 17, 2008, rather than July 11, 2008. On August 20, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied this motion to reopen. On September 29, 2008, the respondent, again through attorney Laguda, filed a motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s August 20, 2008, decision. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider on October 20, 2008, and it is from this decision that the respondent, who has retained new counsel, has appealed.
 
On appeal, the respondent has not claimed, nor can we conclude, that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reconsider for the various reasons provided in the October 20, 2008, decision: that the motion to reconsider was untimely filed; the motion did not specify errors of law and fact in the prior decision denying the motion to reopen; and, in the alternative, the respondent did not demonstrate extraordinary circumstances warranting reconsideration and reopening of his proceedings or his prima facie eligibility for relief from removal. Based on the record before him on October 20, 2008, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider.
 
Rather than challenging the propriety of the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2008, decision, the respondent on appeal has presented evidence in support of a newly raised claim of ineffective assistance by attorney Laguda, who filed the unsuccessful motions to reopen and reconsider with the Immigration Court. We construe the respondent’s submissions on appeal as a motion to remand and reopen his proceedings based on new evidence. This motion will be denied.
 
*2 Although the respondent has alleged a claim of ineffective assistance against attorney Laguda, the respondent, through current undersigned counsel, has failed to comply with the filing requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), against attorney Wallace, who the respondent claimed misinformed him of his hearing date, caused his failure to appear, and caused his applications for relief from removal to be deemed abandoned. There is no evidence that the respondent filed a complaint against attorney Wallace with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and he has not explained why one was not filed. There is no verifiable evidence that attorney Wallace was informed of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel against him and was given an opportunity to respond.¿FN2º As found by the Immigration Judge in his previous decisions, the respondent has not established that ineffective assistance of counsel caused his failure to appear for his July 11, 2008, hearing, such that would constitute exceptional circumstances justifying the reopening of the proceedings and rescission of the in absentia removal order. On appeal, the respondent, who is now represented by new counsel, has still failed to establish a claim that ineffective assistance of counsel caused his failure to appear for his July 11, 2008, hearing. Accordingly, notwithstanding the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance against Laguda with respect to the motions filed, he has not established on appeal that he was prejudiced by attorney Laguda’s conduct. The respondent has not established that reopening of his proceedings is warranted. The following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand and reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

In his submissions, the respondent has indicated that his true, complete and correct name is “Abdoulie Samba.”

The record, however, does not show that this letter was filed with the Immigration Court. Not only is the letter’s origin unclear, but the letter is likewise unnotarized, unsworn, and unsigned by attorney Wallace.

IN RE: EUGENE OSAZE JOSEPH

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Vanessa G. Pocock
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Nigeria. On May 27, 2008, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On August 5, 2008, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen, arguing that his proceeding should be reopened due to ineffective assistance by his former attorney. On March 9, 2009, the Board denied the respondent’s motion pursuant to Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The Attorney General’s decision in Matter of Compean has now been vacated. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). On March 27, 2009, the Seventh Circuit dismissed the respondent’s petition for review from our May 27, 2008, decision, Joseph v. Holder, No. 08-2570, 2009 WL 792751 (7 th Cir. 2009).
 
On March 25, 2009, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider our March 9, 2009, decision. In addition, the respondent has attached a number of exhibits to the motion so we will also consider his motion as a motion to reopen, but only to the extent the evidence raises new issues, as opposed to supplementing deficiencies in his original motion to reopen, which we denied on March 9, 2009. Mungongo v. Gonzales, 479 F.3d 531, 535 (7th Cir. 2007) (since argument not presented to the Board as a basis for reopening the alien’s removal proceedings at the time of her motion to reopen, Board’s failure to consider such information could not constitute a defect in the process by which it decided to deny the alien’s motion to reopen).
 
With respect to reconsideration, the motion is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent has not identified a material error of fact or law as of the time that our decision was rendered. Mungongo v. Gonzales, supra: Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Further, although Matter of Compean has been vacated, we find that under the pre-Compean standards, the respondent still has not demonstrated prejudice with respect to the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of the respondent’s request for a waiver under section 212(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), or our affirmance of that denial. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 577 F.2d 10 (1 Cir. 1988). See Jezierski v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 886, 891 (7th Cir. 2008)(query is whether proceeding might have different outcome if alien represented by competent counsel). With respect to withholding of removal, the respondent has not provided sufficient evidence or demonstrated how he might be entitled to such relief, and, as such, has not demonstrated prejudice.
 
*2 With respect to the additional evidence attached to the motion, most of it is an attempt to supplement the respondent’s original motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and, as such, is not properly before the Board. Mungongo v. Gonzales, supra. The respondent also asserts (not in his motion), but included as attachments, that his first attorney on appeal before the Board, Mr. Ivan Yacub, provided ineffective assistance of counsel because he failed to file a motion to remand during the pendency of the appeal. The motion to reopen is denied as number-barred with respect to the additional evidence involving Mr. Yacub because the respondent has not demonstrated why these facts were not included in his original motion, and there has been no showing that the evidence was previously unavailable. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Mungongo v. Gonzales, supra, 479 F.3d at 535 n. 1. Further, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice because he hired a new attorney, Paschal O. Nwokocha, who filed the motion to reopen and remand during the pendency of the appeal and presented the evidence and arguments that Mr. Yacub was going to present, which motion the Board rejected. Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice with respect to Mr. Yacub’s actions.
 
For these reasons, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

IN RE: ERROL EBANKS
File: AXXX XX8 536 - Dallas, TX
September 30, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonette Russell, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of Jamaica. On October 7, 2003, the Immigration Judge administratively closed these proceedings pursuant to a request of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). On June 14, 2006, the DHS filed a motion to recalendar the proceedings. The Immigration Judge granted the motion and mailed the Notice of Hearing to Michael J. Williams, Esquire. The respondent did not appear for his hearing, and the Immigration Judge ordered his removal from the United States in absentia on September 5, 2006. On November 5, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen arguing that lack of notice and “exceptional circumstances” prevented his appearance at the hearing. The DHS indicated in response that it did not oppose reopening. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on January 28, 2008. On March 18, 2008, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reconsider arguing that he was not adequately notified of the hearing. The appeal will be sustained.
 
As stated, the Notice of Hearing in these proceedings was mailed to Michael J. Williams, Esquire. Documentation contained in the record reflects Mr. Williams as having represented the respondent before the DHS. Mr. Williams’ name is also handwritten on a Notice of Hearing dated February 18, 2003, that also contains the crossed name of Deborah M. Ferdinand. However, the record does not contain any evidence of Mr. Williams having filed a Notice of Appearance or having represented the respondent before the Immigration Court. It is thus unclear regarding why Mr. Williams was treated as the respondent’s representative. The fact that Mr. Williams represented the respondent before the DHS does not render him the respondent’s attorney of record in proceedings before the Immigration Court.
 
The Immigration Judge was correct to note that notice is proper if served on an alien’s counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5. However, the record contains no indication that Mr. Williams entered an appearance on behalf of the respondent. We therefore are unable to conclude that the respondent was adequately notified of his hearing, even though the Notice of Hearing may have been served on Mr. Williams. We find under these circumstances that the respondent did not receive proper notification of his September 5, 2006, hearing. We therefore need not address whether the respondent established that he was also the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and these proceedings are reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RESENDE AFONSO DA CUNHA A.K.A. RESENDE ALFONSO CUNHA
File: AXXX XX2 020 - Boston, MA
September 30, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandra A. Strempel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bernard Menendez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On March 24, 2009, the respondent, a native and citizen of Brazil, filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i).
 
The motion was not filed within the statutory and regulatory 90-day time limit for filing a motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal). A final administrative order was issued in this case on July 20, 2006.¿FN1º
 
The respondent’s motion is premised, in part, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former counsel, Mr. Jose Del Castillo.¿FN2º The respondent has substantially complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) asserts that the motion is untimely filed and that the respondent has failed to establish that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his case so as to equitably toll the 90-day motions deadline. See Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d 33, 37 (1st Cir. 2009) (if the doctrine of equitable tolling is available, it applies only to those who exercise due diligence).
 
In reply, the respondent, by and through counsel, asserts that the motion is timely filed because the First Circuit’s decision is the final administrative order. See Respondent’s Reply to DHS’ Memorandum in Opposition to Motion to Reopen at 3, filed with the Board on May 28, 2009. The respondent is incorrect. The Board issues the final administrative order, not the federal court. Moreover, the filing of a petition for review in federal court does not extend the 90-day motions deadline. See Matter of Susma, 22 I&N Dec. 947 (BIA 1999).
 
Although the respondent is misguided regarding the timeliness of the instant motion, we find that he acted diligently once he was aware of Mr. Del Castillo’s misconduct.¿FN3º Mr. Del Castillo represented the respondent before the Immigration Court, this Board and the First Circuit. He failed to submit documents to the Immigration Judge that were available and material to the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act. When he filed a motion to remand with this Board supported by these documents, it was properly denied because the documents were previously available. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen shall not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing). Because of Mr. Del Castillo’s misconduct, this material evidence was never considered by the Immigration Judge or the Board. Significantly, the respondent’s instant motion is supported by correspondence indicating that the Department of Labor accepted the respondent’s labor certification as properly filed on April 27, 2001. Had this document been presented to the Immigration Judge, the respondent would have been able to establish his eligibility for 245(i) treatment. The respondent has demonstrated prejudice resulting from Mr. Castillo’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 In light of the respondent’s diligence in pursuing his case once he had new counsel and Mr. Castillo’s obvious prejudicial misconduct, we will reopen these proceedings on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The record will be remanded to afford the respondent the opportunity to apply for adjustment under section 245(i) of the Act.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROXANA MARIBEL MAGANA-MAGANA
File: AXXX XX6 728 - Los Angeles, CA
September 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Cruz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lily C. Hsu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of El Salvador. She was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on May 14, 2007, after failing to appear for a hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on February 7, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied it on March 28, 2008, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that an “exceptional circumstance” prevented her from appearing for her heating. She indicates in her declaration that she was unable to afford to hire an attorney. She indicates that she was referred to a non-attorney supposedly versed in immigration law. According to the respondent, the non-attorney advised her not to appear for her hearing because she was not eligible for relief from removal, and she would be apprehended by immigration authorities if she appeared. The respondent argues that she is the victim of professional negligence. She further requests the Board to equitably toll the period for seeking reopening and reopen and remand these proceedings for a hearing.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit recognizes that an alien may be denied due process if he or she receives negligent advice from an individual who falsely purports to be an attorney. See Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). The alien must, however, establish that he suffered prejudice from the conduct of a non-attorney, she was unaware of the fraud, and she remained ignorant of it without any fault or want of diligence or care on her part. See Lopez v. INS, supra.
 
In the instant case, the respondent admits in her declaration that there was no misrepresentation that the non-attorney was licensed to practice law. Under these circumstances, we do not find that the respondent received what may be tantamount to ineffective assistance to counsel. See, e.g., Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that there is a “crucial difference” between ineffective assistance of counsel and ineffective assistance of an immigration consultant); see also Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943, 946 (9th Cir. 1999) (concluding that the erroneous advice of an immigration consultant does not constitute an exceptional circumstance requiring reopening of the proceedings). The respondent avoided her hearing based on a non-attorney’s advice despite being notified to appear and of the consequences for failing to do so. It is inappropriate to equitably toll the 180-day period for filing her motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion as untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 3250358 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARY GRACE BALMEO
File: AXXX XX8 889 - San Francisco, CA
September 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Judith Goodman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the respondent’s motion. In our decision dated December 29, 2008, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which found her removable and denied her request for a continuance. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on July 14, 2009, is untimely. However, the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel. In Matter ofCompean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
The respondent has met the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and has adequately demonstrated prejudice. See Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004) (to show prejudice, the alien only needs to show that he has plausible grounds for relief). A visa petition filed by the respondent’s United States citizen spouse on her behalf was approved on April 10, 2008. However, former counsel did not file a motion to remand, even though the respondent’s appeal remained pending before the Board until December 29, 2008. Counsel also failed to timely submit a motion to reopen. Either a motion to remand or a motion to reopen, timely filed, could have presented a plausible basis for granting the motion. Finally, the respondent has shown due diligence in pursuing this matter. See Sun v. Mukasey, 555 F.3d 802, 806 (9th Cir. 2009) (concluding that the alien was entitled to equitable tolling where she acted with due diligence).
 
Thus, we will equitably toll the time limit, grant reopening, and remand the case to allow the respondent to pursue an adjustment application. Because we will grant the respondent’s motion, her stay request has been rendered moot.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 3250380 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DANIEL MARTINEZ A.K.A. MARTINEZ DANIEL
File: AXXX XX0 299 - New York, NY
September 24, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew L. Guadagno, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated July 9, 2008. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on July 29, 1998, pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The facts culminating in this appeal are as follows: The respondent, a lawful permanent resident, was personally served with the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) on May 29, 1998, upon applying for readmission after a trip abroad on the basis of his criminal record comprised of two narcotics and one fraud conviction (Exhs. 1, 3). The Notice to Appear in the record of proceeding directed the respondent to appear at the Immigration Court located at 26 Federal Plaza, Room 10000, New York, NY 10278 on July 29, 1998 (Exh. 1). However, the Immigration Court address on the Notice to Appear that was served upon the respondent, although otherwise identical to the Notice to Appear in the record of proceeding, did not indicate the room number at which the respondent was directed to appear (I.J. at 2).¿FN1º The respondent failed to appear as directed and was ordered removed in absentia on July 29, 1998.
 
Nothing was heard again from the respondent until May 13, 2008, when the respondent filed the underlying motion to reopen the record of proceeding and rescind the in absentia order of removal (I.J. at 1; Respondent’s M.T.R.). In the motion to reopen the respondent presented two contradictory theories of relief. First, the respondent argued (and renews his argument on appeal) that his failure to appear was due to deficient notice resulting from the incomplete Immigration Court address indicated on the Notice to Appear that was served upon him on May 29, 1998 (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s M.T.R. at 4-6; Respondent’s Br. at 7). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (2009). Second, the respondent argued (and renews his argument on appeal) that his failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances” created by the ineffective assistance of the counsel (or individual falsely representing himself as legal counsel) he retained to represent him in these proceedings (I.J. at 1-2; Respondent’s M.T.R. at 6-9; Respondent’s Br. at 6-7). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (e)(1) of the Act (defining “exceptional circumstances”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996) (“¿Wºe note that one must look to the “totality of circumstances” to resolve this issue of exceptional circumstances.”). However, the Immigration Judge rejected each theory and denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, and this appeal ensued.
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge on both accounts. First, the respondent’s deficient notice theory based on the incomplete Immigration Court address on the Notice to Appear is baseless because the respondent, by his own admission in the affidavit he submitted in support of the motion to reopen, never went to the New York Immigration Court or otherwise attempted to appear at the July 29, 1998 hearing (Respondent’s M.T.R. at Exh. D, page 2). Thus, even assuming that the minor discrepancy between the address on the Notice to Appear in the record of proceeding and the address on the Notice to Appear served upon the respondent were sufficiently significant to cause a delay in finding the actual hearing room, that would not amount to a failure of notice, but at most would provide an excuse for a late arrival. The respondent cannot demonstrate any harm resulting from the discrepancy, however, because he never attempted to appear for the July 29, 1998, hearing as directed.
 
Second, while the respondent’s allegation that his failure to appear at the July 29, 1998, hearing was due to the fact that the former counsel misinformed him that the hearing had been continued would constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, we find no clear error in the refusal of the Immigration Judge to fully credit those allegations, for the reasons provided in the July 9, 2008, written decision (I.J. at 1-2). See Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008) (“We now join our sister circuits in concluding that, under BIA precedent applicable to the pre-1996 version of the INA as well as its current iteration, a lawyer’s inaccurate advice to his client concerning an immigration hearing date can constitute ‘exceptional circumstances’ excusing the alien’s failure to appear at a deportation hearing ¿º and meriting the reopening of an in absentia deportation order ¿º.” (citing Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798, 801-02 (5th Cir. 2007); Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 937-38 (9th Cir. 2003); Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54, 59 n.4 (2d Cir. 2005)); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact in this regard are clearly erroneous because the Immigration Judge “fail¿edº to consider” the respondent’s affidavit (Respondent’s Br. at 5). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(1); see also United States v. National Ass’n of Real Estate Bds., 339 U.S. 485, 495 (1950) (stating that a factual finding is not “clearly erroneous” merely because there are two permissible views of the evidence); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002) (stating that the Board must defer to the factual determinations of an Immigration Judge in the absence of clear error). However, we would point out that the Immigration Judge specifically compared the documentary evidence presented (as well as not presented) in support of the motion to reopen with the statements in the respondent’s affidavit and reasonably concluded that the documentary evidence raised significant questions and did not adequately corroborate the respondent’s affidavit (I.J. at 1-2; Respondent’s Br. at 5).
 
*3 Finally, even were we inclined to agree with the respondent that the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact are clearly erroneous, and, again, we are not, the respondent’s motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances would still be untimely, and the respondent’s failure to take any action in the interest of his case from the entry of the in absentia order on July 29, 1998, until he retained new counsel and filed the underlying motion to reopen on May 13, 2008, would preclude application of the equitable tolling doctrine developed by the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit in Iavorsky v. INS, supra, because the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge’s written “addendum” decision is not paginated.


2009 WL 3250380 (BIA)

2009 WL 3250414 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONIO RODRIGUEZ-DE LA CRUZ
File: AXXX XX4 451 - San Antonio, TX
September 23, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriel S. Perez, Esquire¿FN1º
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 12, 2009. The Board affirmed a decision of an Immigration Judge, who found that the respondent, who has been convicted of knowingly and willfully aiding and abetting the illegal entry of aliens, had abandoned the right to file an application for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(a). In a timely motion to reopen filed on May 5, 2009, the respondent blames former counsel Jose S. Tellez for his cancellation application being deemed abandoned.
 
The motion is unaccompanied by a detailed affidavit from the respondent that would support his claims against Tellez. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)(among other requirements not satisfied by the respondent, ineffective assistance of counsel claim must be supported by detailed affidavit).¿FN2º Moreover, at the February 4, 2008, removal hearing, the Immigration Judge remarked that, despite Tellez saying that the failure to submit the application was “partly my fault”, Tr. at 14, the respondent himself was also to blame for the application being deemed waived, in that he had been advised about the requirements for submitting an application for relief (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 16, 19). The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 3250405 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERNST RAMPONEAU
File: AXXX XX4 105 - New York, NY
September 23, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew L. Guadagno, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On December 18, 2008, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his application for a waiver of inadmissability under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen the proceedings. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We will grant the motion.
 
Based on the respondent’s testimony and evidence at his removal hearing, the Immigration Judge found that he could not determine whether the respondent maintained a lawful domicile in the United States as a permanent resident for at least 7 consecutive years preceding the filing of the application, and he concluded that the respondent failed to demonstrate statutory eligibility for a section 212(c) waiver. The Board found no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding of fact, and we accordingly affirmed his decision.
 
In his motion, the respondent claims that his former attorney’s mishandling of his case resulted in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the section 212(c) waiver. Specifically, he asserts that his former attorney failed to properly prepare him for the removal hearing, and he failed to solicit available evidence from the respondent in support of his application for the waiver. See Motion to Reopen at 14. In support of his claim that he is returning to a lawful unrelinquished domicile of 7 consecutive years, the respondent has now submitted a copy of his passport bearing exit and entry stamps, as well as sworn statements from friends and relatives. He has also submitted an affidavit describing his relationship with his former attorney (Tab A), a copy of a complaint filed against his former attorney with the New York State Bar Association (Exhibit K), and a copy of a letter to his former attorney informing him of the allegations against him (Tab I).
 
We find that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Twum v. INS, 411 F.3d 54, 59 (2nd Cir. 2005). Upon consideration of the motion and supporting evidence, we will grant the respondent’s timely unopposed motion to reopen, and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
*2 David B. Holmes

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted by Larin-Dominguez v. Holder, 9th Cir., May 17, 2013
2009 WL 3250484 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR ARMANDO LARIN-DOMINGUEZ
File: AXXX XX5 015 - Lancaster, CA
September 18, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary Silbiger, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 17, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent was previously ordered removed from the United States on August 4, 2004. The respondent departed the United States, subsequently re-entered illegally, and was ordered removed from the United States again on March 14, 2005. The respondent illegally returned to the United States, was arrested by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), and filed a motion to reopen on February 3, 2009, with the Immigration Court alleging that (1) the Immigration Judge erroneously informed the respondent that he was ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), due his drug possession conviction and (2) he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and this timely appeal followed.
 
Upon our de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). We first address the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge wrongfully informed the respondent at his August 4, 2004, hearing that he was ineligible for cancellation of removal due to his conviction for possession of cocaine, which constituted an aggravated felony. The respondent cites to the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47 (2006) and the decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in Cazarez-Gutierrez v. Ashcroft, 382 F.3d 905 (9th Cir. August 24, 2004) as support for his contention that his conviction for possession of cocaine did not constitute an aggravated felony “drug trafficking crime.” However, neither of these cases had been decided at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision on August 4, 2004. Rather, at the time of the Immigration Judge’s August 4, 2004, decision, the Ninth Circuit had yet to issue a precedent decision deciding whether a state felony drug offense is an aggravated felony for immigration purposes if the offense is not punishable as a felony under federal drug laws. See Cazarez-Gutierrez v. Ashcroft, supra, at 910. Absent a Ninth Circuit decision on the issue, the Board would have applied the Ninth Circuit’s rule used in the sentencing enhancement context to determine whether the respondent’s conviction was for an aggravated felony. See id. at 911-912; Matter of Yanez-Garcia, 23 I & N Dec. 390 (BIA 2002). As the Immigration Judge indicated in his decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Ninth Circuit’s decision in U.S. v. Rios-Beltran, 361 F3d. 1204, 1207 (9th Cir. 2004), wherein the court defined an aggravated felony as encompassing any drug offense that is: (1) punishable under Controlled Substances Act, and (2) a felony under either federal or state law, would have controlled. Under the definition employed in U.S. v. Rios-Beltra, supra, the respondent’s conviction for possession of cocaine constituted an aggravated felony that would have rendered him ineligible for cancellation of removal. Hence, we find that the Immigration Judge did not err in stating to the respondent that he was likely ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act at his hearing on August 4, 2004 (Tr. at 19-21).
 
*2 We next address the respondent’s argument that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, due to counsel’s failure to appear for the respondent’s hearing and counsel’s failure to advise the respondent of his eligibility to apply for cancellation of removal. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that he suffered any prejudice as a result of his attorney’s failure to appear. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir.2003) (to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim a petitioner must demonstrate prejudice). The respondent specifically alleges prejudice based upon his counsel’s failure to advise him of his eligibility for cancellation of removal; however, as discussed infra, the respondent was not eligible for cancellation of removal or any other form of relief at the time of his August 4, 2004, hearing, due to his conviction for an aggravated felony.
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge did not provide the respondent with a full and fair hearing that comported with due process requirements. At his initial hearing, the respondent’s counsel waived the reading of the respondent’s rights in removal proceedings (Tr. at 2). At the respondent’s subsequent hearing, when the respondent’s counsel failed to appear, the Immigration Judge offered to postpone the respondent’s hearing until a later date when his attorney could be present (Tr. at 8); however, the respondent indicated that he was “going to dismiss” his attorney anyway and that he wished to proceed with his hearing unrepresented, despite the Immigration Judge’s further offer to give the respondent more time to decide how to proceed with his immigration case (Tr. at 8-11). In the absence of representation, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge carefully explained the relief that was available to the respondent and his concerns with regard to the respondent’s eligibility for these forms of relief (Tr. at 13-22). Under these circumstances, we find that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that the Immigration Judge violated the respondent’s due process rights.
 
Additionally, with regard to the respondent’s argument that his waiver of the right to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision was not knowing and intelligent, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge clearly asked the respondent if he understood his right to appeal (Tr. at 22). The respondent indicated that he understood his right to appeal and that he accepted the Immigration Judge’s decision as final (Tr. at 22). The Immigration Judge even further clarified the consequences of waiving appeal for the respondent by telling him that he could not later change his mind and file an appeal (Tr. at 22). Despite this further warning, the respondent indicated that he still wanted to accept the Immigration Judge’s decision (Tr. at 22). As such, we find that the respondent’s waiver of his right to appeal to the Board was knowing and voluntary.
 
*3 Lastly, we find that the respondent’s motion does not establish an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 3250484 (BIA)
End of Document

2009 WL 3063640 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE IGNACIO HEREDIA
File: A097 917 015 - Los Fresnos, TX
September 14, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has filed a motion to reconsider our prior decision. He also moves to reopen for the conduct of a new hearing and for consideration of his newly-prepared affidavit. The motion will be denied.
 
Insofar as the respondent seeks reconsideration of our July 10, 2009, decision dismissing his appeal, we find no error of fact or law in that decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
In moving to reopen, the respondent seeks consideration of his new personal affidavit in which he contends that (1) his guilty plea underpinning his September 2008 state court conviction for trafficking in cocaine was not knowingly and intelligently made because he was not informed of its immigration consequences; (2) he misinterpreted the information provided by the Immigration Judge, and had little to no knowledge of immigration law or the consequences he faced by accepting the allegations that were made; and (3) that the Immigration Judge erred in allowing him to “plead guilty” to the alleged charges without an attorney being present. See Motion to Reopen (“Motion”) at 7; see also Motion at 4-5. In the motion itself, he claims that his right to counsel at his immigration hearing was violated. See Motion at 3.
 
It is well settled that an alien cannot collaterally attack the legitimacy of a criminal conviction in immigration proceedings. See e.g., Zinnanti v. INS, 651 F.2d 420, 421 (5th Cir. 1981) (holding that “whatever the merits of ¿an alien’sº ineffective assistance of counsel claim may be, ¿heº cannot collaterally attack the legitimacy of his otherwise valid state criminal conviction in the deportation proceedings”); Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529 (BIA 1992), and the cases cited therein. Although the respondent may want, or may have already initiated proceedings, to collaterally attack his conviction, such circumstances do not affect his present removability. See generally Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750, 751-52 (BIA 1993). On this record, the respondent’s conviction is final for purpose of the immigration laws, and he is removable as an alien convicted of a violation of a law relating to a controlled substance. See I.J. at 3; Group Exh. 2 (record of conviction).
 
As to the respondent’s due process-based claims concerning his proceedings in Immigration Court, there is no indication that his privilege of being represented by an attorney at no expense to the Government was violated, or that his hearing was not conducted in a fundamentally fair manner. At his February 11, 2009, hearing the Immigration Judge asked if he had an attorney to represent him, and he replied that he did not and needed time to look for representation. A continuance was granted for this purpose (Tr. at 2). At the next hearing the respondent appeared without counsel. The Immigration Judge specifically asked him if he was requesting more time to get an attorney, to which he replied “No” (Tr. at 6). Despite the respondent’s current allegations, there has been no showing that his counsel privilege or due process rights were violated in his removal proceedings.
 
*2 We also note that although the respondent’s alienage was established by his own admissions at the hearing which remain undisputed, his removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), was independently established by his certified record of conviction (Tr. at 6-8). See I.J. at 2-3; Group Exh. 2. Contrary to his declaration representations, he did not accept the allegations leveled against him by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), and he did not “plead guilty” to the charges filed against him in Immigration Court. See I.J. at 2; Exh. 1 (Notice to Appear reflecting (by separate handwritten insertions by the Immigration Judge) the respondent’s having denied both factual allegation #4 and his removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act).
 
The respondent also asserts that his removal will result in extreme hardship to his wife and children. See Motion at 2, 4-5. However, he has not identified any form of relief for which he is eligible. See I.J. at 4-6 (discussing the respondent’s ineligibility for various forms of relief). Moreover, we do not have the authority to grant an otherwise removable alien permission to remain in the United States solely for humanitarian reasons. Only the DHS has that authority. See generally Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982) (noting that deferred action status, giving a person permission to remain in the United States indefinitely, is matter of prosecutorial discretion); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 241.6(a) and 1241.6(a) (regarding administrative stays of removal).
 
Further consideration of the new evidence submitted with this motion would not change the result reached in this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 110 (1988).
 
Based on the foregoing considerations, the motion is denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 3063640 (BIA)

2009 WL 3063771 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESTHER UWAIFO
File: A073 039 395 - New York, NY
September 10, 2009
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerald Karikari, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eileen McCrohan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, has filed a motion to reconsider and reopen the Board’s February 6, 2009, decision denying her motion to reopen. The motion to reconsider and reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied because she has failed to identify any error of fact or law in our February 6, 2009, decision. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C) (a motion to reconsider shall specify the errors of fact and law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority). She simply restates her claims that she received ineffective assistance of counsel and that she is eligible for adjustment of status. We considered the respondent’s arguments before rendering a decision in this case. We decline to revisit them.
 
Since the respondent filed her motion to reopen a new standard applies to aliens raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (the Board and Immigration Judges should apply pre-Compean standards), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Under both Compean and pre-Compean standards, the alien must show that she exercised due diligence so as to warrant the equitable tolling of the 90-day motions deadline. As we previously determined, the respondent failed to exercise due diligence. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000) (finding that aliens relying on equitable tolling must establish, inter alia, that they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled).
 
The instant motion constitutes a motion to reopen insofar as it is supported by documentation. See section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(B) (a motion to reopen shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted, and shall be supported by affidavits or other material evidence). The motion to reopen will be denied as time and number barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Act, and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Even if we considered the proffered documentation (some of which was previously submitted), it would not alter our prior determination that the respondent failed to establish that she is the beneficiary of an approved or pending employment-based immigrant visa petition (Form I-140). Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen will be denied.
  
ORDER: The motion to reconsider and reopen is denied.
 
*2 Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 3063771 (BIA)
End of Document

2009 WL 3063598 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARBARA ROBINSON
File: A079 072 823 - Buffalo, NY
September 2, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carol G. Bridge
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 26, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. On appeal, the respondent requests that the Board find “exceptional circumstances” or another general equitable exception to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(1). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(I). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
Upon our de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). As an initial matter, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and numerically barred, inasmuch as it was not submitted within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s December 3, 2003, decision and is the respondent’s second motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). We also note that this Board does not have the authority to apply an “exceptional circumstances” or other general equitable exception to the penalty provisions for failure to depart within the time period afforded for voluntary departure under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act. See Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87 (BIA 2007).
 
Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent seems to argue that her failure to depart the United States under her voluntary departure order was a result of ineffective assistance of counsel, i.e., her failure to depart was not voluntary, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reopen failed to comply with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Zmijewska, supra, at 91 (noting that the penalties under section 240B(d)(1) of the Act were not intended to apply to every alien who failed to depart within the voluntary departure period but was meant to limit the penalties to only those aliens whose failure to depart was voluntary). The respondent’s motion to reopen did not contain a personal affidavit setting forth her agreement with former counsel, evidence that former counsel was informed of the allegations against him, or evidence that a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. We recognize that the respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal regarding her claim of ineffective assistance; however, we do not consider evidence first offered on appeal as our review is a review of the record created before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). The proper time to submit evidence is before an Immigration Judge at an evidentiary hearing. We note that where evidence is new or previously unavailable it may support a remand. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(1), (4). However, the respondent has failed to establish that the information contained in the documents she seeks to offer on appeal could not have been produced before the Immigration Judge. In light of the foregoing, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 3063598 (BIA)
End of Document

2009 WL 2981850 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERNAN OJEDA
File: A079 705 961 - Newark, New Jersey
August 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Susana Abarca, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 By an untimely motion filed on February 26, 2009, the respondent seeks to reopen proceedings that were last before the Board on September 8, 2004, based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion was not filed within the statutory and regulatory 90-day time limit for filing a motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(I) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal). However, because the respondent’s motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel we will consider whether the 90-day time limit should be equitably tolled. The respondent’s claim is premised upon the failure of former counsel to file a brief in support of the appeal, which was the basis for the dismissal of the respondent’s appeal by the Board. Although the respondent’s motion is premised upon the ineffective assistance rendered by former counsel, the respondent has not properly asserted that claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25, I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”). Therefore, the motion to reopen is, hereby, denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2981850 (BIA)

2009 WL 2981843 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NAKI OCRAN
File: A098 050 383 - St. Louis, MO
August 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fatma E. Marouf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorraine L. Griffin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has submitted a motion, which in substance is a motion to reconsider taking issue with the Board’s February 23, 2009, decision concerning the respondent’s motion to reopen. The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The pending motion does not cause the Board to reconsider its February 23, 2009, decision. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006)(a motion to reconsider must allege a material factual or legal error, assert that the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion, or argue a change in law). In that decision, the Board declined to reissue a November 7, 2008, decision, based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board also granted the respondent’s motion to withdraw the request for voluntary departure, citing Dada v. Mukasey, 128 S.Ct. 2307, 2319 (2008) (recognizing an alien’s unilateral right to withdraw a request for voluntary departure before the voluntary departure period expires). The DHS argues, in the pending motion, that the Board’s February 23, 2009, decision “does not contain an order of removal”, so the Board should amend its decision. However, the Board’s November 7, 2008, final order stated that
In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
Therefore, contrary to the DHS’ motion, the Board has issued an order of removal in this case, effective where, as here, the respondent has elected not to voluntarily depart the United States. The DHS’ motion to reconsider is, therefore, denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2981843 (BIA)

2009 WL 2981834 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARMANDO SAMPERIO LOPEZ
File: A035 834 729 - Los Angeles, CA
August 27, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case, last before us when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in an order dated November 30, 2004, is now before us on remand from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. Samperio-Lopez v. Holder, 321 Fed. Appx. 646 (9th Cir. 2009) (unpublished). The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, filed a motion to reopen our July 16, 2004, decision affirming without opinion the November 18, 2002, Immigration Judge’s decision. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had abandoned his application for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (1995); 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), when he failed to comply with the fingerprints requirement.
 
In its decision, the Ninth Circuit found that the respondent had substantially complied with the procedural requirements to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and that this Board erred when it determined otherwise. The court also noted that, at the time of our decision, we did not have the benefit of the intervening decision in Cui v. Mukasey, 538 F.3d 1289 (9th Circ. 2008), which held that the denial of a continuance for fingerprint processing prior to April 2005 may be an abuse of discretion. It appears that the respondent’s case may come within the holding in Cui. A remand is therefore necessary for further consideration of the issue of the fingerprints in accordance with Cui, and for consideration of the application for a 212(c) waiver if appropriate. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s order of November 30, 2004, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision and the decision of the court.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2981834 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTA CELENIA DIAZ
File: A0 74 081 757 - Boston, MA
August 21, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adam S. Elman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on September 16, 2008, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 24, 2007, decision denying her motion to reconsider the denial of her prior motion to reopen proceedings, which was based on lack of proper notice, and to rescind the in absentia order entered against her on December 12, 2006. At that time, we found no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s motion to reconsider was untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b) (30-day time limit for filing motion to reconsider).
 
On January 16, 2009, the respondent filed another motion to reopen, presenting new evidence to show that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was not served on her at the most recent address that she had provided to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), and thus that she was not properly notified of the scheduled removal proceedings. She urges that any time and number limits should be equitably tolled because her prior attorney — who represented her before the DHS, filed the prior motions on her behalf with the Immigration Court, and filed the appeal with the Board — provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel where he did not present all evidence pertaining to the change of address that had been filed with the DHS prior to initiation of the removal proceedings.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the First Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has declined to rule on whether equitable tolling is available where an alien claims ineffective assistance of counsel. See, e.g., Chedid v. Holder, 573 F.3d 33, 2009 WL 2100615 (1st Cir. Jul. 17, 2009). This Board, however, has generally accepted the concept of equitable tolling, so long as certain criteria are met, including a showing of due diligence and prejudice. Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2008, 2009). Although there are no time limits on a motion to reopen alleging lack of proper notice of removal proceedings, there are number limits. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) and 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii) & (c)(7)(A) (providing no time limit on motions to reopen claiming a lack of proper notice, but providing that only one may be filed); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Thus, the question here is whether the respondent has shown that the number limit should be tolled because of the actions of her prior counsel.
 
*2 We find that tolling of the number limit is warranted in this case, where the respondent has shown she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s failure to present dispositive evidence to the Immigration Court in connection with her first motion to reopen alleging lack of proper notice. In the alternative, we would reopen sua sponte under the circumstances of this case. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent’s motion complies with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in that she has provided her affidavit setting forth the agreement with prior counsel, filed a complaint against prior counsel, and notified prior counsel of her claims against him and provided an opportunity for him to respond. She submits evidence showing that she notified the DHS of an updated address as early as January 2005, well prior to the time the DHS served the NTA in this case on the respondent at her former address of record in July 2005 (Motion, tab C, USCIS notices dated February 2005 through August 2005; Exh. 1). This evidence establishes that the NTA was not properly served on the respondent, as it was not sent to her at the last address she had provided to the DHS. Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001). She therefore was prejudiced by her former attorney’s failure to include this evidence in the prior motion to reopen.¿FN1º Consequently, we find it appropriate to grant the motion to reopen, vacate the prior in absentia removal order, and remand for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: These removal proceedings are reopened, and the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order of removal dated December 12, 2006, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In addition, to the extent that service of the NTA on her personally by the DHS in October 2005 might have been considered to overcome the defect in service of the NTA, it still cannot overcome the fact that subsequent notices of hearings, sent by the Immigration Court to the respondent on January 5, 2006, August 9, 2006, and October 31, 2006 (Exh. 1A), were served at her old address that was not the most current address she had provided to the DHS prior to the initiation of the proceedings. Moreover, her prior counsel’s failure to file an appearance with the Immigration Court when he was hired to represent her in November 2005 in the removal proceedings, as well as his failure to advise the Immigration Court of her updated address, compounded the notice problems in this case and also resulted in prejudice to her (Motion, tab M, ¶¶ 8-13).

APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On July 13, 2009, the respondent filed this appeal from the Immigration Judge’s July 7, 2009, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on April 16, 2009. In the motion, the respondent moved to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b) for “exceptional circumstances,” namely that his prior attorney specifically advised him not to appear at the removal hearing. In the motion, the respondent’s attorney explained that the respondent was in detention, and that the motion would be amended upon the arrival of correspondence from the respondent. The Immigration Judge, however, correctly noted that the respondent failed to comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and denied the motion.
 
With his appeal brief, the respondent has submitted documentation in compliance with the Lozada requirements. We note that this material was submitted within 180 days of the initial in absentia order, and thus would have been timely had it been filed initially with the Immigration Judge. Given this record, we will construe the respondent’s appeal to be a timely supplement to his motion to reopen. We will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for a new adjudication of the motion to reopen, and for the entry of a new order. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
Edward R. Grant
