2009 WL 2981776 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JEAN DANIEL MAURICE
File: A043 676 328 - Fishkill, NY
August 20, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura A. Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board issued its final administrative decision on December 5, 2008. On July 10, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen is untimely (filed more that 90 days after our decision on the merits). See Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent, a native and citizen of Haiti, essentially seeks reopening based on equitable tolling, contending that his former accredited representative provided ineffective assistance of Counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes reopening, which will be denied.
 
On January 7, 2009, the United States Attorney General issued a decision setting forth standards for claims involving ineffective assistance of counsel, but this decision was recently vacated by the current Attorney General. Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Pending rulemaking procedures, the Board was directed to “apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed.” Aliens raising an ineffective assistance claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124, 129-133 (2d Cir. 2000). Aliens seeking to prevail on an ineffective assistance claim must establish 1) that counsel’s performance was so ineffective as to have impinged on the fundamental fairness of the proceedings because counsel’s conduct was such that competent counsel would have acted otherwise; and 2) counsel’s performance must have resulted in prejudice to the alien. See Romero v. United States INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005), citing U.S.v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97, 101 (2d Cir. 2001).
 
The respondent asserts that he has diligently but unsuccessfully attempted to “communicate with” Reverend Robert Vitaglione, his former accredited representative. However, as the respondent has not provided any copies of any written correspondence he has sent to Reverend Vitaglione or shown that he made Reverend Vitaglione aware that he filed a motion to reopen based on an ineffective assistance claim, the respondent’s assertion is insufficient to establish that Reverend Vitaglione has been informed of the nature of the respondent’s complaints and given an opportunity to respond thereto as required. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Consequently, the respondent has not established that he is entitled to equitable tolling of the time limit on reopening based on the ineffective assistance claim. The additional evidence concerning country conditions in Haiti also submitted in support of the motion was available at the time of the hearing in 2008 and does not establish changed country conditions in Haiti warranting reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii); Shao v. Mukasey, 546 F.3d 138, 168 (2d Cir. 2008); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 2981768 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DARIO ANTONIO ACOSTA
File: A099 623 164 - Boston, MA
August 20, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Raymond Fasano, Esquire
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
On April 27, 2009, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The Immigration Judge on December 1, 2008, found the respondent subject to removal, and denied the respondent’s request for voluntary departure under section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. The respondent, who is from the Dominican Republic, filed a timely motion to reopen on July 23, 2009. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The motion presents a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against the respondent’s former attorney, contending that counsel did not assist the respondent to submit an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act.¿FN1º
 
A respondent claiming that he received ineffective assistance of counsel must show “a reasonable probability of prejudice resulting from ¿hisº former representation.” Zeru v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 59, 72 (1st Cir. 2007)(citation omitted); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent fails to show such prejudice. A decision whether to grant cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act is a discretionary determination. Bencosme de Rodriguez v. Ashcroft, 433 F.3d 163, 164 (1st Cir. 2005); Matter of Almanza-Arenas, 24 I&N Dec. 771, 774 (BIA 2009). The Board concluded, in its April 27, 2009, decision, that:
We are unpersuaded to disturb the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that voluntary departure is not warranted in the exercise of discretion upon evaluation of the relevant factors, in view of the serious criminal charge of insurance fraud related to arson that was pending against the respondent in New York. The Immigration Judge was within her authority to deem that adverse factor to outweigh the positive factors presented by the respondent, including his 23-year presence in the United States and his ties to 2 United States children and to a United States citizen fiancee who is the children’s mother. See Tr. at 7-13; I.J. at 2-3; Matter of Thomas, 21 I&N Dec. 20 (BIA 1995). We have considered the respondent’s appellate statements, in which he acknowledges that his criminal case remains ongoing and asserts that he plans to marry his fiancee, who then will file a visa petition on his behalf. However, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s request for voluntary departure as a matter of discretion.
 
 
*2 Given the Board’s agreement that the respondent did not merit voluntary departure as a matter of discretion, there is not a “reasonable probability” that he would have been granted cancellation of removal as a matter of discretion, even had such relief been pursued at the removal hearing. The motion argues that counsel should have requested a continuance pending the disposition of the criminal case (Respondent’s Mot., at 7). In fact, counsel did seek “administrative closure”, but the request was opposed by the DHS and denied by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 12).
 
The respondent now presents an email, apparently from a prosecutor, stating that the respondent pled to criminal trespass after the removal hearing (Respondent’s Mot., Exh. O). There is no evidence to substantiate the email. Moreover, the fact that the respondent may have pled guilty to criminal trespass, rather than a more serious conviction, does not warrant reopening of these proceedings.
 
The motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Pending rulemaking procedures, the Board was directed to “apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed.”

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KATALIN SCHMIEL, PETITIONER, AS WIDOW OF BERNAT SCHMIEL
File: A077 953 140 - Vermont Service Center
August 20, 2009
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
David Kaplan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mary H. Burford
Service Center Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
 
*1 The petitioner has appealed from the Vermont Service Center Director’s March 21, 2007, denial of her self-petition (Form I-360) as a widow of a United States citizen. The denial was based on a finding that the Form I-360 was not filed within 2 years of the death of her United States citizen spouse. The Director further noted that, even if he had jurisdiction over the self-petition, the petition would be denied as there was a discrepancy between the name of the petitioner and her name on the marriage certificate. He further noted that there was a lack of evidence of the termination of all prior marriages of both the petitioner and her United States citizen spouse, who died on September 20, 2004, prior to the adjudication of a visa petition (Form I-130) that he had filed on March 16, 2000, on her behalf.
 
We have reviewed the record, including the decision of the Director and the arguments made on appeal, and we find that the record should be remanded. The petitioner raised the issue of ineffective assistance of her former counsel and argued that the negligence of her former counsel resulted in the untimely filing of the Form I-360. The Director did not acknowledge or address this argument. We do not make any determination on the merits of whether a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel may equitably toll the Director’s jurisdiction in this case, however, we find it appropriate to remand the matter to give the Director an opportunity to address this argument in the first instance. On remand, the Director may consider any additional evidence to determine if there was ineffective assistance of counsel that whether that could toll the two-year filing period for the filing of the Form I-360 in this case.
 
The petitioner should also be prepared to address the discrepancy in her name and any evidence of the termination of all prior marriages of the petitioner and her spouse. There appears to be evidence regarding both these matters in the record that should likewise be considered by the Director in the first instance.
 
If, upon review of any additional evidence submitted, and of the record as a whole, the VSC director determines that the Form I-360 petition should again be denied, he should issue a decision clearly setting forth the reasons for the denial, and should promptly forward the record to the Board in the event the petitioner files an appeal.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the VSC Director for further proceedings.
 
Ana L. Mann

2009 WL 2576459 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIAN YUAN ZHEN A.K.A. ANDY ZHEN
File: A044 195 740 - Portland, OR
August 13, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Frick, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah C. Schreck
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be denied.¿FN1º
 
The respondent’s conditional permanent resident status was terminated in 1998, and he was charged with marriage fraud under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i), and being subject to removal under sections 237(a)(1)(A) and (D) of the Act. The respondent was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge on October 15, 1999, and the Board affirmed this decision without opinion on March 12, 2003. The Board denied motions to reopen on November 21, 2003, and February 19, 2009. The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals affirmed the Board’s final order of removal in 2004. Zhen v. Ashcroft, 110 Fed.Appx. 795 (9th Cir. Sept. 22, 2004)(unpublished). The respondent, who is from China, filed another motion to reopen on June 18, 2009. The motion was filed many years out of time, and is barred by numerical limitations for motions to reopen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The motion presents a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against 2 former attorneys, contending that they did not assist the respondent to request a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Act, either before the Immigration Judge, or earlier before the Board.¿FN2º
 
A party claiming that equitable tolling applies to the reopening deadline based on claimed ineffective assistance of counsel must show that he acted with reasonable diligence. Escobar v. Holder, 567 F.3d 466, 480 (9th Cir. 2009); Valeriano v. Gonzales, 474 F.3d 669, 673 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent fails to provide an adequate reason to justify tolling the motions deadline for such a long period of time. DHS Opp., at 6. Significantly, the respondent made a decision, after years had gone by since the Board’s final decision, and after being detained in October, 2008, not to hire “competent legal counsel” to advise him, until May, 2009 (Respondent’s Mot., at ¶¶ 26-28).¿FN3º
 
In any event, under Ninth Circuit case law, a respondent claiming that he received ineffective assistance of counsel must show that counsel failed to perform with “sufficient competence,” Alcala v. Holder, 563 F.3d 1009, 1019 (9th Cir. 2009); Ahmed v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 768, 771 (9th Cir. 2008), such that proceedings are “so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting ¿hisº case.” Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1100 (9th Cir. 2009). The respondent “must also show substantial prejudice by demonstrating that the alleged violation affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id. (citation omitted).
 
*2 As the DHS argues, the respondent fails to show such prejudice (DHS Opp., at 4, 6). A decision whether to grant a waiver under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Act is a discretionary determination. San Pedro v. Ashcroft, 395 F.3d 1156, 1157 (9th Cir. 2005); Matter of Tijam, 22 I&N Dec. 308, 412-13 (BIA 1998). After considering all the evidence, the Immigration Judge concluded that: “… the respondent is not eligible for voluntary departure, because he does not possess good moral character. He does possess bad character, and so that form of relief is denied” (I.J. at 13). Given the Immigration Judge’s conclusion, as the DHS argues, “it is unlikely that the Immigration Judge would have granted a discretionary waiver … even if it had been requested” (DHS Opp., at 4).
 
The respondent also should have met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), but did not. Most significantly, the respondent has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explained why no filing was made. See Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603-605 (BIA 1996); Hernandez v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 1014, 1018 (9th Cir. 2008); Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 587-88 (9th Cir. 2006) (Lozada requirements generally reasonable, but full compliance is not required if the record independently shows that claims are clear and obvious); cf. Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 896 (9th Cir. 2008)(bar complaint not required where attorney already suspended). The respondent’s assertion that counsel may have acted “in good faith” is not an adequate explanation for failing to meet this requirement. DHS Opp., at 3. The motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied, and the request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2

On January 7, 2009, the United States Attorney General issued a decision setting forth standards for claims involving ineffective assistance of counsel, but this decision was vacated by the current Attorney General. Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). Pending rulemaking procedures, the Board was directed to “apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed.”

3

In any event, just as the respondent fails to show reasonable diligence in raising his late-filed and number-barred claim again former counsel, he fails to show diligence in raising this argument.

2009 WL 2576474 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADAO DE CASTRO-MAMBUANDA
File: A095 132 480 - Tucson, AZ
August 5, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa R. Pannone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case has a long procedural history that is reflected in the Immigration Judge’s decision. We briefly note that the respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on August 14, 2008, after failing to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on March 23, 2009, but it was denied on April 22, 2009. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We will first address whether the respondent received the proper notices in these proceedings. The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, and the Notice of Hearing was mailed to him at 4255 E. 22nd Street, #457, Tucson, AZ 85710. The respondent states in an affidavit first provided on appeal that the aforementioned address was submitted by him to the Immigration Court on a change of address form. He indicates, however, that he inadvertently provided an incorrect address, and the correct address should have been provided as 4425 E. 22nd Street, #457, Tucson, AZ 85710. The Board appreciates the respondent’s honesty, and it does not dispute that the mistaken submission may have been unintentional. However, the respondent has apparently caused the failure of the Immigration Court to notify him of his hearing at his correct address by having provided an incorrect mailing address. An alien waives his right to receive written notice of a hearing if the alien does not provide a correct mailing address. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(5)(B). We therefore are unable to find that there was a defect in the notice provided to the alien.
 
We will next address whether “exceptional circumstances” caused the respondent’s failure to appear for the hearing. The respondent alleges that his former counsel was negligent in these proceedings, and counsel’s ineffective representation caused the respondent’s absence from the hearing. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances” was untimely and did not meet the procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel. Even if we overlook the untimeliness of the motion and assume that the procedural requirements are now met based on evidence first provided on appeal, we still are unable to reopen these proceedings. The respondent indicates in a letter to the Arizona Bar Association dated June 23, 2009, that his former counsel was his representative until December 13, 2007. He also indicates that former counsel’s representation was derelict prior thereto. However, former counsel did not cause the respondent’s failure to appear on August 14, 2008. Notice of that hearing was sent to the respondent on June 19, 2008, at an address provided by the respondent. His failure to appear may have been avoided had the respondent provided a correct mailing address. We do not find that the respondent proved that his former counsel prevented him from appearing for his hearing. We therefore will not reopen these proceedings based on “exceptional circumstances.”
 
*2 The respondent further argues on appeal that these proceedings should be reopened based on international law. The Board lacks authority to apply international law in deciding whether to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia. See Matter of H-M-V-, 22 I&N Dec. 256 (BIA 1998); Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 1988). We further do not find that an exceptional situation exists herein to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed with the exception of his determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen is number-barred. The numerical limitations only apply to a motion to reopen the instant order of removal. Only one motion to reopen the instant removal order was filed. Motions to reopen prior orders of removal may not be considered in determining whether a number-bar applies in the instant request for reopening.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2576474 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2009 WL 2437184 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MACHOUDI BOLADJI KAMODJOU A.K.A. MACHONDI B. KAMODJOU
File: A023 557 713 - Houston, TX
July 31, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda Walker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, has filed a timely motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) to reconsider our decision dated November 12, 2008. In that decision, we denied a motion to reopen.¿FN1º We concluded that the respondent had not established that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance by opting to forego a continuance to wait for the adjudication of an immediate relative petition filed by the respondent’s adult daughter, in favor of going forward with an application for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996). The motion to reopen also argued that the prior counsel was ineffective because she admitted the allegations and conceded removability. Our final decision in this matter was issued on June 18, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of relief in discretion, and also denied a motion to remand filed while the appeal was pending. The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
Motions to reconsider must include, in pertinent part, (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision supported by pertinent authority; and (2) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change. See Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent, who adjusted to lawful permanent resident status in 1980, had been convicted in 1995 for aggravated assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Texas State Penal Code section 22.01(a)(2). He departed the United States in January 2006, attempted to reenter as a returning lawful permanent resident alter that year, and was placed in removal proceedings and charged as inadmissible as an alien who has been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. Section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act; see Pichardo v. INS, 104 F.3d 756 (5th Cir. 1997); Matter of Medina, 15 I&N Dec. 611 (BIA 1976); Matter of Goodalle, 12 I&N Dec. 106 (BIA 1967). In denying the motion to reopen, we concluded that the prior counsel’s decision to forego a continuance was a strategic decision and the respondent’s subsequent dissatisfaction is not grounds to reopen. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998). Furthermore, the respondent does not dispute the facts.
 
*2 The motion does not identify any legal or factual errors in prior decisions, or arguments overlooked previously. The motion essentially seeks to re-litigate the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, which we have already addressed. Also, a motion to reconsider may not be used to make new legal arguments that could have been made previously. Matter of 0-S-G-, supra. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The motions to reopen filed on July 18, 2008, and September 11, 2008, were considered together as a timely-filed, consolidated motion to reopen. We mistakenly cited to the earlier motion as having been filed on July 16, 2008. This was harmless error.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDREA PIMENTEL-BRICENO
File: A077 020 695 - Orlando, FL
July 30, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge De La Mar, Esquire
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 27, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen. The respondent now moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision.
 
In her motion, the respondent contends that she never received a notice of her scheduled court date. She has submitted an affidavit for the first time with her motion, stating that she did not receive notice of her hearing.
 
The Board addressed the issue of notice in our previous decision dated April 27, 2009. We thoroughly reviewed the evidence of record and concluded that the notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s counsel of record at the address he provided on the Form EOIR-28 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court) and Form EOIR-27 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Board of Immigration Appeals). We agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to establish that she lacked notice of her removal hearing. We noted that although counsel stated on appeal that neither he nor the respondent received notice of the respondent’s October 4, 2007, hearing, the respondent failed to submit affidavits or other evidence to establish that she lacked notice of such hearing. As the evidence of record supports the finding that the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).¿FN1º Consequently, the respondent has failed to present any additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case which was not previously considered by the Board. The respondent has not alleged ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to appear. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley

2009 WL 2437146 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FENG JI CUI
File: A098 296 527 - Phoenix, AZ
July 28, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Taihe Wang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of the People’s Republic of China. He was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 28, 2005, after failing to appear for a hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on April 25, 2008, alleging that he did not receive notice of the hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on July 25, 2008, finding that the respondent was adequately notified because the Notice of Hearing was served on the respondent’s former counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent now asserts on appeal that “exceptional circumstances” prevented him from appearing for his hearing. He contends that his former counsel neglected to notify him of the hearing or advise him of his obligation to appear. The respondent argues that these proceedings should be reopened because he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The record contains an affidavit provided by the respondent. Therein, he indicates that he became aware that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia after having hired his present attorney. We note, however, that the respondent’s present attorney also submitted the motion to reopen. No allegation was raised therein of the respondent having been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We further consider that the record contains the affidavit of the respondent’s former counsel’s secretary, Lai Fong Cheong. She states that the respondent contacted her on February 13, 2006, to seek the return of his passport. She further indicates that she informed the respondent that he had been ordered removed from the United States, and he had previously been advised of the consequences of not attending his hearing. Former counsel’s secretary further indicates that she later returned to the respondent his passport and birth certificate. Her account is corroborated by documentation maintained by her reflecting that she had phone conversations with the respondent on February 13, 2006. In addition, the record contains a statement from former counsel wherein he indicates that the respondent was advised of his obligation to appear for his hearing. He further states that the respondent did not provide former counsel with a means of contacting him to notify him of his hearing.
 
The Board has limited authority to render findings of fact when deciding an appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). See also Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). The Board therefore may not determine whether the respondent is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We further do not find it appropriate to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to render a factual finding on whether the respondent was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. This is in essence a motion to remand. A motion to remand must meet the same requirements as a motion to reopen. A motion to reopen must state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and must be supported by affidavits or other documentary evidence. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The motion will not be granted unless it appears to the Board that the evidence offered in support of the motion is material and was not available or could not have been discovered or presented previously. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-5 (1988). A party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue previously denied relief bears a heavy burden of proving that if the proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 We find significant that the respondent did not claim that he was a victim of attorney negligence when filing his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. The respondent was then represented by current counsel, and current counsel presumably had explored whether the respondent’s failure to appear for his hearing was caused by an “exceptional circumstance.” That claim was an assertion that should have been raised to the Immigration Judge in the respondent’s motion to reopen, and it should not have first been presented on appeal. We find that the respondent waived his opportunity to argue that an “exceptional circumstance” prevented his appearance from the hearing because it was not first raised in the motion to reopen. We also do not find that the new evidence would likely change the result in the case as respondent’s former counsel has convincingly challenged the respondent’s allegations.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Jim Hilz
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2437146 (BIA)
End of Document

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BOLADEIDOWU ABIOYE A.K.A. BOLADE ABIOYE
File: A076 827 700 - Houston, TX
July 23, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica Thompson Guidry
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Nigeria. He was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on April 20, 1999, after failing to appear for a hearing. The respondent filed motions to reopen with the Immigration Judge through former counsel, but they were denied on May 14, 1999, and June 7, 1999. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 16, 2008, denying his third motion to reopen. It was filed by present counsel on December 31, 2007. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that an “exceptional circumstance” prevented him from appearing for his hearing. He asserts that his former counsel failed to properly advise him of his hearing date. He also asserts that his first two motions to reopen were defectively prepared by his former counsel. He further indicates in his affidavit that his former counsel did not inform him that his second motion to reopen was denied on June 7, 1999. The respondent requests that the Board equitably toll the time and number limits on motions to reopen and rescind in absentia removal orders on the basis of claimed exceptional circumstances and reopen these proceedings.
 
A request for rescission of an order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date that the order of removal is issued. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Because a request for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of removal, it is barred from consideration even assuming (without deciding) that equitable tolling ever is available; it is not available here. The respondent was aware that he had been ordered removed from the United States in absentia and had a first or second motion to reopen pending before the Immigration Judge. He apparently took little action to ascertain the status of that motion until more than eight years elapsed after it was denied. Even assuming arguendo that the respondent’s former counsel rendered ineffective assistance of counsel, we will not equitably toll the 180-day period for seeking reopening of these proceedings, based on the respondent’s lack of due diligence.
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that an exceptional situation is not present herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We acknowledge the lack of notice issue raised on appeal. However, the argument was not raised before the Immigration Judge. In any event, proper notice to counsel is deemed to be notice provided to an alien. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5. In addition, the motion is untimely to the extent that the respondent seeks reopening to pursue a separate application for adjustment of status under the regular motion to reopen statute and regulations. See also Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HECTOR MERINO-RAZO
File: AXXX XX2 353 - Chicago, IL
July 13, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Erin Cobb, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mirta J. Woodall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen the Board’s April 23, 2009, decision dismissing his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his application for cancellation of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has expressed its opposition to the motion. The motion will be granted, and the record remanded for further proceedings.
 
The respondent contends that former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance by failing to consider his eligibility for derivative United States citizenship through his native-born United States citizen mother, and as he did not adequately prepare the respondent’s claim for cancellation of removal. The DHS reports that it has granted the respondent’s request for an administrative stay of removal. It opposes reopening, asserting that the same is “premature” as the respondent’s application for a certificate of citizenship (Form N-600) has not been adjudicated. The DHS essentially avers that unless and until this application is granted, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel cannot be proven.
 
The respondent has presented evidence reflecting that he was born in Mexico in 1968. Therefore, he is presumed to be an “alien” within the meaning of section 101(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8U.S.C.§1101 (a)(3), and bears the burden of presenting evidence to demonstrate that he is a United States citizen. Matter of Rodriguez-Tejedor, 23 I&N Dec. 153, 164 (BIA 2001). The respondent contends that he derived United States citizenship through his mother pursuant to section 309(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1409(c). That provision provides that a child born outside the United States and out of wedlock acquires the “nationality status of the mother, if the mother had the nationality of the United States at the time of ¿the child’sº birth, and if the mother had previously been physically present in the United States or one of its outlying possessions for a continuous period of one year.” The respondent has presented evidence showing that his mother is a native-born United States citizen and was unmarried at the time of his birth. She has provided a statement averring that she resided in the United States for the first three or four years of her life, also essentially alleging that she continuously resided in the United States for a period of more than one year prior to the respondent’s birth. As the DHS has reported, the respondent has provided it both with an application for citizenship, and supportive documentation.
 
In light of this evidence, we agree with the respondent that prior counsel provided him with ineffective assistance by failing to consider, much less present before the Immigration Judge, the claim that he has derived United States citizenship from his mother. We observe that the respondent has submitted statements from himself and a sister who hired his former counsel, stating both the complaints with the representation by former counsel, and the representational agreement between counsel and the respondent. A complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and former counsel was provided an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We disagree with the suggestion by the DHS that we cannot find that the respondent was provided with deficient service by former counsel absent a favorable decision on the pending application. Rather, the evidence indicates that the respondent has, at the least, a colorable claim to the requested relief, and counsel clearly failed in presenting the same before the Immigration Court. Accordingly, because it appears that the respondent has complied with the threshold requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra, and since the respondent, as the record now stands, appears prima facie eligible for relief, the motion will be granted. As the DHS indicates, if the respondent’s application for a certificate of citizenship is granted, the instant proceedings will be terminated. Therefore, on remand the Immigration Judge should consider holding proceedings in abeyance pending the adjudication of the respondent’s N-600 application, or other such action as deemed appropriate.¿FN1º
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In light of our decision, we need not reach the respondent’s arguments regarding former counsel’s handling of his claim for cancellation of removal. However, we note that neither our finding of ineffective assistance of counsel, nor the respondent’s arguments with respect to his cancellation claim, would support termination of the proceedings.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILKINS MONTILLA
File: AXXX XX7 713 - Albion, PA
July 7, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Orest Bezpalko II, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maureen C. Gaffney
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B))
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 4, 2009 decision ordering him removed from the United States based on his admissions and concession of removability. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
As an initial matter, the summary of the Immigration Judge’s oral decision dated March 4, 2009 indicates that both parties waived appeal, which is also apparent from each counsel’s statements recorded in the transcript of proceedings (Tr. at 7-8). See Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000) (“Asking the parties whether they accept a decision as ‘final’ is a shorthand expression commonly used by Immigration Judges…. Those who understand the meaning of this shorthand expression, such as aliens represented by attorneys or accredited representatives, may effectively waive appeal in response to this simple question.”). Nonetheless, on April 3, 2009, the respondent through different counsel filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). Upon review, we find that the respondent’s former counsel’s stated waiver of appeal was not understood as such by the respondent and was contrary to the respondent’s apparent intention at the time, as indicated by the respondent’s questions on the record immediately following the waiver submitted on his behalf (Tr. at 8) (“Is there anything that can be done to continue fighting this case? … is there any possibility, anything that can be done?”). Thus, under these unusual circumstances in which the respondent immediately stated on the record his desire to “continue fighting this case” in apparent discord with his counsel’s waiver of appeal, and subsequently filed a timely Notice of Appeal, we find it appropriate to review this case by certification (thereby resolving any potential uncertainty regarding our jurisdiction in this matter). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(c), 1003.39. The parties have already been given a fair opportunity to make representations before the Board regarding the case.
 
In his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), the respondent contends that the evidence presented by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) was insufficient to sustain the sole charge of removability. In addition, the respondent argues that his former counsel failed to object to the evidence presented by DHS. DHS contends that the Immigration Judge reached the correct decision in this matter.
 
*2 Initially, we find that the Immigration Judge properly relied on the respondent’s admission of the allegations in the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) and his concession of removability as charged (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 6-7). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c); see also Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 190-93 (2d Cir. 2009). We also note that the respondent does not allege clear error in the facts alleged and admitted below, and we find none. See 1003.1(d)(3)(i). Moreover, we reject the respondent’s contention that the records of conviction in this case are insufficient to support the sole charge of removability.¿FN1º Although the respondent’s admitted conviction under section 780-113(a)(30) of title 35 of the Pennsylvania Consolidated Statutes is not categorically an aggravated felony under the law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, see Evanson v. Attorney General of the United States, 550 F.3d 284 (3d Cir. 2008), it is clear from the respondent’s guilty plea “to the charge preferred” in the criminal information that cocaine was the controlled substance at issue, not marihuana. Thus, even if the respondent’s admission of the allegations and concession of removability were not sufficient to sustain the sole charge, had the records of conviction presented below been admitted into evidence, they would have supplied clear and convincing evidence under the modified categorical approach that the respondent was in fact convicted of an aggravated felony. See 8 U.S.C. 841; cf. Evanson, supra, at 292-93.
 
Furthermore, to the extent the respondent contends that his former counsel failed to object to the admission of evidence, we reject the respondent’s implicit argument that his attorney provided ineffective assistance because he has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988), and has failed to demonstrate that he suffered prejudice as a result of his counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, supra; Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”); see also Rranci v. Attorney General of the United States, 540 F.3d 165, 172-77 (3d Cir. 2008).
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the conviction records challenged on appeal were not formally admitted into evidence, as the factual allegations and sole removal charge were not contested below (Tr. at 6-7). However, DHS submitted the documents to the Immigration Court “with the intention of offering it into evidence” more than 2 months prior to the respondent’s hearing.

2009 WL 2171581 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PRINCE KENNY AGBAI
File: AXXX XX3 464 - Atlanta, GA
July 7, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a “Motion to Reargue Motion to Reopen,” challenging the Board’s May 19, 2009, decision denying his motion to reopen that was previously filed with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
As a motion to reconsider, the motion is timely, but fails to identify any particular error in the Board’s prior decision, and will therefore be denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Rather than assert error, the respondent raises an entirely new claim in the current motion. Specifically, in the prior motion to reopen filed with the Board, he presented additional assertions pertaining to his claim that he did not appear at his scheduled removal hearing because he did not receive notice. The Board had found that the record clearly reflected that his attorney of record was aware of the hearing date when he requested a change of venue, which refuted the respondent’s claim that he had not received the notice of hearing. In the current motion, the respondent now alleges that he was affirmatively advised by his first attorney that he would not need to attend the scheduled hearing because a request for change of venue had been made. Because the respondent no longer appears to challenge the conclusion that he received notice of the hearing, his current motion does not set forth any error of fact or law in our prior decision. Therefore, to the extent the motion requests reconsideration, it will be denied.
 
As pointed out, the motion is more in the nature of a motion to reopen, in that it raises a new claim and presents new evidence to the Board. As such, the motion is time- and number-barred. First, an alien may file only one motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order, regardless of whether the basis for the failure to appear is lack of notice, being in federal or state custody, or exceptional circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In addition, a motion claiming exceptional circumstances for the failure to appear must be filed within 180 days after the date of the Immigration Judge’s final order of removal. Id. The respondent’s current claim is essentially that he was unable to attend his May 12, 2005, hearing due to “exceptional circumstances,” where his attorney specifically told him that he did not need to attend that hearing because counsel had requested a change of venue. He further asserts that it was only after the hearing date had passed that counsel informed him that the motion to change venue had been denied. Thus, this claim must have been raised within 180 days of the May 12, 2005, in absentia order, but the motion raising this claim was not filed until 4 years later on June 1, 2009.
 
*2 With regard to the timeliness issue, we point out that, although some of the United States Courts of Appeals recognize equitable tolling of the time period for the filing of motions to reopen where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel has been made, the Eleventh Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that the time period for filing motions to reopen is mandatory and jurisdictional. Ali Abdi v. U.S. Attorney General, 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005); Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999). In addition, even if equitable tolling of the time limit could be considered in this case, counsel against whom this allegation has been made has specifically refuted the respondent’s claim. In a March 2009 letter submitted in response to the respondent’s complaint to the local attorney disciplinary committee, counsel states that he orally informed the respondent of the need to attend his scheduled hearing after he learned in April that his request to change venue had not been granted, and that he gave the respondent a letter at that time to present to the Immigration Court when he appeared. Counsel supports his letter with a copy of a letter dated May 10, 2005, addressed to the Immigration Court, explaining his inability to be present with the respondent at the scheduled hearing because of a conflict and renewing the request for a change of venue. His assertions regarding denial of the change of venue request are also supported by the record, where the record reflects that counsel filed two motions to change venue: one that was denied on January 31, 2005, but was mailed to counsel on April 19, 2005; and, a second that was filed on May 3, 2005, and denied on May 11, 2005. The evidence refutes the respondent’s claim that counsel affirmatively told him not to attend the scheduled hearing.
 
To summarize, to the extent the current motion is in the nature of a new motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal based on a claim of exceptional circumstances for the failure to appear, it is number-barred because the respondent already filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order. It is also untimely because it should have been filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s May 12, 2005, in absentia order. Finally, while the Eleventh Circuit does not recognize equitable tolling of time limits on motions to reopen, even if we were to consider the possibility of equitable tolling, we would not find it warranted in this case, where accused counsel has credibly refuted the respondent’s claim that counsel told him not to attend the scheduled hearing. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 2171581 (BIA)
End of Document

2009 WL 2171622 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LIHUA ZHU, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY CONG NGOC LAI, PETITIONER
File: AXXX XX6 509 - Los Angeles, CA
July 1, 2009
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Luisito L. Lopez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret M. Jambor
Associate Regional Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
 
*1 The petitioner appeals from the May 7, 2008, decision of the Department of Homeland Security, Citizenship and Immigration Services (“DHS”), denying the petition filed on behalf of the beneficiary the spouse of a United States citizen. The appeal will be dismissed as the petitioner did not meet his burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence that his marriage to the beneficiary is bona fide.
 
In response to a Notice of Intent to Deny, the petitioner submitted documentary evidence to establish a shared life together with the beneficiary. However, such evidence must be considered in conjunction with the material discrepancies between the statements of the petitioner and the beneficiary at interviews with the DHS and the results of an investigation into the petitioner’s marriage by the DHS. The inconsistencies revealed by the DHS investigation, which are set forth in the February 12, 2008, Notice of Intent to Deny the petition, provided serious reasons for believing that the petitioner’s marriage to the beneficiary was not bona fide.
 
The petitioner and the beneficiary did not know the names and sex of each other’s siblings, and the beneficiary did not know how much her husband earns, even after 4 years of marriage. Further, an investigation conducted by the DHS showed the petitioner and the beneficiary did not appear to have been together in their home as they claimed, a roommate had not seen the petitioner at the house in a long time, the petitioner and the beneficiary changed their stories about when they left the house, and finally, the beneficiary was evasive when interviewed at work about her arrival there, even being evasive about her time card.
 
In visa petition proceedings, the petitioner has the burden of establishing eligibility for the benefits sought. See Matter of Brantigan, 11 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 1966). The petitioner must prove the required elements by a preponderance of the evidence. See Matter of Pazandeh, 19 I&N Dec. 884 (BIA 1989).¿FN1º Where the bona fides of a marriage are challenged, the petitioner must present documentary or testimonial evidence to show that it was not entered into for the primary purpose of evading the immigration laws.¿FN2º See Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975). However, we note that the failure to produce affirmative evidence of the bona fides of the marriage, by itself, is not sufficient to establish that the marriage is a fraudulent marriage. Compare 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B), (D) with 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(ii).
 
*2 In light of the doubts raised about the validity of the marriage, we agree that the weight of the documentary evidence submitted by the petitioner does not overcome the doubts in order to meet his burden of establishing the marriage is bona fide. When the bona fides of a marriage are in question, evidence that might ordinarily be sufficient to meet the petitioner’s burden of proof in this matter, is insufficient in this case because of the substantial evidence in the record that weighs against a finding that the petitioner’s marriage to the beneficiary is bona fide.
 
On appeal, the petitioner asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Ineffective assistance of counsel in a removal proceedings is a denial of due process if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. See Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). In any case presenting a claim of ineffective assistance, the performance inquiry must be whether counsel’s assistance was reasonable considering all the circumstances. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 688 (1984). Methods of case presentation are within counsel’s professional judgment and are afforded great deference. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 688-89. Strategic decisions, such as whether to call a particular witness at the hearing, rest upon the sound professional judgment of the lawyer. Such appropriately informed strategic decisions are virtually unchallengeable. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 690.
 
The District Director found that statements by counsel made in rebuttal to the NOID were not evidence as there were not personal affidavits. However, even if we accept the statements of counsel as true, we are not persuaded by the explanations for the lack of knowledge by the petitioner and the beneficiary about each other’s lives. Furthermore, counsel’s attempts to create doubt about the discrepancies revealed by the investigation of the DHS are unconvincing as the investigation revealed discrepancies material to the bona fides of the petitioner’s marriage to the beneficiary. In this regard, the fault for denial of the petition is not with counsel but with the petitioner and the beneficiary as their statements and actions are at the root of the denial. Thus, the petitioner was not prevented from reasonably presenting his case and he has not established ineffective assistance.
 
The petitioner is not precluded from filing a new petition on the beneficiary’s behalf that is fully supported by consistent evidence that the petitioner’s marriage to the beneficiary is bona fide. Nevertheless, the petitioner’s motion to remand is denied and the appeal will be dismissed because the petitioner did not meet his burden of proving the bona fides of his marriage to the beneficiary by a preponderance of the evidence. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
David L. Neal
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The “clear and convincing” standard applies to a marriage entered into by an alien during removal proceedings. See 8 U.S.C. §§ 1154(g), 1255(e); 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii). As the beneficiary was not in removal proceedings when the parties married, that standard is inapplicable here.

2

Evidence to establish the bona fides of a marriage includes proof of joint ownership of property, proof of joint tenancy of a common residence, proof of commingling of financial resources, birth certificates of children born of the petitioner and the beneficiary, and affidavits of others having knowledge of the bona fides of the marital relationship. See 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B).

IN RE: JAIME VASQUEZ-ARRIAGA
File: AXXX XX1 140 - Los Angeles, CA
June 25, 2009
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas J. Tarigo, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º -Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated September 14, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for lack of jurisdiction. The respondent has appealed from that decision, and has also re-filed his motion to reopen with this Board. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was granted the privilege of voluntary departure by an Immigration Judge in July 1993, but he failed to depart by the court-imposed deadline, resulting in the automatic entry of an order of deportation against him. In February 2004. approximately 10 years after his deportation order became final, the respondent filed a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge in which he requested an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely in a decision dated March 23, 2004, and this Board affirmed that decision in an order entered on June 16, 2004. On July 18, 2007, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, in which he argued that his due process right to a full and fair deportation hearing had been deprived by the ineffective assistance of two attorneys, the one who represented him in his initial deportation proceedings, and also the one who filed his first motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge denied this second motion for lack of jurisdiction, concluding that authority to adjudicate the motion rested with the Board, which had issued the last substantive order in the matter.
 
The respondent has filed an appeal, but the appeal will be dismissed because it does not challenge any aspect of the Immigration Judge’s decision below. We note in this regard that the Immigration Judge expressed no opinion as to the merits of the respondent’s motion; instead, he simply found that he lacked jurisdiction to adjudicate it. Indeed, it appears that the respondent simply filed an appeal as a means of placing his motion to reopen before this Board. We agree with the Immigration Judge that this Board is the proper forum for consideration of the respondent’s motion, which has been re-filed with us by the respondent. Accordingly, we now turn to the merits of the motion to reopen.
 
As a threshold matter, the respondent’s motion to reopen is both number-barred and untimely. The regulation applicable to the respondent provides that he may file only one motion to reopen his deportation proceedings, and that he must file any such motion by not later than September 30, 1996. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2007). As noted previously, the respondent already filed a motion to reopen in February 2004. Furthermore, the respondent’s final administrative order of deportation was rendered in July 1993, and therefore his motion to reopen was subject to the September 30, 1996, deadline. Yet the respondent did not file his second motion to reopen until 2007, more than 10 years late. Finally, the motion does not qualify for any regulatory exception to these time and number limits. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 Although the respondent’s motion is both time- and numbered-barred, this Board possesses limited discretionary authority to reopen deportation proceedings sua sponte in exceptional circumstances. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Furthermore, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction these proceedings arise, has held that the regulatory limitations on motions may be “tolled” for equitable reasons. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent’s motion claims that his due process rights were violated by the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, a serious allegation. Under the circumstances, however, we find no valid basis for either sua sponte reopening or equitable tolling.
 
First, although the respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from an attorney who had been retained to represent him in connection with his 1993 deportation hearings, there is no indication that any attorney ever entered an appearance before the Immigration Court or otherwise qualified as the respondent’s attorney of record in those proceedings. And indeed, the respondent’s bar complaint form (which is attached as Exhibit G to his motion), specifies that the attorney was employed by him in 2002 and 2003, nearly 10 years after the respondent’s deportation proceedings were concluded. In light of these discrepancies, we conclude that it would be inappropriate to grant the respondent extraordinary equitable relief.
 
In any event, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s motion contains no such affidavit, and therefore we have no means of determining whether the actions and omissions complained of were even within the scope of prior counsel’s representation. In this regard, we observe that statements of counsel, set forth in the motion, are not evidence. INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Furthermore, there is no evidence that the respondent’s prior attorneys have been informed of the allegations leveled against them and given an opportunity to respond. Matter of Assaad, supra, at 556. Thus, we have no means of determining whether the actions and omissions complained of were “strategic” in nature, or otherwise justified by circumstances existing at the time they allegedly occurred. Accordingly, the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is not properly substantiated, and would be insufficient to justify reopening even under normal circumstances.
 
*3 Finally, although the respondent claims that his first attorney was ineffective by failing to file an application for Family Unity benefits on his behalf, it is evident from the respondent’s own submissions (see Exhibit B to the motion) that he has been aware of the availability of such relief since early 1995. The respondent has offered no explanation as to why he waited until 2007 to raise this issue with the Board, although he does suggest that his second attorney should have raised the matter in connection with his 2004 motion. But even if the issue had been raised in 2004, an alien does not exercise due diligence—as required to merit sua sponte reopening or equitable tolling—by waiting 9 years to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096-97 (9th Cir. 2007) (holding equitable tolling of motions deadline unavailable where alien failed to exercise due diligence in raising ineffective assistance of counsel claim against prior attorney).
 
In conclusion, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed because it does not articulate any challenge to the Immigration Judge’s decision, which merely denied his motion to reopen for lack of jurisdiction. Furthermore, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied because it is time-and number-barred.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ISIDRO SIMBRON-GARRIDO
File: AXXX XX9 309 - Phoenix, AZ
June 23, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer Gaz
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the January 14, 2009, decision of the Immigration Judge which found that the respondent had abandoned his claim for relief due to his failure to file any application for relief by the date ordered. In his decision, the Immigration Judge also vacated a merits hearing that he had scheduled for September 16, 2009. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Our review of the record reveals that, on May 15, 2008, the Immigration Judge granted a continuance requested by the respondent’s then counsel, who was seeking additional preparation time (Tr. at 3). The parties were instructed to return to court on July 10, 2008. On that date, counsel for respondent conceded proper service, admitted all allegations contained in the Notice to Appear except the date of entry, and requested an additional 180 days to file an application for cancellation of removal, based on alleged hardship to a United States citizen son of the respondent. See Tr. at 5-7. The Immigration Judge granted the counsel’s request, ordering the parties to return to court on September 16, 2009, and instructing the respondent to file his application by January 6, 2009. See Tr. at 7. The Immigration Judge specifically warned the respondent, on the record, that a failure to file his application for relief by the stated date “may constitute abandonment of the application for relief without hearing.” Id.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that, since the respondent failed to file his application for relief from removal by the ordered date of January 6, 2009, he is considered to have abandoned his claim. See I.J. at 1-2; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). As shown in our earlier discussion, proceedings in this matter had already been delayed to allow the respondent ample time to prepare for his merits hearing, and the respondent had been specifically warned of the potential consequences of a failure to submit his application for relief by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge. Moreover, on appeal, the respondent does not dispute that he failed to timely file his application for cancellation of removal, which the record shows was filed over a week after the established deadline, specifically on the date of the Immigration Judge’s decision. Rather, the respondent alleges that his prior counsel (who represented him before the Immigration Judge) was responsible for the untimely filing, as that counsel “did not advise me that ¿the application for reliefº was required to be filed by a certain date and in fact did not inform me of anything regarding the status or progress of my case.” See Respondent’s NOA, Box 6. The respondent also generally alleges on appeal that this attorney defrauded him and provided him with “inaccurate and plainly false advice on ¿hisº case.” Id. The respondent’s allegations of fraud and inaccurate and false advice are no more than cursory, unsubstantiated allegations. Also, the allegations regarding lack of advice are not only likewise cursory, but do not demonstrate any prejudice to the respondent inasmuch as the specific advice that the respondent claims he did not receive from his attorney was specifically provided to him by the Immigration Judge at the hearing. See Tr. at 7. Moreover, we note that the respondent has not shown that he has substantially complied with the threshold requirements to appropriately pursue a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988)¿FN1º. As such, we do not find that a remand is warranted for consideration of any claim pertaining to purported ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, we further note that, while an application for cancellation of removal has now been filed in this case, that application shows no prima facie eligibility for this particular form of relief inasmuch as it identifies no qualifying relatives.¿FN2º Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See Voluntary Departure: Effect of a Motion To Reopen or Reconsider or a Petition for Review, 73 Fed. Reg. 76,927, 937-38 (Dec. 18, 2008) (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c)(3)(iii), (e)(1)).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252. the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 73 Fed. Reg. at 76,938 (to be codified at 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i)).
 
*3 Linda S. Wendtland

IN RE: ELADIO SOLER A.K.A. LUIS BONILLA RIVERA

June 18, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lauren L. Baker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Peter J. Pratt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) appeals from an Immigration Judge’s February 11, 2009, decision terminating proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge terminated proceedings¿FN1º on the basis of the vacatur of the respondent’s December 6, 2005, conviction for distribution of cocaine in violation of section 32A(a) of chapter 94C of the Massachusetts General Laws. The respondent argued below that this vacatur was based on his July 2008 motion to vacate plea and for a new trial, in which he argued that he did not receive effective assistance from his criminal defense counsel, in that he was not aware of, and did not understand, his constitutional rights prior to his admission of sufficient facts to support a conviction. See Motion to Terminate at Tab E. The respondent’s motion to vacate his plea was granted by order of the trial judge on August 5, 2008, by way of a written notation on the face of the motion which states that it was granted “based on ineffective assist of counsel.” Id. The respondent argued below that this document reflects evidence that his underlying conviction was vacated based on a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings rather than upon a rehabilitative statute or immigration hardship. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). The Immigration Judge agreed, and terminated proceeding (I.J. at 2).
 
On appeal, the DHS argues that the Immigration Judge’s written notation, as set forth above, that he was vacating the conviction and ordering a new trial based on “insufficient assist of counsel” does not provide a statement of the “reasons for allowing the motion for a new trial.” See DHS’s Brief at 4.
 
We disagree. The test for deciding whether a conviction has been vacated for immigration purposes as set forth in Matter of Pickering, supra, is whether the conviction has been vacated on the merits. Id. A conviction vacated due to ineffective assistance of counsel qualifies as a vacation on the merits. See id.; see also Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31, 40 n. 13 (1st Cir. 2006) (endorsing the idea that ineffective assistance of counsel was a “clear defect in the underlying criminal proceedings”). Therefore, it appears from the record that the respondent has established that his 2005 conviction has been vacated and is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. Cf. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006). We specifically note that the record does not contain any Immigration Judge finding of fact that would indicate that the respondent’s arguments regarding the sufficiency of legal representation were solely related to his understanding of the “immigration consequences” of his plea (DHS’s Brief at 7). Rather, the trial judge’s decision to grant the motion to vacate the guilty plea and order a new trial is written on the face of the respondent’s actual motion, on which the respondent states that he did not “fully understand his constitutional rights” due to the ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Terminate, Tab E). While the respondent’s later-acquired knowledge of the immigration consequences of his plea may well have triggered his 2008 motion to vacate his guilty plea, there are no factual findings in the record to support the DHS’s argument.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The decision to terminate proceedings was the second such order, following a January 8, 2009, remand by the Board requiring clarification of the Immigration Judge’s prior order terminating proceedings on the basis of the State of Massachusetts’ vacatur of an underlying criminal conviction.
