2009 WL 1863846 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA ALICIA GONZALEZ-FRANCO
File: AXXX XX1 507 - Phoenix, AZ
June 16, 2009
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kari Hong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica Austin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 24, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen proceedings in which she had been ordered deported in absentia on September 6, 1995. On January 8, 2009, the respondent submitted an untimely motion to reopen proceedings, which the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes.¿FN1º 8C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion and the respondent’s request for a stay will be denied.
 
In her current motion the respondent avers that former counsel, Ms. Rossiter, provided her with deficient legal assistance in representing her in her initial motion before the Immigration Judge, and the ensuing appeal to the Board. We note that the decision of the United States Attorney General in Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710, 716-727 (A.G. 2009) finding that aliens have no constitutional right to effective assistance from counsel has recently been vacated. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Therefore, we shall review the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to the standards enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (hereinafter, Lozada).
 
The respondent essentially requests equitable tolling of the motion’s deadline based on her claim of ineffective assistance by former counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that the court recognizes equitable tolling of deadlines and numerical limits on motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when an alien is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and upheld in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We conclude that equitable tolling is not warranted.
 
In Lozada the Board outlined several procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Id. at 639. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has held that these requirements “are not sacrosanct,” particularly when a record reveals “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000). Rather, the Ninth Circuit has held that, “Ineffective assistance of counsel in a deportation proceeding is a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). As such, to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that she was prejudiced by an attorney’s deficient performance. Iturribarria v. INS, supra at 903 (finding that an attorney’s “flawed” performance did not provide a basis for a due process claim because the alien could not “demonstrate that counsel’s alleged malfeasance prejudiced his claim for relief”). However, where an attorney’s performance was inadequate but an alien still had an opportunity to present her claim, “¿pºrejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
*2 Here, the respondent contends that former counsel erred by failing to submit a motion to reopen supplemented by adequate evidentiary support, which delineated all legal theories relevant to her claim for relief, and was supported by an explanation of how she acted diligently in pursuing her claim (Motion at 1-22; Suppl. Motion at 1-3; Response to DHS Opposition at 1-2 (hereinafter, “Response”)). The respondent argues that Ms. Rossiter failed to raise four legal arguments in the motion which would “more likely than not” have changed the outcome in the Board’s review of her appeal (Response at 3). Specifically, she alleges that Ms. Rossiter erred by not arguing that the respondent’s father’s failure to notify her of his receipt of her hearing notice and his allegedly false notification to the former Immigration and Naturalization Service (“INS”) that she had departed the United States for Guatemala following her detention by INS constituted exceptional circumstances excusing her failure to appear at her hearing; as the respondent was a minor she received inadequate notice pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 242.24; because of her father’s actions the notice of hearing was not served on a “responsible person” as required; she did not receive notice pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act, and, the Board should have vacated proceedings pursuant to Matter of M-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 125 (BIA 1998) (Response at 1-2).
 
However, by the respondent’s own admission she was not a minor at the time that she was served with the Order to Show Cause on December 1, 1994 (Motion at 140; Exh. 1).¿FN2º Rather, she has repeatedly reported that she was born on October 31, 1973, which means that she was 21-years of age when the charging document was served upon her. Therefore, any protections then relevant to a minor were not applicable to the respondent. As such, it is unclear why current counsel repeatedly raises arguments that pertain solely to minors detained by immigration authorities, and we shall not address these arguments further. We observe that former counsel’s failure to raise these irrelevant arguments does not constitute error, much less represent a prejudicial failing on her part.
 
As this Board has previously noted, the respondent was personally served with the Order to Show Cause which was read to her in her native Spanish language, and which contained warnings regarding her obligation to inform the Immigration Court of any change of address and the consequences attending a failure to appear for her deportation hearing (Exh. 1). When she was released from detention her father’s address was provided as the location where she could receive mail from the Immigration Court. By her own admission the respondent affirmatively elected not to inform the Immigration Court when she left her father’s residence for another state, as she chose to rely upon him to forward her mail to her new residence (Motion at 140). Thus, as the respondent failed to provide an address pursuant to section 242B(a)(1)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(a)(1)(F), then “no written notice shall be required.” Section 242(c)(2) of the Act; see also Matter of Villalba, 21 I&N Dec. 842 (BIA 1997). Therefore, former counsel did not err in failing to raise arguments regarding; whether the respondent’s father was a “responsible person” or his alleged actions constituted exceptional circumstances since the respondent’s failure to inform the Immigration Court of her correct address is dispositive.¿FN3º See also Chaidez v. Gonzales, 476 F.3d 773 (9th Cir. 2007) (examining whether a “responsible person” had signed for a notice of hearing sent by certified mail where the respondent still resided at the address to which it had been sent). Moreover, a claim alleging she failed to appear due to “exceptional circumstances” (i.e., her father’s failure to inform her of her hearing date) would have been untimely. See Section 242B(c)(3), 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3) (a motion to reopen claiming exceptional circumstances prevented a respondent from appearing at his hearing must be filed within 180 days of the date of the order of deportation).
 
*3 We observe that as the Board did not predicate our dismissal of the respondent’s appeal on her failure to demonstrate due diligence, and as she has not shown that any failings by Ms. Rossiter were prejudicial, her arguments in this regard are unavailing. The respondent’s reliance on Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA1998) is misplaced as, unlike the alien in that case, she did receive oral warnings in her native language of the consequences of failing to appear at her deportation hearing (Motion a: 16). Moreover, even if Matter of M-S- was applicable, which it is not, the respondent’s motion was untimely filed, and reopening on that basis would not be appropriate. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b). Finally, we do not find that the facts presented in this case represent exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (holding that the Board’s discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); see also Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Thus, while we are not unsympathetic to the respondent’s situation, we find that she has failed to demonstrate that Ms. Rossiter’s provided her with deficient performance, much less that any actions on her part were prejudicial such that reopening is warranted. Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
In light of our decision, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal is rendered moot. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: GUO HUA JIN

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ping C. Shen, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Warren R. Kaufman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the October 17, 2007, decision of the Immigration Judge which denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings held in absentia on March 19, 2007. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
In her motion, the respondent asserts ineffective assistance of counsel, claiming that the attorney she retained to assist her at the original hearing neglected to advise her to attend the hearing even though the respondent requested a change of venue. The respondent asserts that such situation warrants reopening as she was not advised to attend the hearing.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(h) and (iii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The factual findings of the Immigration Judge are not clearly erroneous, and we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s thorough and well-reasoned decision. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994); see generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We considered the respondent’s contentions on appeal but we concur in the Immigration Judge’s determinations concerning the respondent’s request for reopening. In particular, the Immigration Judge noted the evidentiary deficiencies in the respondent’s case, primarily the lack of a certified Chinese translation (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge also correctly noted that the attorney of record at the time of the hearing was not the attorney whom the respondent now claims ineffectively assisted her (I.J. at 6). The attorney of record notified the respondent and he himself appeared at the hearing. The respondent fails to present an explanation for this situation. Finally the respondent has not presented evidence of ineffective assistance by counsel and indeed has neglected to state any shortcomings by the attorney of record. On the basis she has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guyana, has filed a motion to reconsider our prior decision. This case was previously before the Board on July 28, 2008, when we remanded the matter to the Immigration Judge. In that decision, we concluded that the Immigration Judge erred in terminating proceedings, and sustained the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) appeal, determining that the record established that the loss to the victim of the fraud offense for which the respondent was convicted was in excess of $10,000. Section 101(a)(43)(M) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(M). On remand, in an October 28, 2008, decision, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had been convicted of an aggravated felony as charged under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act.¿FN1º In our March 12, 2009, decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. A motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must include, in pertinent part, (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority; and (2) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The instant motion essentially disagrees with our conclusion that the record contains clear and convincing evidence that the loss to the victim, Fleet Bank, exceeded $ 10,000. The motion essentially repeats the facts and arguments in the prior motion, but does not demonstrate that reconsideration is warranted. The motion does not establish any errors or law or fact in our prior decision that would warrant reconsideration. 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2009 WL 1800105 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YARENIA ANALCO-COLON
File: AXXX XX6 700 - Atlanta, GA
June 8, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from a decision dated January 8, 2008, by the Immigration Judge in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent argues that she was misinformed by her former counsel with respect to having to appear at her October 26, 2006, hearing and as such, she was ordered removed in absentia as the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. An ineffective assistance of counsel claim can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order, pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). However, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy certain criteria as set forth in Matter of Compean, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009) (establishing an administrative framework superceding, but drawing upon, the approach set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 639 (BIA 1988)). Although the new document filing requirements established in Matter of Compean, supra, do not apply here because the respondent’s motion was filed prior to the issuance of Matter of Compean, supra, the substantive standards set forth in that decision must be met by the respondent. See Matter of Compean, supra, at 741-742 (holding that the new document filing requirements set forth in this decision only apply to motions filed after the issuance date of this decision (January 7, 2009); for motions filed prior to this decision, the Lozada factors continue to apply). Matter of Lozada requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
*2 Here, we conclude as did the Immigration Judge on January 8, 2008, that the respondent did not comply with the Lozada requirements. Although the respondent has alleged ineffective assistance of counsel and submitted a photocopy of a complaint she claims to have filed with North Carolina State Bar, she has failed to otherwise comply with the above requirements. In this regard, she has failed to submit an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, there is no indication that her former counsel was notified of the allegations or that he was allowed an opportunity to respond. In reaching this conclusion, we find no merit to the respondent’s appellate contention that the Immigration Judge did not adequately consider her claims.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003. 1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

*1 This case is before the Board pursuant to the February 20, 2009, order issued by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit granting the government’s unopposed motion to remand for further consideration of the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim in light of orders of the Supreme Court of Arizona, issued after the Board’s April 18, 2008, decision, which address the competence of her prior attorney, Ronald G. 25 I. & N. Dec. 1 (U.S.Atty.Gen.), Interim Decision 3643, 2009 WL 1570283
U.S. Department of Justice
Office of the Attorney General
MATTER OF ENRIQUE SALAS COMPEAN, RESPONDENT
MATTER OF SYLLA BANGALY, RESPONDENT
MATTER OF J-E-C-, ET AL., RESPONDENTS
File A078 566 977 - Houston, Texas
File A078 555 848 - Houston, Texas
Decided by Attorney General June 3, 2009
**1 *1 The Attorney General vacated the decision in Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), and pending the outcome of a rulemaking process, directed the Board of Immigration Appeals and the Immigration Judges to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
FOR RESPONDENT COMPEAN:
Cyril Chuckwurah, Esquire,
Houston, Texas
FOR RESPONDENT BANGALY:
Isuf Kola, Esquire,
Glen Ellyn, Illinois
FOR RESPONDENT J-E-C-, et al.:
Robert J. Jacobs, Esquire,
Gainesville, Florida
AMICI CURIAE:
American Immigration Law Foundation, and other organizations
FOR THE DEPARTMENT OF HOMELAND SECURITY:
David A. Martin,
Acting General Counsel;
David A. Landau,
Chief Appellate Counsel
 
BEFORE THE ATTORNEY GENERAL
 
On January 7, 2009, Attorney General Mukasey overruled in part the decisions of the Board of Immigration Appeals (“Board”) in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and affirmed the Board’s orders denying reopening in Matter of Compean, A078 566 977 (BIA May 20, 2008), Matter of Bangaly, A078 555 848 (BIA Mar. 7, 2008), and Matter of J-E-C- (BIA Apr. 8, 2008). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009) (“Compean”). In Lozada, the Board established the procedural requirements for filing a motion to reopen deportation (now removal) proceedings based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and required the alien to show *2 that he was prejudiced by the action or inaction of his counsel. Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639-40. The Compean decision acknowledged that the Lozada framework had “largely stood the test of time,” having been expressly reaffirmed by the Board 15 years after its initial adoption. Compean, 24 I&N Dec. at 731; see also Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. at 556-57 (affirming the application of Lozada to removal proceedings). Nonetheless, Compean both rejected Lozada’s constitutional reasoning and ordered the Board not to rely upon the Lozada framework, even as a discretionary matter. Instead, Compean set forth, as an exercise of the Attorney General’s administrative discretion, a new substantive and procedural framework for reviewing all such claims and a formulation of the prejudice showing different from that followed by many courts, despite the limited discussion of the Lozada framework in the briefs submitted in Compean by the parties and amici curiae. Compean further provided that this new administrative framework should apply “henceforth,” even though the decision acknowledged it might conflict with the Lozada-based approach taken by a number of Federal Courts of Appeals. See Compean, 24 I&N Dec. at 730 & n.8.
 
**2 For the reasons stated herein, I have determined that it is appropriate to reconsider the January 7, 2009 decision.
 
Establishing an appropriate framework for reviewing motions to reopen immigration proceedings based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel is a matter of great importance. I do not believe that the process used in Compean resulted in a thorough consideration of the issues involved, particularly for a decision that implemented a new, complex framework in place of a well-established and longstanding practice that had been reaffirmed by the Board in 2003 after careful consideration. The preferable administrative process for reforming the Lozada framework is one that affords all interested parties a full and fair opportunity to participate and ensures that the relevant facts and analysis are collected and evaluated.
 
Accordingly, I direct the Acting Director of the Executive Office for Immigration Review to initiate rulemaking procedures as soon as practicable to evaluate the Lozada framework and to determine what modifications should be proposed for public consideration. After soliciting information and public comment, through publication of a proposed rule in the Federal Register, from all interested persons on a revised framework for reviewing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel in immigration proceedings, the Department of Justice may, if appropriate, proceed with the publication of a final rule.
 
*3 In Compean, the introduction of a new procedural framework depended in part on Attorney General Mukasey’s conclusion that there is no constitutional right to effective assistance of counsel in removal proceedings. Because that conclusion is not necessary either to decide these cases under pre-Compean standards or to initiate a rulemaking process, this Order vacates Compean in its entirety. To ensure that there is an established framework in place pending the issuance of a final rule, the Board and Immigration Judges should apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed. The litigating positions of the Department of Justice will remain unaffected by this Order. Finally, prior to Compean, the Board itself had not resolved whether its discretion to reopen removal proceedings includes the power to consider claims of ineffective assistance of counsel based on conduct of counsel that occurred after a final order of removal had been entered. Given the absence of a pre-Compean standard of the Board to apply pending issuance of a final rule, I resolve the question in the interim by concluding that the Board does have this discretion, and I leave it to the Board to determine the scope of such discretion.
 
**3 Turning to the merits of the particular cases at issue, I find that, for the reasons stated by the Board, its orders denying reopening of the three matters reviewed in Compean were appropriate under the Lozada framework and standards as established by the Board before Compean. On that basis, I concur with Attorney General Mukasey’s decision to affirm the Board’s decisions denying reopening of these matters. Compean, 24 I&N Dec. at 743.

2009 WL 1800031 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARGARITA MAXIMTCHOUK A.K.A. MARGARITA MOSHKOVICH
File: AXXX XX0 386 - Newark, NJ
June 1, 2009
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dmitry Paniotto, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; adjustment of status
 
In a decision dated February 25, 2008, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen her deportation proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under former section 242B(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c). The respondent has filed a timely appeal from that decision. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent entered the United States without inspection on or about October 25, 1994, at or near Champlain, New York. The respondent subsequently was placed into deportation proceedings. On April 27, 1995, the respondent submitted a change of address form to the immigration court, indicating that her address had changed from Fort Lee, New Jersey to Los Angeles, California (Exh. 4). On May 03, 1995, the respondent was sent a hearing notice, via certified mail at her most recent address, informing her of her upcoming deportation hearing at the Newark, New Jersey immigration court on June 20, 1995 (Exh. 5). The return receipt shows the respondent’s signature (Exh. 5). On May 04, 1995, the Immigration Judge issued a decision denying the respondent’s motion to change venue from Newark to Los Angeles, sent to the respondent’s most recent address (Exh. 7A). This decision again informed the respondent of her upcoming hearing in Newark, New Jersey on June 20, 1995 (Exh. 7A). A person purporting to be the respondent’s attorney then submitted a second motion to change venue, received by the immigration court on June 09, 1995 (Exh. 6). The Immigration Judge also denied this motion in an order dated June 13, 1995 (Exh. 7). In her order, the Immigration Judge observed that the person purporting to be the respondent’s attorney had not filed a form EOIR-28 with the immigration court, such that she was not considered to be the respondent’s attorney of record. Nevertheless, the Immigration Judge sent a copy of the denial of the motion both to the respondent at her most recent address of record and to the person purporting to be her attorney “as a courtesy only” (Exh. 7). This order again reminded the respondent for a third time of her obligation to appear for her hearing scheduled on June 20, 1995 (Exh. 7).
 
The respondent failed to appear at her scheduled deportation hearing on June 20, 1995, and was ordered deported in absentia in an order signed on June 23, 1995 (I.J. Dec. dated 6/23/95).
 
Some 13 years later, on February 01, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order in order to adjust status based on an approved I-130 visa petition filed by her lawful permanent resident husband. At that time, the respondent argued that she never received notice of the June 20, 1995, deportation hearing, claiming that her former counsel failed to submit a change of address form for the respondent.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion in a decision dated February 25, 2008.
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to make a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in this case. To the extent that the respondent is asserting that her failure to appear for his deportation hearing was due to exceptional circumstances based on the ineffective assistance of her former counsel, such a claim is precluded as untimely. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (holding that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(A), on the basis of exceptional circumstances). We recognize that the Third Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has indicated that an allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel might provide a basis for equitably tolling the relevant filing deadlines where the respondent has exercised due diligence in discovering the ineffective assistance of counsel and filing the motion to reopen. Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248 (3d Cir. 2005). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s failure to file her motion for some 13 years after the in absentia order was entered shows a lack of the requisite due diligence (I.J. at 3).
 
Moreover, we need not address the merits of the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim because we agree with the Immigration Judge that proper notice of the June 20, 1995, hearing was provided to the respondent, such that there is no evidence of any ineffective assistance of counsel. The record reflects that a copy of the respondent’s hearing notice was mailed to the respondent via certified mail, return receipt requested, at her correct address (Exhs. 5, 7, 7A). See section 242B(c)(l) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(1)). Such notice is sufficient to establish by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that the alien received written notice of the deportation hearing within the meaning of section 242B(c)(1) of the Act. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Moreover, while the respondent argues that her prior counsel failed to file a change of address form for the respondent, the record is clear that this form was properly filed (Exh. 4), and that subsequent correspondence from the immigration court, including the respondent’s hearing notice (Exh. 5) and both denials of the motions to change venue (Exhs. 7, 7A), were sent to the respondent at this new address. Accordingly, we do not find that the respondent was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 To the extent that she seeks sua sponte reopening, we find no basis to reverse the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not demonstrated any exceptional circumstances that would warrant the exercise of the limited discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373, 380 n.9 (A.G. 2002); Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While the respondent’s motion to reopen is not entirely clear, it appears that the respondent is claiming that she was first represented by a notary public, then by an attorney at the time of her prior proceedings, neither of whom submitted a change of address form. We will assume, without deciding, that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel can lie against a non-attorney in the Third Circuit, such that our analysis applies to both persons. See Gunawan v. Gonzales, 132 Fed.Appx. 997, 1000 n.4 (9th Cir. 2005) (unpublished decision) (indicating skepticism that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel could lie against “a non-attorney who never purported to be an attorney”).

2009 WL 1799983 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARVIN B. PAREDES
File: AXXX XX3 377 - Los Angeles, CA
May 29, 2009
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Acosta, Jr., Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º -Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility; continuance
 
In a decision dated June 3, 2005, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States as an arriving alien convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude (“CIMT”). Section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) (2000). The respondent filed a timely appeal to this Board in which he argued that he had been deprived of his due process right to a full and fair hearing because of the ineffective assistance of his former attorney. On September 11, 2006, we issued a decision dismissing the appeal based on our determination that the respondent had failed to demonstrate prejudice arising from his prior counsel’s allegedly deficient performance. The respondent thereafter filed a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has now remanded the matter to us for consideration of two intervening precedents, Camins v. Gonzales, 500 F.3d 872 (9th Cir. 2007), and Matter of Abosi, 24 I&N Dec. 204 (BIA 2007). For the reasons that follow, we conclude that neither of the aforementioned decisions is applicable to the respondent’s case, and therefore we will once again dismiss his appeal.
 
Section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13)(C)(v), provides that a lawful permanent resident (“LPR”) of the United States is to be treated as an applicant for admission (or “arriving alien”) if he or she “has committed an offense identified in section 212(a)(2), unless since such offense the alien has been granted relief under section 212(h) or 240A(a).” This provision, which became effective on April 1, 1997, abrogated the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Rosenberg v. Fleuti, 374 U.S. 449 (1963), which held that LPRs who had been convicted of crimes in the United States could make “innocent, casual, and brief overseas trips without being classified as seeking “entry” upon return and thus being exposed to a charge of inadmissibility. In Camins v. Gonzales, supra, the Ninth Circuit held that section 101 (a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act could not be applied retroactively to LPRs who pled guilty to their crimes when the Fleuti doctrine was still in effect, because such aliens would have had a reasonable reliance-based expectation that they would continue to be able to make “innocent, casual, and brief departures from the United States without being subjected to the legal disadvantages associated with treatment as an arriving alien.
 
*2 On remand, we are called upon to determine whether Camins v. Gonzales, supra, is applicable to the respondent’s circumstances. We conclude that it is not. The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, entered the United States without being admitted or paroled in 1989. In December 1990 he was convicted of engaging in disorderly conduct by means of a lewd act in violation of section 647(a) of the California Penal Code. More than a decade later, on June 1, 2001, the respondent’s status was adjusted to that of an LPR, but on February 28, 2002, he sustained another criminal conviction, this time for petty theft in violation of section 484(a) of the California Penal Code. The respondent thereafter departed the United States and traveled abroad. On September 17, 2003, he arrived at the Los Angeles International Airport port of entry and presented himself for inspection as a returning LPR. Upon discovering the respondent’s convictions, however, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) initiated these removal proceedings, charging the respondent with inadmissibility as an arriving alien convicted of a CIMT.
 
As the foregoing facts make clear, the respondent can be treated as an arriving alien despite his LPR status because he departed the United States after committing petty theft, “an offense identified in section 212(a)(2)” for which no waiver was ever granted. Section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act. In Camins v. Gonzales, supra, the alien’s only “offense identified in section 212(a)(2)” was committed (and resulted in a plea and conviction) in January 1996, more than one year prior to the April 1, 1997, effective date of section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act. In contrast, the respondent was convicted of a CIMT in February 2002, nearly five years after section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) became effective. Thus, under the law in effect when the respondent entered his plea to petty theft in February 2002, he had no reasonable, reliance-based expectation that the Fleuti doctrine would be applied to him in the event he chose to travel abroad in the future.
 
We recognize that the offense underlying the respondent’s February 2002 conviction would have been a “petty offense” under section 212(a)(2)(A)(ii)(II) of the Act but for the fact that he was also convicted in 1990 of engaging in disorderly conduct by means of a lewd act, a CIMT in its own right. Yet Camins v. Gonzales, supra, is simply inapposite with respect to that earlier conviction, which was entered more than ten years before the respondent became an LPR. Indeed, when the respondent sustained his disorderly conduct conviction he had no lawful immigration status at all, and thus he could not at that time have had a reasonable, reliance-based expectation that the Fleuti doctrine would apply to him. Furthermore, even if the respondent had been an LPR when he pled guilty to disorderly conduct in 1990, by the time of his 2002 plea to petty theft he would have been on notice that the earlier crime could be used against him in the future to preclude application of the petty offense exception. Thus, we conclude that Camins v. Gonzales, supra, is inapplicable, and that the respondent is properly classified as an arriving alien despite his LPR status.
 
*3 Having determined that Camins v. Gonzales, supra, is inapposite, we must now decide whether the respondent’s case is affected by Matter of Abosi, supra, in which the Board held that a returning LPR who was charged with inadmissibility as an arriving alien could apply for a “direct” waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, and did not need to seek such a waiver in conjunction with an application for adjustment of status. While the respondent’s returning LPR status does place him in the class of aliens who can apply for the direct form of section 212(h) relief, Matter of Abosi, supra, is inapplicable because the respondent’s ineligibility for section 212(h) relief arises from independent grounds.
 
Section 212(h) provides that “¿nºo waiver shall be granted under this subsection in the case of an alien who has previously been admitted to the United States as an alien lawfully admitted for permanent residence if … the alien has not lawfully resided continuously in the United States for a period of not less than 7 years immediately preceding the date of initiation of proceedings to remove the alien from the United States.” In other words, section 212(h) relief is—as an LPR or otherwise—throughout the 7-year period immediately preceding the institution of proceedings. Matter of Rotimi, 24 I&N Dec. 567 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent’s removal proceedings were initiated on November 21, 2003, when the Notice to Appear was filed in Immigration Court. See 8 C.F.R. § 239.1(a) (2003). Thus, the respondent must come forward with evidence to establish that he “lawfully resided” in the United States for a continuous period beginning not later than November 21, 1996. No such evidence has been proffered. On the contrary, the only evidence of lawful residence provided by the respondent pertains to his adjustment to LPR status in June 2001, a mere 2 1/2 years before these proceedings were initiated. Accordingly, based on the record as currently constituted, there is no evidence that the respondent qualifies for section 212(h) relief, whether or not he seeks it in conjunction with adjustment of status. Matter of Abosi is therefore inapposite, and provides no basis for further proceedings.
 
In conclusion, neither Camins v. Gonzales, supra, nor Matter of Abosi, supra, has any discernible effect on the respondent’s removability or eligibility for relief from removal. Moreover, no issue has been raised as to the propriety of our earlier decision in this matter, which pertained solely to ineffective assistance of counsel, the only issue raised in the respondent’s appeal. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
*4 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2009 WL 1799983 (BIA)
End of Document

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven Goldstein, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maria T. Armas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security’s request that we consider its late response to the motion is denied. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. In our last decision, we noted that, “to the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of his former counsel, he has failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).” At the time the instant motion was filed, the respondent was aware that the Attorney General was expected to issue a decision regarding the requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. The respondent’s motion was filed in the hope that the Attorney General would issue a decision with less stringent guidelines than those established in Matter of Lozada. However, in Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General ruled that the procedural requirements established in Matter of Lozada would continue to apply to ineffective assistance claims, such as the respondent’s, made prior to January 7, 2009, and set forth more stringent requirements for deficient performance of counsel claims made thereafter. The Attorney General’s decision in Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C- therefore does not effect our rejection of his ineffective assistance claim for failure to comply with the Lozada requirements. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Juan P. Osuna

Lucia Garrett, Esquire

ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Ghana. On March 15, 2007, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen, which the Immigration Judge denied. The respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. On December 11, 2007, the Board dismissed the appeal. On November 26, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. She contends that former counsel told her not to appear for her hearing because he was seeking a change of venue, and that such advise was ineffective because the motion was not granted and she was ordered removed in absentia through no fault of her own. Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996).
 
On January 7, 2009, the United States Attorney General issued a decision announcing a new standard for claims involving what was formerly known as) ineffective assistance of counsel. In Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General announced several new substantive standards for evaluating such claims, now known as “deficient performance of counsel” claims, that apply regardless of whenever a motion to reopen that raises such claims has been filed, as well as new evidentiary standards that are not implicated here because they only apply to a motion to reopen filed after January 7, 2009. See 24 I&N Dec. at 741. Consistent with that decision, the respondent’s claims are designated “deficient performance of counsel” and will be analyzed in a manner that is consistent with the holdings in that decision. See id. at 730 n.8 and 741.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Compean, supra, as well as the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the motion to reopen shall be granted, the Board’s decision issued December 11, 2007, as well as the Immigration Judge’s decisions, are vacated, and the record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
